THE BED PAGE.
In a notice of Stephen Phillips’s ‘ Christ in
Hades, and Other Poems,’on Nov. 6, ’97, it was
here suggested that his blank-verse method did
not compare too favorably with the standards.
But the new book of ‘ Poems ’ fairly wins us away
from the standards. By the side of Phillips’s
music Milton’s majesty is stiff—and Keats himself
was never tenderer, sweeter. Etiglish critics eagerly
hail the birth of a new Great Poet; and it looks as-
if English critics were right. Certainly nothing
so large and exalted as ‘ Marpessa ’ has been done
for years (the only comparable recent performance,
Symons’s ‘ Mundi Yietima,’ suffers—despite just
extolment— the penalty of a too-personal appeal
in failing to rise to imagination’s topmost sether).
And, by sis appreciation or non-appreciation of
‘Marpessa ’ anyone may certainly judge whether
se responds or not to the best in poetry. The
myth says that Marpessa, being given by Zeus her
choice between the god Apollo and Idas a mortal,
chose Idas. And Phillips illustrates the contest
and the choice. There is a preliminary passage—
Wounded with beauty in the summer night
Young Idas tossed upon his couch, aud cried
“ Marpessa, O Marpessa! ’’ From the dark
The floating smell of flowers invisible,
The mystic yearning of the garden wet,
The moonless-passing night—into his brain
Wandered, until he ro'se and outward leaned
In the dim summer: Twas the moment deep
When we are conscious of the secret dawn,
Amid the Asraness that we feel is greem
To Idas hau Marpessa been revealed,
Roaming with morning thoughts amid the dew,
All fresh from sleeping ; and upon her cheek
The bloom of pure repose ; like perfect fruit
Even at. the moment was her beauty ripe.
The god Apollo from the heaven of heavens
Her mortal sweetness through the air allured ;
And on this very noon she shall decide
’Twixt Idas and the god, take to herself
A brief or an eternal lover. So
When the long day that glide! h without cloud,
The summer day, was at h o- blue deep hour
Of lilies musical with busy bliss,
When very light trembled as with excess,
" And heat was frail, ap'd every bush.and flower
Was drooping in the glory overcome ;
They three together met; on the one side, .
Fresh from diffusing light on all the world
Apollo ; on the other w ithout sleep
Idas, and in the midst Marpessa stood.
Just as a flower after drenching rain,
So from the falling of felicity
Her human beauty glowed, and it was -new;
The bee too near her bosom drowsed and dropped.
But as the god sprang to embrace her, they
Heard thunder, and a little afterward
The far Paternal voioe, “ Let her decide.”
Then Apollo bends to make his plea—
And as a-flame blown backward by a gust,
Burned to and fro in fury beautiful
The murmuring god ; but at the last he spoke,
Ahdstpiled as on his favorite western isle.
“ Marpessa, though no trouble, nor any pain,
So is it willed, can touch me ; but I live
For ever in a deep deliberate bliss,
A spirit sliding through tranquillity ;
Yet when I saw thee I imagined woe.
That thou who art so fair, shouldst ever taste
Of the earth-sorrow: for thy* life has been
The history of a flower in the air,
Liable but to breezes and to time,
As rich and purposeless as is the rose:
Thy simple doom is to be beautiful.
Thee God created but to grow, not strive,
And not to suffer, merely to be sweet,
The favorite of his rains ; and thou indeed
Lately upon the summer wast disclosed.
'Child, wilt thou taste of grief? On thee the hours
Shall feed, and bring thy soul into the dusk :
' Even now thy face is hasting to the dark !
For slowly shalt thou cool to all things great,
And wisely smile at love ; and thou shalt sea
Beautiful Faith surrendering to Time,
The fierce ingratitude of children loved,
Ah, sling of stings ! A mourner shalt thou stand
At Passion’s funeral in decent garb.
The greenly silent and cool-growing night.
Shall be the time when most thou art awake,
With dreary eyes of all illusion cured,
Beside that stranger that thy husband is.
But if thou’lt live with me, then shalt thou bide
In mere (elioity above the world,
In peace alive and moving, where to stir
Is ecstacy, and thrilling is repose.
What is the love of men that women seek it?
In its beginning pale with cruelty,
But having sipped df beauty, negligent,
And full of languor and distaste: for they
Seeking that perfect face beyond the world
Approach in vision earthly semblances,
And touch, and at the shadow's flee away.
Then wilt thou die ? Part with eternal thoughts,
Lie without any hope beneath the grass,
All thy imaginations in the dust ?
And all that tint and melody and breath,
Which in their lovely unison are thou,
To be dispersed upon the whirling sands!
Thy soul blown seaward on nocturnal blast 1
O brief and breathing creature, wilt thou cease
Once having been ? Thy doom doth make thee rich,
And the low grave doth make thee exquisite.
But if thou’lt live with me, then will I kiss
Warm immortality into thy lips ;
And I will carry thee ahove the world,
To share my ecstacy of flinging beams,
And scattering without intermission joy.
And thou shalt know that first leap of the sea
Toward me ; the grateful upward look of earth,
Emerging roseate .from her bath of dew, —
We two in heaven dancing,—Babylon.
Shall flash and murmur, and cry from under us,
And Nineveh catch fire, and at our feet
Be hurled with her inhabitants, and all
Adoring Asia kindle and hugely bloom ;
We two in heaven running,—continents
Shall lighten, ocean unto ocean flash
And rapidly laugh till all this world is warm.
Or since thou art a woman, thou shalt have
More tender tasks ; to steal upon the sea,
A long e ) eted bliss to tossing men.
Or build upon the evening sky some wished
And glorious metropolis of cloud.
Thou shalt persuade the harvest and bring on
The deeper green ; or silently attend
The fiery funeral of foliage old,
Connive with Time serene and the good hours.
Or, —for I know thy heart, —a dearer toil, —
To lure into the air a face long sick,
To gild the brow that from its dead looks up,
To shine on the unforgiven of this world ;
With slow sweet surgery restore the brain,
And to dispel shadows and shadowy feat **
+
[lt was formerly said of Phillips that there is a
Suggestion of physical weakness—lack of vitality
—through his work : his phrases flicker where
they ought to blaze. That is still so : P. fails to
character Apollo : here would Milton sweep and
burn. But P. preserves always the same soft,
half-melancholy touch, soothing and sweet and
sometimes unsuitable. But Idas ! —never any
lover has told his love more thrillingly ! How
phrase and cadence wed fine thought in this beau-
tiful apostrophe !]
When he had spoken, humbly Idas said :
After such argument what oan I plead ?
Or what pale promise make ? Yet since it is
In women to pity rather than to aspire,
A little I will speak. I love thee then
Not only for thy body packed with sweet
Of all this world, that cup of brimming June,
That jar of violet wine set in the air,
That palest rose sweet in the night of life;
Nor for that stirring bosom all besieged
By drowsing lovers, or thy perilous hair ;
Nor for that face that might indeed provoke
Invasion of old oities ; no, nor all
Thy freshness stealing on me like strange sleep.
Not for this only do I love thee, but
Because Infinity upon thee broods ;
And thou art full of whispers and of shadows.
Thou meanest what the sea has striven to say
So long, and yearndd up the clifls to tell;
Thou art what all the winds have uttered not,
What the still night suggesteth to the heart.
Thy voioe is like to music heard ere birth,
Some spirit lute touched on a spirit sea ;
Thy face remembered is from other worlds,
It has been died for, though I know not when.
It has been sung of, though I know not whera.
It has the strangeness of the luring West,
And of sad sea-horizons ; beside thee
I am aware of other times and lands,
Of birth far-back, of lives in many stars.
O beauty lone and like a candle clear
In this dark country of the world ! Thou art
My woe, my early light, my music dying.”
Then Marpessa, in a courteous re-statement of
the god’s argument—
As he was speaking, she with lips apart
Breathed, and with dimmer eyes leaned through theair
As one in dream, and now his human hand
Took in her own ; and to Apollo spoke:
“ O gradual rose of the dim universe !
Whose warmth steals through the grave unto the dead,
Soul of the early S’-y, the priest of bloom !
Who beautifully goest in the West,
Attracting as to an eternal home
The yearning soul. Male of the female earth 1
O eager bridegroom springing in this world
As in thy bed prepared! Fain would I know
Yon heavenly wafting through the heaven wide,
And the large view of the subjected seas,
And famous cities, and the various toil -
Of men : all Asia at my feet spread out
In indolent magnificence of bloom !
Africa in her matted hair obscured,
And India in meditation plunged !
Then the delight of flinging the sunbeams,
Diffusing silent bliss ; and yet more sweet,-
To oherish fruit on the warm wall; to raise
Out of the tomb to glory the pale wheat,
Serene ascension by the rain prepared ;
To work with the benignly falling hours,
And beautiful slow Time. But„dearest, this,
To gild the face-that from its dead loots up,
To shine on the rejected, and arrive
, To women that remember in the night ;
Or mend with sweetest surgery the mind.
But Marpessa is human —
And yet, forgive me if I can but speak
Most human words. Of immortality
Thou singest; thou would'sthold me from the ground,
And this just opening beauty from the grave.
As yet I have known no sorrow ; all my days
Like perfect lilies under water stir,
And God has sheltered me from His own wind ;
The darling of his breezes have I been.
Yet as to one inland, that dreameth ione,
Sea-faring men with their sea-weary eyes,
Round the inn-fire tell of some foreign land;
So aggd men, much tossed about in life, -
Have told me of that country, Sorrow far.
How many goodly ships at anchor lie
Within her ports ; even to me indeed
Hath a sea-rumor through the night been borne.
And I myself remember, and have heard,
Of men that did believe, women that loved,
That were unhappy long and now are dead,
With wounds that no eternity can close,
Life had so marked them : or of others who
Panted toward their end, and fell on death
Even as S'diUing runners breast the rope.
And most 1 remember of all human things
My mother ; often as a child I pressed
My face against her cheek, and felt her tears-;
Even as she smiled on me, her eyes would fill,
Until my own grew ignorantly wet ;
And I in silence wondered at sorrow.
When I remember this, how shall I know
That I myself may not, by sorrow taught.
Accept the perfect stillness of the ground?
Where, though I lie still, and stir not at all,
Yet shall I irresistibly be kind,
Helplessly sweet, a wandering garden bliss.
My ashes shall console and make for peace ;
This mind that injured, be an aimless balm.
[lt is for a singular fineness of vision and dis-
tinction of statement that Phillips must have
credit : intrinsically, his thought is not impres-
sively original : it wins originality through his
eyes and utterance. Around Marpessa ap-
parently float many echoes—the beautiful ‘wander-
ing garden bliss,’for example, surely embodies a
memory of- Eitzgerald’s ‘Omar’?] Marpessa
continues—
Or if there be some other world, with no
Bloom, neither rippling sound, nor early smell,
Nor leaves, nor pleasant exchange of human speeoh ;
Only a dreadful pacing to and fro
Of spirits meditating on the sun ;
A land of bardd boughs and grieving wind;
Yet would I not forego the doom, the place,
- Whither my poets and my heroes went
Before me ; warriors that with deeds forlorn
Saddened my youth, yet made it great to live.;
Lonely antagonists of Destiny,
That went down scornful before many spears,
Who soon as we are born, are straight our friends ;
And live in simple music, oountry songs,
And mournful ballads by the winter fire.
Since they have died, their death is ever mine ;
I would not lose it. Then, thou speak’st of joy,
Of immortality without one sigh, ,
Existence without tears for evermore.
Thou would’st preserve me from the anguish, lest
This holy face into the dark return.
Yet I being human, human sorrow miss.
The half of music, I have heard men say,
Is to have grieved when comes the lonely wail
Over the mind; old men have told it me
Subdued after long life by simple sounds.
The mourner is the favorite of the moon,
And the departing sun hisQlory owes " :
To the eternal thoughts of creatures brief, „
Who think the thing that they shall never see.
Since we must die, how bright the starry track 1
How wonderful in a bereaved ear
The Northern wind ; how strange the summer night,
The exhaling earth to those who vainly love.
Out of our sadness have we made this world
So beautiful; the sea sighs in our brain,
And in our heart that yearning of the moon.
To all this sorrow was I born, and since
Out of a human womb I came, I am
Not eager to forego it; I would scorn
To elude the heaviness and take the joy,
For pain came with the sap, pangs with the bloom:
This is the sting, the wonder.
[lt is the limpid ease of this that fixes.its charm.
The words seem the natural overflow of a brain
flooded with beauty,—“ Yet should I,” Marpessa
goes on—]
Yet should I
Linger beside thee in felicity,
Sliding with open eyes through liquid bliss
For ever ; still I must grow old. Ah I
Should ail beside thee Apollo, and should note
With eyes that would not be, but yet are dim,
Ever so slight a change from day to day
In chee my husband ; watch thee nudge thyself
To little offices that once were sweet;
Slow where thou once wert swift, remembering
To kiss those lips which once thou could’st not leave.
I should expect thee by the Western bay,
Faded, not sure of thee, with desperate smiles,
And pitiful devices of my dress
Or fashion of my hair: thou wouldst grow kind;
Most bitter to a woman that was loved.
I must ensnare thee to iny arms, and touch
Thy pity, to but hold thee to my heart.
But if I live with Idas, then we two
On the low earth shall prosper hand in hand
In odours of the open field, and live
In peaceful noises of the farm, and watch
The pastoral fields burned by the setting snn.
And he shall give me passionate children, not
Some radiant god that will despise me quite,
But clambering limbs and little hearts that err.
And I shall sleep beside him in the night,
And fearful from some dream shall touch his hand
Secure ; or at some festival we two
Will wander through the lighted city streets;
And in the crowd I’ll take his arm and feel
Him oloser for the press. So shall we live.
And though the first sweet sting of love be past,
The sweet that almost venom is ; though youth,
With tender and extravagant delight,
The first and secret kiss by twilight hedge,
The insane farewell repeated o’er and o’er.
Pass off; there shall succeed a faithful peace ;
Beautiful friendship tried by sun and wind,
Durable from the daily dust of life.
And though with sadder, still with kinder eyes,
We shall behold all frailties, we shall haste
To pardon, and with mellowing minds to bless.
Then though we must grow old, we shall grow old
Together, and he shall not greatly miss
My bloom faded, and waning light of eyes,
Too deeply gazed in ever to seem dim ;
Nor shall we murmur at, nor much regret
The years that gently bend us to the ground,
And gradually incline our face ; that we
Leisurely stooping, and with each slow step,
May curiously inspect our lasting fiome.
But we shall sit with luminous holy smiles
Endeared by many griefs, by many a jest, .
And custom sweet of living side by side ;
And full of mem tries not unkindly glance
Upon each other. Last, we shall desoend
Into the natural ground—not without tears—
One must go first, ah god ! one must go first;
After so long one blow for both were good ;
Still like old friends, glad to have met, and leave
Behind a wholesome memory on the earth.
And thou, beautiful god, in that far time,
When in'thy setting sweet thou gazest down
On this grey head, wilt thou remember then
That once I pleased thee, that I once was young?”
And the closing lines —
When she had spoken, Idas with one cry
Held her, and there was silence ; while the god
In anger disappeared. Then slowly they,
He looking downward, and she gazing up,
Into the evening green wandered away.
A poem not indeed flawless, bub singularly
harmonious, sustained, and sincere. And since
Phillips is but young—26 or so—he is likely to
better this performance before the bloom fades
from his imagination. Even as his book stands,
he is decisively lifted ahove the heads of minor
poets, if not yet decisively fixed among the ranks
of major.
Besides “Marpessa,” which is perhaps its chief
adornment, Phillips’s “Poems” contain “Christ in
Hades,” already tasted; and the others from the
shilling volume. The new verses are all more or
less marked by P.’s peculiar charm —“ The Wife ”
maybe most memorable. Quotation can end with
some lines enforcing the lately-discussed dogma
that ‘ contrast is essential to consciousness ’ —
I in the greyness rose ;
I could not sleep for thinking of one dead.
Then to the chest I went,
Where lie the things of my beloved spread.
Quietly these I took ;
A little glove, a sheet of music torn,
Paintings, ill-done, perhaps ;
Then lifted up a dress that she had worn,
And now I came to where
Her letters are ; they lie beneath the rest;
And read them in the haze:
She spoke of many things, was sore opprest.
But these things moved me not;
Not when she spoke of being parted quite,
Or being misunderstood,
Or growing weary of the world’s great fight.
Not even when she wrote
Of our dead child, and the hand-writing swerved ;
Not even then I shook :
Not even by such words was I unnerved.
I thought, she is at-peace ;
Whither the child is gone, she too has passed.
And a much needed rest
Is fallen upon her, she is still at last.
But when at length I took
From under all those letters one small sheet.
Folded and writ in haste ;
Why did my heart with sudden sharpness beat ?
Alas, it was not sad!
Her saddest words I had read calmly o’er.
Alas, it had no pain !
Her painful words, all these I knew before.
A hurried happy line !
A little jest, 100 slight for one so dead:
This did I not endure :
Then with a shuddering heart no more I read.

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"Maybe Fate's weightcloths are breaking Ms heart."
—Rudyaud Kipling.
Life’s race for all is even-lapped
To watching eyes it seems,
But how we may be handicapped
The wide world never dreams.
Ah ! well for those whose lot is cast
Where open war demands ;
But brave men fighting down the Past
Are fighting with chained hands.
The girls who loved us long ago,
With love long changed to scorn,
Will, watching, think, “How weak and slow ! ”
But never “ How forlorn ! ”
Yet by the bitterness of Fate
The night we chose to part
It was their soft hands laid the weight
Above our throbbing heart.
The memory of dark deeds done
That blot a family page,
The father leaving to his son
Sin’s ghastly heritage,
The treason of a friend, untrue,
The wild dreams better dead,
The hopes ’tis madness to renew—
These are our weights of lead.
Life’s race for all is even-lapped
To watching eyes it seems,
But how we may be handicapped
The wide world never dreams ;
Remember, when you cheer the best,
It was no equal start,
And some who toil behind the rest
Have leaden weights at heart!
WILL H. OGILVIE.
GOOD-BYE.
There are times when the mile-laps seem weary,
And minutes are ages too long,
When you feel the dull stars are but dreary
Night-lights on a wide sea of Wrong ;
When the fetters of custom are galling
And snap ’em you can’t —how you try !
For you haven’t a bean to be calling
Your own; and you must say good-bye I
So you go, with a grin and a simper,
To see her away per express,
And there isn’t a brake-tube looks limper
Than you—in your sprucest of dress ;
And you know that your still heart but wails for
The shock of the first piston-jar,
While the steam-pipes get bubbling and sobbing
“ Far-far-away—ever so far ! ”
Oh ! it’s then that the memories gather
And crush you, and jostle and crowd !
Oh, it’s then that the past pleasures wrap you
Around with a sombre-hued cloud;
Delights that may come again never,
Warm nights ’neath the old trysting-tree,
Long walks and long talks by the river,
And silences sweet by the sea.
And it’s then that the scenes you have painted—
Bright visions of Home and a Wife—
Spread over the whole of the canvas
And blot out the rest of your life ;
Oh ! it’s then with all Hell you could wrestle
For her—and be happy to die !
But—you haven’t a bean to be calling
Your own ; so you—just—say Good-bye !
Perth, W.A.
L.M.D.
A DREAM OF THUMBS.
I dreamt a dream of thumbs,
And every thumb was down /
Consider 8., that potentate
Whose lightest word is law
To all who at his bidding wait
And bow to him w th awe !
“Supreme, at least,” I thought, “is he,”
Until it struck me dumb
To learn by chance one day that B.
Was under H.’s thumb.
“The greatest, then, is H.,” thought I,
But more was yet to come,
For someone told me then that Y.
Had H. beneath his thumb !
On this I meditated when
A light began to shine,
And—talk of thumbs ! it struck me then
That Y. was under mine !
But swiftly at the thought I get
A view of Damocles :
Ihe thumb again ! A hanging debt,
And I am under C’s !
And so I traced the sequence through,
And felt my brain grow numb ;
Lo ! ev’rywhere the pinching shoe,
And someone else’s thumb !
Till doubt has reached the farthest gate —
Does God Himself succumb ?
And through the aeons’ march has Fate
Got Him beneath its thumb?
I dreamt a dream of thumbs,
And ev'ry thumb was doivn !
Sydney, Feb., 1898.
BERNARD ESPINASSE.
Dear Bulletin. —Be recent contradictory
assertions that people manage to control their
passion for washing with amazing success, and
about the extent to which the bathing debauchery
prevails, the question arises: What place, after
all, does the bath take in the history of the world ?
Do you suppose that Adam and Eve wasted much
time bathing after they had adopted clothes and
thus reduced the area that required washing ? At
the time of the flood Noah had great facilities for
washing, but it is doubtful if he embraced them.
The rest of humanity was certainly well washed,
but the results were discouraging. Did Solomon
in all his glory have a daily bath ? Did the
Apostles bathe every day ? I should smile !
Doubtful whether Bacon, or Shakespeare, or
Milton, or Burns had a good wash-down once a
year. The fact is, the human race worships clean-
liness much too sincerely ever to become very
familiar with it. Washing is an aesthetic luxury
indulged in by a few fastidious fin-de-sttcle dudes
and degenerates. It isn’t essential to virtue,
wisdom, strength, wealth, or comfort. —Dirty
Dick.

SATURDAY, APRIL 16, 1898.
Pbice, 6d.
" A member of one of the volunteer regiments,
who has left the samp, called at the Sunday Times
Office yesterday to complain ‘of the execrable man-
ner in which the provisions had been provided to
the men.’ In each tent, he said, there are nine men,
and on Friday the steak supplied would not have
been sufficient for two ; while only two
pots of potatoes of about 201 b. each were
provided for 250 men. The water supply was
also unsatisfactory, and the potatoes which
tbe men received were, in consequence, but half-
cooked. Upon such food the men were expected to
go out on a four or five hours’ march. The third
and fourth sections of the Scottish Rifles, he de-
clared, refused for some time to move, but were
finally persuaded on the promise of something more
substantial on their return, with the exception of
three or four, and they were instantly dismissed. The
men, the complainant also added, not being able to get
anything to eat or drink, sought relief at the bar,
and some imbibed rather freely. About 50 of the
Irish Rifles, dissatisfied with their treatment, left
the camp in a body, and out of- the remainder enough
men to form a guard were unobtainable. In conclu-
sion, he added that in his opinion it was ‘ the biggest
blow the volunteer movement has had.’ ”

SATURDAY, APRIL 16, 1898.
More About tie Devil and the Deep Sea.
The old familiar war-cloud came nearer to
bursting within the last few weeks than it
usually does, and though it now wears a
somewhat alleviated aspect, it is a cloud that
is always with us. Presumably it will burst
very badly some day ; everything in this
world bursts some day. Australia has grown
much more deeply interested in that cloud
within the last few years than it ever w*as
before. Circumstances have been changing
very fast, and the alterations of the map of
the world have gone on so rapidly that
geographers have hardly been able to keep
pace with them ; and almost every change
has brought the row and racket of European
politics closer to our doors. Up till a very
few years ago Australia was more absolutely
isolated from European complications than
any other place on earth—barring such lone-
some regions as Greenland and Tierra del
Fuego and Kerguelen and similar spots of
no account. Until very lately there were
no European powers other than Great Britain
on this side of Africa ; no French in Tonquin;
no Germans in Papuano Russians nearer
than Vladivostock ; Japan wasn’t, invented ;
and the harmless, necessary brown nigger
had the Pacific isles practically all to himself.
In those days Australia had no non-British
European neighbors at all except a few
slumberous old Dutch and Spanish settle-
ments, from which there was no reasonable
fear of aggression. It was then like China
before the Jap. deluge—a flowery land that
lay outside the regions wherein that little
geographical speck called Europe is always
making trouble.
• • * *
Now things have changed very greatly,
and the pleasant isolation of even 10 years
ago is altogether gone. One of the great
reasons why Australia should make haste to
federate arises out of this same change.
There is no other country in the world so
well worth grabbing _ that has so small a
population for its oWn defence. And even
that population is under six different govern-
ments, and six different military systems ;
and there are six different railway manage-
ments to mix up the mobilisation of what
troops there are, and six different plans for con-
ducting the campaign (w’hen it becomes neces-
sary toconductany campaign), and six generals
all hollering orders at one private, and six
dogs chasing the one sheep in six different
directions simultaneously, and six hens are
employed to lay the one egg—the natural
result being that what defence there is in Aus-
tralia is vastly less efficient than it should
be. The same men and money and military
pomp, and the same amount of yelling,
might supply Australia with a defence sys-
tem many times more efficient than the one
it possesses, if there was one little army under
one head, instead of six special battalions
under as many separate commands. Six
separate Napoleons, each equally boss,
and all hollering contradictory orders, would
have wrecked even the army which won
Areola and Lodi. The present situation of
affairs furnishes all kinds of reasons why
Australia should think over the situation
very hard. Five Powers have set themselves
of late to chew up the available earth. In
the course of a very few years they have
eaten up all Africa and all the thinly-peopled
regions of Central Asia. They are now
wading in gaily at China, and, when it is
done, provender will be running short. The
Spanish - American states are difficult of
access; they are dreadfully indigestible,
owing to the fighting qualities of their in-
habitants ; and they have the United States
as a sure ally, so they may probably be left
out of the calculation. This increasing
scarcity of victuals makes the situation very
different from what it used to be.
• * * *
No one knows exactly what the situation
is as regards Australia. This country de-
pends on Britain for its main defence, and
no one knows with any certainty what Britain
amounts to nowadays. That country hasn’t
fought a big war single-handed for over a
century ; its last really great effort was in
the Crimea, when the soldiers of Britain and
three other nations spent two years in taking
one Russian city—not a very large city either,
and one which had only been hurriedly forti-
fied at the last moment. Britain certainly
possesses the finest ironclad fleet on earth,
but no one knows for certain what ironclads
amount to, or what kind of luck they will
meet with in actual warfare. Anyhow,
Australia admits that it may have to defend
its own territory from invasion as best it
can by its own unaided efforts, and that in
some of the chances of that war which is cer-
tain to be raised some day by some person or
persons unknown, Britain may not be able
to defend it either by sea or land. It admits
the fact by keeping up six separate military
establishments which are rendered miserably
inefficient by divided control. And, having
thus admitted that there is a danger to be
met, it is obviously Australia’s business to
make its defences as efficient as possible by
federating its six alleged military establish-
ments—by having one decent army with one
recognised head, one system of mobilisation,
uniform weapons and clothes, Federal rail-
way control, so that the little force can be
shifted from place to place with the mini-
mum of delay, a Federal arsenal and ammu-
nition factory, and at least a vague idea
where it is proposed to go and how it is pro-
posed to get there.
• * * *
Because the Federal party in Australia
points to the possibility of invasion and the
consequent need for a solid and coherent
military system it is now described by the
provincial crowd (in New South Wales par-
ticularly) as an alarmist faction which wants
war as a means of obtaining Federation at
any price. Which is a hideously illogical
process of reasoning. If by chance invasion
did come to Australia, there are only two
cities (Sydney and Melbourne) which would
yield magnificent loot to an invader, and of
these Sydney is in most danger, inasmuch as
its lies nearer to our most adjacent enemies
in Asia, and is much more accessible from
the sea than the Victorian capital. If inva-
sion did happen this way, Sydney would
probably be the first scene of trouble, and if
invasion did hasten on Federation it would
probably be merely by making New South
Wales federate on any terms the other pro-
vinces proposed, for the sake of their assist-
ance. The national party urges Australia to
federate now, in time of peace, when the
matter can be considered in a calm, judicial
spirit; it is the small parochial crowd which
wants to leave it over, and let the matter be
hustled through in red-hot haste in some
future period of alarm.
* * * *
Moreover, the anti-Federal party is much
more distinctly a war-scare party than its
opponents. It has always kept up some
kind of military establishment. It is brist-
ling with awful amateur “majahs” like
Dibbs and Neild. It is perpetually making
an exhibition of itself in warlike trappings
on top of a gaunt horse with a view to doing
something dreadful to some enemy or other.
It imports gorgeous warriors from Britain,
and believes in the naval subsidy, and it is
the great war-scare faction par excellence.
The real difference between the two parties
in this respect is that the Provincial party
believes in having six little squads of
soldiers, so thaf there may be room for si”
separate sets of officers in gaudy rags to
shout contradictory orders when the neces-
sity arises. And the Federal party, with far
less millinery on, suggests having the force
consolidated, and one set of officers to shout
just one set of orders. And, as no army can
start out efficiently in six directions at once,
any more than one sheep can run six differ-
ent ways simultaneously because it has six
dogs after it, there is a great deal to be said
for the Federal view of the case.
* * * *
The Bulletin doesn’t believe in the
war-scare policy—the kind which leads un-
warlike accountants and bankers such as
Neild and Dibbs to devise strange draperies
after the manner of Solomon in a fit, and.
prance at the head of battalions dressed like
themselves. It doesn’t believe either in the
kind of military fervor which requires such
an awful amount of liquor to drink the
Queen s health in. It desires to leave the
old stories of Crecy and Waterloo out of the
case, as being totally irrelevant. The real
issue is : Australia is liable to attack just
like any other place. That fact is admitted
by the provincialists themselves—the main-
tenance of six scraggy military forces in the
country is sufficient proof of the fact. If
Australia was one State, instead of six, it
would be possible to concentrate all these
forces where they may happen to be wanted,
and there are so very few of them that if
they are wanted at all the chances are they
will all be wanted very badly indeed. It
would save the cost of five commanders, and
live staffs, and five complete sets of
arrangements ; and a lot more private
could be bought with the money. It
would prevent the possibility of one province
doing a scrambling fight with its own little
force, and running out of ammunition,
while the other five States kept their
soldiers and ammunition at home lest they
should be attacked later on. It would
promote uniformity, whereas the most not-
able blessing of the provincial system is that,
if one province did lend cartridges to another
the other province couldn’t get them into its
gun. It would create some kind of Federal
control over the railways, therefore if S.A.
wanted to send men to assist New South
Wales, the Yic. railway department couldn’t
refuse them means of conveyance on the
ground that it wanted all its rolling-stock to
carry the local Fatman of Melbourne and his
goods to a place of safety, and tell them to
go overland by camel from Broken Hill. And
it would make all the provinces share the
cost of the trouble, which is something—
especially to New South Wales, which lies
rather near the most probable source of
trouble, and has valuable coal supplies on its
coast to attract a hostile fleet. If the anti-
Federal party is of opinion that these con-
siderations are of the war-scare order, its
own obvious and logical course is to disband
the local forces, and sack Dibbs and Neild
and the other giddy amateur warriors, and
go out of the war-scare business on its own
account.
A Labor-Politician.
On Wednesday last week, N.S.W. politician
Hughes, M.L.A., appeared in public to define
the attitude of the local Labor-party towards
Federation. At least, it is generally assumed
that lie was defining the attitude of the party in
general, and it was a rather lamentable atti-
tude. The N.S.W. Labor-party, in the main, is
against the present Federation movement. It
is allied, in the interests of provincialism, with
sueb. dismal Tories as Lyne, Dibbs, Jack
Want, the bulk of the heavy-going fossils in
the Legislative Council, the ghost of old John
Robertson, the Australian Star, the Cab-
bage-tree Mob in general, Heydon, George
Pile, and the like. Most people expected
better things of the Labor-party, but it has
failed most lamentably to live up to its reputa-
tion. And the reasons given by Hughes,
M.L.A. (speaking, apparently, on behalf of the
party), for this utter failure to rise to its great
opportunity, were mostly dreadful samples of
gross ignorance, or premeditated misrepresenta-
tion, or both. If Hughes is an authentic pro-
phet, and he correctly defined the attitude of
the other “ Solidarity” men, then the Solidarity
crowd deserves to have something serious
happen to it at next election, and is probably in
a fair way to get it.
* * * «
Hughes, M.L.A., speaking presumably on
behalf of his party, urged the people of N.S. W.
to reject the Convention Bill, in the first place,
because of the utterly unjust measure of power
which it gave the Federal Senate. The follow-
ing is extracted from the Daily Telegraph’s
lengthy and sweetly-approving report:—
The Lower House was subject to dissolution at any
time, while the Upper House, except on one condition,
was secure from dissolution. The Upper House must neces-
sarily be the stronger, because it was not subject to
dissolution, or the fears of dissolution. He could remem-
ber Mr, Lucas, in the Upper House, secure from all the
chances of political adversity, getting up and telling the
Assembly to go to the country. (Laughter.) He hadn’t
to go himself. He took no risk. Although one was given
to understand that in this Federation only the "most
intellectual men would be returned, still even these would
be swayed by just the same kind of motives as the man
in the street. The Upper House was not subject to disso-
lution until the Lower House came back from an appeal
to the country. Then, if they failed to agree, both
Houses went. But if the Lower House came back
strongly the Senate might knuckle down and avoid going
to the country at all. In an3' case, there never need bo
any danger to itself. ... The Senate could only be
dissolved on one condition—that was after the Lower
House had been to the country, and the two still failed to
agree.
I he Bulletin doesn’t pretend to know for cer-
tain whether this is sheer ignorance or whether
it comes under the head of bunkum with malice
aforethought. But, either way, it is an abso-
lutely incorrect statement of the case. The
Constitution (as set forth in clause 57 of the
Draft Bill) does not provide, in the case of a
deadlock, for the Lower House being dissolved
first; it distinctly provides that both Houses
shall be dissolved simultaneously. Therefore
the statement that “ the Upper House was not
subject to dissolution till the Lower House
came back from an appeal to the country ” is
misrepresentation, and fiction, and a very mean
kind of Tozee, and all the argument built upon
it about the superior power conferred on the
Senate is bosh of the worst description.
* * « •
Another lovely sample of Hughes’s ignor-
ance, or the other thing :
• STMtesrt reason for pausing was that-, once entered
into. Federation could not be receded from. Once in, we
could not get out of Federation, nor amend it, even if all
the people in N.S. Wales were unanimously for amend-
J^ccor hing to the Bill, if all the people of Victoria
“dfc-S. Wales were in favor of a change, and the people
ot Tasmania, West Australia, and South Australia were
against, the change could not be obtained. But these
three colonies, in population unequal to Sydney and
suburbs, could alter the Constitution to please them-
selves. To this day the rule of the dead hand was all
°' e J’ America, and this should be a warning. There
could be no amendment by any one colony, except by an
appeal to force of arms. That such a fearful thing as
that should be contemplated or invited by the passage of
this Bill, was a serious reflection on those who drew that
Bui, and was a retrograde policy opposed to Democracy.
It stamped this as behind other Constitutions.
In the first place, the three provinces mentioned
have not a population “ unequal to Sydney and
suburbs ” —instead they have 50 per cent, more
population. This is a small matter, but it
helps to demonstrate the ignorance and un-
reliability of this awful 'misinformer of the
public. Secondly, the three provinces of
Westralia, S.A., and Tasmania can’t “ alter
the Constitution to please.themselves.” Before
the Federal Constitution can be altered it is
necessary (as provided by clause 127 of the
Draft Bill) to secure the assent of a majority of
both Houses, a majority of the States, and a
majority of the total number of votes cast.
The only way in which the Constitution could
be altered without the assent of N.S.W. would
be by a unanimous combination of the other
four provinces. The statement that the three
small provinces can make what changes they
please is so utterly false that it would seem
either that the alleged mouthpiece of the
Labor-party has been shamefully misreported,
or else that he never read the Constitution Bill
at all, or that he invented his facts as he went
along. And Hughes, M.L.A, not only de-
nounces the Constitution because it can be
amended by the three small provinces—which
isn’t the case—but also because it can’t be
amended by one province. According to
Hughes it is “ a fearful thing ” that one
province can’t amend the Constitution “ on its
own,” and apparently without the consent
of the other four. And it certainly is—just as
fearful as the fact that one back-blox county of
N.S.W. can’t amend the N.S. W. Constitution
without the consent of the other counties.
* * » *
More of Hughes, M.L.A. :
The claims of New South Wales to the Federal city were
contemptuously set aside.
As a matter of fact, they were not. The Con-
vention, where the small States had a powerful
majority, simply decided that the matter should
be left to the Federal Parliament, where
N.S.W. will have far the largest vote. In
other words, it was left to the people. The
N.S.W. representatives themselves gave only
five votes in .favor of the proposal that the
Convention should fix the capital in New
South Wales. The proposal to fix it in
Victoria was set aside even more decisively,
only one Victorian supporting it. The idea that
the Convention should fix the capital in
S.A. or Tasmania was simply laughed out
of court, and didn’t get one vote. The
plaint of Hughes is, first, that the small States
have too much power to boss the popular
majority ; second, that they didn’t use their
excess of power in the Convention to boss the
popular majority in this particular matter,
and fix the capital in N.S.W. without the
assent of the people or even of the ten N.S.W.
representatives. In other words, he wants the
popular majority to rule when it suits him,
and is mad because the popular minority didn’t
rule in this instance.
* * * *
More Hughes :
The Bill was a subversion of the principles of Demo-
cracy and an abandonment of the rule by majority.
Which would be fine, impressive language if it
were not that Mr. Hughes neither spoke nor
voted against the Bill which deprived the
majority in N.S. Wales of the power to accept
Federation, and so fixed things that one vote
against the measure may actually outweigh
70,000 in its favor. And, considering how many
of the Labor-party voted to thus practically
disfranchise the electors, the less it says now
about its admiration for the rule of the
majority the better. Another sample from the
same orator :
Federation will cost N.S. Wales £1,300,000 per annum.
Coghlan’s figures have always showed that
N.S. Wales’s share of the probable extra cost of
the Federal Government will be under <£200,000
a year, but the other J 81,100,000 is dabbed ou
promiscuously to make the figures look large
and awful. But probably the finest thing of
all is Hughes’s explanation of the reasons why
the Federal Senate will always be a Tory insti-
tution :
Western Australia and Tasmania would probably send
Conservative members who “ gobbled ” like Mr. Dobson,
and experience of the last election had shown that only
those who were taken up by the papers, or had a big
reputation at their back, had any chance of getting
elected. Therefore, the Senate would always be a Con-
servative House.
The Senate will consist of men who have a
reputation. Therefore, it will always be Con-
servative. Consequently it seems that no Demo-
crat has any reputation.
* * * *
Hughes himself, as an opponent of Federa-
tion, doesn’t matter much. Like most of the
other anti-Federal orators up to date he does
his best, in a mean little way, to scare the
people by boldly stating things that are abso-
lutely untrue, and by putting things into the
•Federal Constitution which are not there, and
by telling of things which didn’t happen, and
by prophesying things that rest on no evidence;
but as one casual political atom he is of but
little account. The great question is—does he,
as is commonly supposed, fairly represent the
bulk of the Labor-party in this matter? N.S.
Wales had great hopes of its Labor-party at one
time, and even now that it is drifting into alli-
ance with provincial bigotry and Toryism, and
becoming the oatspaw of the Lyse and WANS’


and Heydon push, and the Cabbage-tree Mob,
and the ghost of old John Robertson, the
country still has hopes of it. There is no
reason why it should not be conscientiously
opposed to the present Federal scheme —so long
as its tactics are honest. No one ever expected
honest tactics from the rest of the bobtailed
political gathering which forms the bulk of the
anti-Federal party in N.S. Wales, and its
mode of carrying on the campaign excites no
surprise. But the Labor-party has a reputa-
tion for honesty and political decency to lose,
therefore it is reasonable to ask it, or the bulk
of it, if it endorses the utterances of Hughes,
M.L.A., and is at the back of his utterly dis-
graceful misrepresentations, and proposes to
run its political campaign in similar fashion.
If so, then it will make a fitting tail to the
Lyne and Heydon crowd; and the Lyne and
Heydon crowd will make an appropriate head ;
and there will be a beautiful finish and sym-
metry about the complete animal, and its front
and hind parts will be in entire accordance.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Representation Question.
When the Sydney Telegraph first turned
its coat on the Federal question, and bade
Premier Reid go and be some one else’s dog in
this matter, it tried to excite the public with
the terrifying information that representation
was so adjusted in the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment that 20 N.S.W. voters only had as much
power as one Tasmanian or Westralian. Last
week it so far climbed down as to state that
“ the value of a vote in some colonies will be
ten times as great as in others, and N.S.W.
happens to be the colony selected for the vote
with the minimum value.” The exact situation
is explained by this table, which shows the ar-
rangement as it really is :
Members!
Populations of i
Colony at end °f House of Senate. Total.
1897. Represen:
tatives. j
New South Wales 1,323,460 25 6 31
Victoria 1,176,238 22 6 28
South Australia... 363,044 7 6 13
West Australia ... 161,908 5 6 11
Tasmania 171,718 5 6 11
Total 3,196,368 64 1 30 I 94 _
So far as concerns the Lower House, therefore,
N.S.W., Victoria and S.A. have exactly the
amount of representation their population en-
titles them to, while Westralia and Tasmania
have rather more, and five Westralians or
Tasmanians equal eight cornstalks, Victorians
or South Australians. So far as concerns the
Senate, one Westralian or Tasmanian about
equals eight N.S. Wales voters, or seven Vic-
torians, or two South Australians. As it will
be two years, at least, before the Federal Par-
liament can really get started, and as both
Westralia and Tasmania are rapidly increasing
in population, it is probable that, before any-
thing really commences, they will have just
about their fair measure of representation in
the Lower House, and the proportions in
the Upper House will be a good deal more
equal than they now' are. Taking the totals
of both Houses, as things now stand, Tas-
mania, the province which is most over-
represented, has three times (not ten or twenty
times as alleged) its fair share of votes, and its
proportion must steadily decline. Even with
this handicap, N.S.W. and Victoria have more
than three-fifths of the total votes, so nothing
very dreadful lias happened. In N.S.W’., at
the 1895 election, Marrickville had one member
for 3296 voters, while Lismore had one for 1378,
and there were many other discrepancies almost
as bad, so the catastrophe which the Little
Cornstalk Party is so much alarmed about is
happening all round it every day quite un-
noticed.
Concerning a Possible Ghost.
The arguments advanced by the anti-Federal
orators in N.S.W. mostly bump up in a lament-
able fashion against their own conduct in
enacting that the Federal Constitution can’t be
accepted by the people unless 80,000 electors
vote for it, even if nobody votes against it.
Think what an unpleasant ghost would haunt
their meetings if some strong-lunged Federa-
tionist always kept chipping in like this :
Orator : The Draft Constitution is wholly inequitable
because it makes the vote of one Tasmanian equal in the
Senate to that of eight New South Welshmen.
Ghost : But it isn’t inequitable for the vote of
one N.S.W. provincialist to equal that of 79,999
federalists.
Orator : We want representation according to popula-
lation.
Ghost : Except at the Federal poll.
Orator : If the mass Referendum were embodied in the
Bill we might waive the question of equal State represen-
tation in the Senate.
Ghost : Why not have the mass Referendum at the
Federal poll then, you dashed, dashed (tapers off into
inaudible blasphemy. )
Orator : The Bill as it stands is a gross outrage on the
principle that the majority must rule.
Ghost (getting excited): Why don’t you let the majority
rule at the Federal poll, then ?
Orator (struggling to retain his composure): Under this
Bill it is possible for three States with 600,000 population
to block the decision of two States with 2,500,000 popu-
lation.
Ghost (returning hurriedly from the pub. across the
street with a clove in his mouth): And under your
blanky Bill it’s possible for one man to block the decision
of— hie— 79,ooo, aren’t it?
Orator : New South Wales would be always out-voted
by the other provinces.
Ghost (breathing a loud gin-breath) : You don’t mind if
70,000 men aie out-voted by yourself and another fellow
when it suits you, though.
Orator: All we want is a vote according to our popu-
lation.
Ghost : Well, if one of your votes is to count for as
much as 79,000 of the other people’s votes, is that accord-
ing to your population ?
Orator : I can’t possibly make myself heard if that man
interrupts.
Ghost (who has been taking more refreshment, and is
growing fightable): And you won’t let 79,999 people be
heard at the Federal election if one fellow on your side
interrupts?
Orator : The Convention Bill to a great extent dis-
franchises the electors of the large provinces
Ghost (with a shriek): And you’ve practically dis-
franchised 80,000 of the electors of New South Wales.
Orator : The Conservative element .
Ghost (jumping on his hat with excitement): You
weren’t conservative when you put. in the 80,000 mini-
mum to prevent a new idea getting a show, were you ?
[Ghost here ; Violently shot out and given into
custody.]
The Federal Tariff Again.
With an awful, dogged persistence Sydney
Telegraph keeps rebuilding the exploded lie
about how the Commonwealth Bill “ irre-
vocably ” commits Australia to Protection in
its most aggravated form, and should be jumped
on by every free and enlightened Cornstalk in
consequence. The utter falseness of this state-
ment has been shown several times or more,
but last Monday the D.T. arose as brightly as
ever, like a refreshed infant in the morning,
and told its busted fiction all over again, in
these words:
Yet the most outrageous fetter in the whole history of
Constitution-making appears in the Convention Bill in
the shape of Sir Edward Braddon’s clause, and the power
of the Commonwealth is limited for all time, and the
tariff is absolutely and irrevocably a foreclosed question.
Just by way of exposing this unscrupulous
attempt to trade on the public ignorance in the
interests of provincialism, and further, by way
of asking the once-respectable Telegraph if it
thinks it is a gentleman, and where it expects
to go to when it dies, the clause in question
(No. 87 of the Draft Bill) is here given ver-
batim :
87. Of the net revenue of the Commonwealth from
duties of customs and excise, not more than one-fourth
shall be applied annually by the Commonwealth towards
its expenditure.
The balance shall, in accordance with this Constitution,
be paid to the several States, or applied towards the pay-
ment of interest on debts of the several States taken over
by the Commonwealth.
If the federal tariff is so framed as to bring in
.£6,000,000, this clause allows the Common-
wealth to retain .£1,500,000 or less. If
the federal tariff is so framed as to bring
in 4d. a year, this clause allows the Com-
monwealth to retain Id. or less. The
Commonwealth has just the same unlimited
powers of taxation as the States have now.
It may decide to pay its own expenses
out of one-fourth of the customs and excise ;
or it may decide to pay them out of a federal
land-tax, income-tax, dog, horse, and bicycle
tax, tax on K.C.M.G.’s, poll-tax, by running
State banks and pawnshops and lotteries and
opium-farms, or by anything else it likes, and
have no tariff at all. The clause in question
doesn’t commit the Commonwealth to any tariff,
and even if it did the tariff couldn’t be absolute
and irrevocable, seeing that there are elaborate
provisions by which the Constitution can be
amended in any and every particular should the
people so desire. Also, nothing that man can do
is fixable for “ all time.” Every sane individual
in the community knows these things, and the
Telegraph is as sane as any other individual —
when it likes. So far, the whole anti-Federal
campaign has been run on a policy of dull and
weary mendacity, and why on earth the
N.S.W. provincial party didn’t nominate
Ananias as its sixth Convention candidate is
past understanding.
More “ Tattersall’s.”
“ Micawber ” to The Bulletin :
Ed. Bulletin. —There will never be that complete satis-
faction among “ Tattersall’s ” subscribers which the pro-
moter, I am sure, desires until he (as you have always
advocated) draws each sweep on the day of the race. I
venture to say that the great bulk of those who contribute
to make up these well-established sweeps have not the
least direct interest in, or knowledge of, racing, and
rarely go on to a course. To many such the privilege of
“ laying off,” if they get a likely starter, is not of any
real'advantage. Only the initiated know what is conveyed
by the expression “ laying off,” and the great majority
haven’t the least notion of how to proceed to “ lay off
in a multitude of cases they don’t even know what it
means. The motive is very praiseworthy on the
part of the promoter, and is typical of “ Tattersall,” but
it is quite ineffectual, and the system works badly. Prac-
tically, “laying off” is rarely indulged in. One must
“approach the owner,” otherwise -well, otherwise one
hasn't even Buckley’s chance. Happily this does not
apply to all owners. Some there are, to their credit be it
said, who discountenance and, so far as they can, dis-
courage the practice ; but all the same the impression is
gaining that unless the drawer of a horse in a sweep
approaches the owner and submits to be squeezed to
the extent of a-third or half of the sweep; he hasn’t
any show of getting a run for his money, or rather
for his ticket. Apropos of these sweeps, “ Tattersall ” en-
joys a unique position. His bluff and rugged honesty is
proverbial, and he is relied on absolutely. It is because
of his straightforwardness, and his recognised desire to
deal fairly with those who support him, that some day—
soon, I hope—l shall have t.ne gratification of reading in
The Bulletin an announcement to the effect “ that, in
response to the wish of many of his patrons, ‘ Tattersall’
has at length determined that henceforth all sweeps will
be drawn bn the day of their race, and not beforehand, as
heretofore.”
The N.S.W. Jury System.
When the N.S.W. Statute Committee presents
to Parliament the Bill consolidating the criminal
laws, it would be well for the Assembly to take
the opportunity of amending it in some im-
portant particulars. One of the parts which
needs reforming most is that relating to juries.
At present, no one seems to know how a jury
is summoned. The Sheriff is supposed to do
this work ; but how he does it, or on what prin-
ciples he acts, are questions to which it is im-
possible to obtain an answer. Carelessly done
the work must be, else it would not happen
that some men on the list are never called to
serve in the criminal-courts, while others com-
plain of their frequent summonses. The public
ignorance of the whole subject leaves it open
to any person to allege that the whole thing is
“ faked ” under the guidance of a conviction-
loving constabulary. This assertion has been
so often made that the public is beginning to
believe it true. The jurors on the list should
be summoned in such a way as to ensure that
no man should have to serve a second time
while there is one juror on the list who has not
sat on a case. Again, the law as to challenging
jurors stands sadly in need of amendment. At
present the Crown, though it has only the
same right of challenge as the accused, has
nevertheless the super-added right to order
jurors to “ stand aside” till the whole panel is
exhausted. Certainly, in such an event, the
panel must be recalled, but then the Crown
has still its right of challenge. This is not
wholly the fault of the legislature, which made
a bona-fide though blundering attempt to place
the accused—as he should be —on an equal
footing with the Crown. The Criminal Law
Amendment Act enacts (sec. 336) that
“... No person shall, except for cause shown, be
allowed , . . more than eight, or, if the offence charged
be capital, twenty challenges, and the Crown shall in
every case have the same, but no greater right."
The Supreme Court in R. v. Jury (10 W.N.)
held, and no doubt rightly, that though this
section effectually limits the Crown’s right of
challenge, it does not take away its common
law right of telling jurors to “stand aside,”
till the exhaustion of the panel, so that Parlia-
ment failed in its object. What is wanted now
is an added section taking away the Crown’s
right to order jurors to “ stand aside,” which
would place accused and Crown on the same
footing as regards this matter. Another
anomaly of the N.S.W. system of challenging
is that though no one, not even his legal ad-
visers, can advise an arraignee whom he should
challenge, the Clerk of the Peace and the police
are always on hand ready to prompt —and they
often do prompt —the Crown Prosecutor as to
what jurors to order to “ stand aside.” It is
noticeable also that a challenge by the Crown is
utterly unknown ; the other formula is just as
effective, and the right to challenge is held in
reserve. Here, too, some alteration of the law
is necessary. Add to these reforms another
preventing a presiding Judge from trying to
ram his opinion of the case down a jury’s
throat, and the jury, instead of being looked
upon as an institution which has almost had its
day, will be considered the only means of trial
worthy of free men, and a strong bulwark
against oppression.
Apropos of a large variety of things. When
all has been said, the Age, perhaps, is the only
daily paper in Australia run on really sound
journalistic lines, and its unique success is due
to that fact. It is owned and controlled abso-
lutely by one hard-headed man who knows his
own mind all the time. David Svme is pro-
bably the only Australian daily-newspaper pro-
prietor thoroughly competent to conduct a
journal personally. Most of the other owners
of dailies are ignorant, grocer-souled wretches
who couldn’t write an intelligent paragraph to
save themselves from roasting eternally in
Sheol—persons who take no real pride in their
newspapers and only regard them as a means
fff making money and “ working the oracle ” in
all sorts of little tin-pot ways. Also, when
the ownership of a newspaper is cut up in com-
pany form, that newspaper is almost invariably
used as a mere “ side-show ” to help along the
more important outside interests of the pro-
prietors. So while the grim, solitary David
Syme ever keeps his keen eye fixed on the face
of his one beloved goddess, the Age, you
see the proprietor-controllers of other dailies
walking round at night, as it were, with
all sorts of strange women to the great
damage of their legitimate wives. To anyone
in the know it is indeed of melancholy interest
to watch the works going in the offices of a lot
of the company papers —to see how reports of
public proceedings are shamelessly doctored
and garbled to suit the private interests and
prejudices of Mr. So-and-so, who holds so
many hundred shares, and is “ on the board ”
to see the policy of the paper get its back vio-
lently ricked because of something that hap-
pened or didn’t happen to Missis So-and-so at
Government House or at the meeting of such-
and-such a committee—or because Mister So-
and-So’s gas-share interests are at hopeless
variance with the proposed electric-lighting of
the city. Of course, what a man owns he’ll
control—whether it’s a newspaper or a brothel.
And they’re more than occasionally just the
same thing, with the result that the position of
“ the board ” is often exactly that of bullies in
a literary bagnio.

Beautiful item from Sydney Daily Telegraph
leading article of 6th April :
In his historic speech at the Town Hall Mr. Reid bared
to the public gaze a skeleton in the Convention cupboard,
the concealment of which would have amounted to an
act of most culpable deception towards the people of this
colony. That is the cut-and-dried arrangement about
the Federal capital. Concerning this matter, we have
the word of the Premier that if the Convention Bill
passes, it will mean the moral certainty of Melbourne
being made the seat of the Commonwealth Government.
And it is to be brought about by a subterfuge which, but
for Mr. Reid’s clear and candid give-away, could hardly
have been suspected. It was at first agreed by the anti-
New South Wales majority that the capital need not neces-
sarily be on Federal territory. The object of this, how-
ever, being somewhat too transparent, and calculated to
alarm the easy-going electors of the parent colony against
the Bill, an amendment was subsequently passed re-
voking that decision. It was thus made to appear that
neither Sydney nor Melbourne could be the capital, and
that in the selection of a site by the Federal Parliament
N.S. Wales might receive some consideration. But, as
Mr. Reid now informs us, this concession was an illusory
one, and calculated to grossly deceive those who regarded
it as placing N.S. Wales in any better position than before.
For all the time there was up the Victorian Premier’s
sleeve a nice little plan for cutting off a portion of Mel-
bourne containing Parliament House, public buildings,
and vice-regal residence, and offering them in a job line
to the Commonwealth as a ready-made capital.
The short answer to this boss prevarication is :
Premier Reid never made the alleged state-
ment. Instead of the Vic. Premier having the
alleged scheme up his sleeve, G. H. Reid
merely said that if he were in Turner’s place
he would make the offer alluded to, and the
scheme would, under certain conditions, be up
his (Reid’s) own sleeve. There was no revela-
tion of any plan for fixing the Federal capital
in Victoria or anywhere else. There was no
skeleton, no cupboard, no cut-and-dried
arrangement. There was also no candid “give-
away ” of anything. The whole tale is a pic-
turesque invention, a Kiama Ghost, and a
beautiful work of fiction generally.
“ L.M.” writes re the great Bush Thirst and its
consequences :
Dear Bulletin.— Why are you so severe on Thrift ? At
present it is the general habit of the bushman to “ knock
down ” his cheque as promptly as possible. No thought
is given to the morrow, and even decent wearing apparel
and blankets are dispensed with in the interests of the
unquenchable thirst for “three-star” and “fine old
Jamaica.” I have known scores of men to leave a station
with £2O or more in their pockets, and within a fortnight
to return in rags cadging for “tucker.” Is a destitute
man, who introduces himself to the manager by asking
for a pannikin of flour, in a position to dispute as to the
price to be paid for a given piece of work? The penni-
less worker aids the “ cutting-down ” process in his
anxiety to “ get off the track.” For instance, a station
wants burr-cutters at 12s. 6d. per week, or perhaps
less. The men who accept this employment do so
with the avowed intention of “ slingin’ it ” as soon
as they have earned the price of a pair of boots
or a shirt or something ; but as there is a steady supply
of toilers all wanting boots or shirts, the short-time sys-
tem makes no difference to the boss. He gets his burrs cut
at 12s. 6d. a week or less. Even in such cases it seldom
happens that the necessary boots are bought with the
money so acquired. It’s far more of a certainty to “ back”
the nearest pub. And thus it is in all classes of bush
work. Each oontract is eagerly rushed by a set of impe-
cunious lushingtons, and, providing.it will pan out a
week’s drunk, their troubles are small whether the price
obtained for their labor be high or low. I know that my
theory will be received with derision, but, nevertheless,
the average bush worker does more to blight his own and
fellow-workers’ lot than the average “ blarsted squatter.”
Let the toilers acquire the habit of keeping the few
pounds that they earn occasionally, so that they may be
in a position to “argue the point” when the next job
turns up, and I’m certain it would not be long before
wages reached a fair figure. At present the bushman
thinks he has done his duty by himself and his fellow-
man when he “ buys his ticket,” but, in my humble
opinion, he hasn’t. The worker must study thrift, for
capital, even in a limited quantity, constitutes strength.
Unity without hard cash gets left every time.

Dear Bulletin;— Re your recent commiseration
of a Chinaman, who suddenly demised through
the cutting off of his opium-supply by recent
enactment of Q. Govt. Don’t you think it’s
just as well for the country that that piteous
Chow handed in his checks ? Can any person,
whose existence depends upon the use of a drug
—opium, alcohol, absinthe, or what-not—be an
unadulterated blessing to any State ? Why ! the
same fate befell a white man not long ago out
Springsure way, and, though we may pity the
weakness of our degraded brother, we must
recognise the justice of the self-imposed punish-
ment, and the fortunate escape of an unborn
progeny. Are not inordinate appetites unmis-
takeable signs of disease ? and would it be an
unmitigated blunder if all the old chuckle-
headed, addle-brained inebriates were wiped
out by a similar enactment with regard to
alcohol ?—Llewelyn.
[This is how the small, smug, prosy, untempted moralist
so frequently “ compounds for sins he is inclined to, by
damning those he has no mind to.” Addiction to exces-
sive use of any drug may be a morbid condition —there is
reason to believe that genius itself is a morbid condition
—but, specially instancing alcohol, there is no getting
over the facts that in thousands of cases the predisposing
influence is an original geniality of nature—a special well-
affection towards mankind—and that men, not only of
the widest human sympathies, but of the greatest practi-
cal value to the social system, have for long periods
virtually lived on alcohol. The strength of a small man
may be feebler than the weakness of a great one. As to
opium, it is a lamentable fact that many men of almost
genius, the work of whose brains gives incalculable plea-
sure to the world, are dependent for the continuance of
their existence upon that and other narcotics. Such men
may not be “ unadulterated blessings ” to a community,
but still they are blessings. For that matter, no man is
an unadulterated blessing to any community.—Ed. B.]

A feasible way of excluding wealthy, or com-
paratively wealthy, patients from Australian
Government-subsidised hospitals—if their ex-
clusion as paying patients be found really
necessary—is to decline all payments over <£l
per week, and then to treat all patients exactly
alike. A wealthy patient who pays £5, .£lO or
£2O per week, often obtains in return a
private room, and special nurses, and extra
care and titivation—in all very much greater
service than his money represents. He
knows this, and goes for it as he would for
any other bargain. At the same time, the ab-
sorption by him and others like him of the ser-
vices of the institution, reduces the amount of
service available for the non-paying or £1 a
week patients. If only £1 a week were
accepted, and all, paying and .non-paying, were
treated to a £1 a week service, with share-and-
share-alike in hospital benefits, Crcesus would
invite doctors to attend him in his own house,
or he would seek a private and not a public and
Government-subsidised institution.
Magistrate Love’s refusal to give costs to the
prosecutor in a recent Redfern (Sydney) case
of non-attendance at school, is fully in accord
with the current practice in such cases in the
police courts of England. Magistrates there
regularly refuse costs to prosecutors on behalf
of school-boards and other educational authori-
ties, the influencing sentiment being that if
non-attendance at school be a punishable offence,
the prospective offenders should, as a condition
precedent, be freed from the obligation of
having to pay for attendance. If N.S.W.
magistrates accept influence of the same kind,
abundance of which exists, prosecutions under
the N.S. W. Act would promptly cease, for the
Department would have to pay half-a-guinea or
a guinea for every case sent into court, and thus
an important step towards free education would
be accomplished. As matters stand, N. S.
Wales Govt, professes to be unable to afford free
primary education, yet it spends £50,000 or
£60,000 a year in giving free postage to all sorts
of newspapers, bonafide and bogus.
TheS.M. Herald for once strikes a great
truth, and strikes it hard :
The Anti-Convention Bill party has, to borrow a phrase
from the French Revolution, resolved upon permanent
session. Three Parliamentary parties, all, under what-
ever disguise, attacking Federation, is surely a feature of
the situation that must have its lesson for the electors, to
whom and to whom alone the fate of Federation is re-
ferred. All this is so opposed to the ordinary working of
politics that we are bound to ask what it means. Ordin-
arily Parliament is quickly responsive to the indications
of public opinion, and seldom is it found, as in the present
case, steadily resisting the people, even to the extent of
disfranchising its own electors, as it did by the Enabling
Act Amendment Act, whereby if 79,000 votes be cast for
Federation they are cast in vain so far as Parliament de-
crees. The plain fact, which is not to be evaded or ex-
plained away, is that Parliament, as far as it can, means
to kill Federation. The only visible reason for this atti-
tude is that members of Parliament stand to lose
a great deal by this bill. Not to impute per-
sonal motives, there is a strong class bias aroused
by the prospect that with federation will come a les-
sening of the power and the dignity of the State Par-
liaments. To many members of the local bodies the
passing of the Commonwealth Bill must almost threaten
their political existence. The number of members in our
Assembly is 125, while the House of Representatives—the
more numerous body of the Federal Legislature, repre-
senting all the electors of Australia—will consist of only
about 60 members, and the Senate of 30 more, so that the
Parliament of the Federation, elected by the whole people
and discharging all the federal legislation and administra-
tion of the Commonwealth, will contain less than three-
fourths of the numbers of one House of the New South
Wales Parliament. The work of the State Parliaments
will necessarily then be less important in character than
novy, and it is scarcely likely that members of those bodies
will retain the same pay as at present. Men not of a cali-
bre that would entitle them to membership of the Federal
Parliament recognise that they are menaced with political
eclipse. Their importance at once shrinks, their func-
tions dwindle to tasks of local government, and it is in-
evitable that these diminutions will in time entail reduc-
tion of numbers and emoluments. While Federation
opens to our political leaders a career of immensely
larger opportunity, it condemns the inferior men to a
narrower and narrowing stage. They naturally dislike
the transformation. Any argument is good that will dis-
credit the Bill—it is un-Demooratic, though constitutional
authorities call it the most Democratic Constitution of
the kind ; it sacrifices the interests of N.S.W., but no one
quite defines these sacrifices ; its financial scheme is
deplorable, as to which experts disagree ; and the loss of
Freetrade appears to cause grief even in Protectionist
quarters. Under all disguises the unoomfortable fact re-
mains that the Bill threatens the interest of Parliament,
and Parliament resists accordingly.
