The new “ Silver King ” opened at Her
Majesty’s to a vast house, and there is every present
sign that business will continue vast. It is no
possible use arguing against a success which blows
criticism right off its legs, and the public appar-
ently doesn’t want to hear whether Barrett’s King
is a little better or a little worse than many pre-
vious monarchs who came back with long grey
hair, and honestly repaid to Dan’l Jaikes the
’a’penny he had advanced in a time of storm.
Taking it all round, the performance is a good one,
though not the best on the Australian record by
some distance. Wilson Barrett is a trifle heavy
in the early scenes, and when he is disguised as
a sailor outside a public-house, and has asked the
deity to put back the universe, and the universe
hasn’t gone back, and then he suddenly drops on
the overwhelming news of his own death in
the evening paper, he doesn’t seem to make
the most of the situation. And when he
throws off the disguise of Dicky, and that ancient
wreck breaks loose on the astounded criminals
with a large piece of wood, his exit is rather less
stirring than it might be. The Spider and
fatherly old Christmas hardly attempt to block his
retreat, and he steps out for home in a compara-
tively peaceful spirit. Wilson Barrett is a very
respectable King, but he isn’t an absolutely enor-
mous beacon on the shores of dramatic art.
* * • •
The new Nellie Denver (Miss Maud
Jeffries) is a clever and loveable woman in dis-
tress. Horace Hodges’ Dan’l is a great success ;
looking back down the leafy aisle of years, and
considering the many Jaikeses strewn thereon, the
writer has a vague idea that there was one
Dan’l somewhere whom he preferred to Hodges,
but the memory is so indistinct, and that
alleged Dan’l is so remote, that he wouldn’t
swear to him. The Elijah Coombe of Ambrose
Manning is good, though he goes to extremes of
piety that no previous Elijah ever attempted ;
and the present Olive Skinner fills the bill well
enough, and the Spider is a very moderate kind
of Spider, and when Dan’l is balanced against
any shortcomings of the other people the net
result is certainly satisfactory. One very small
item, by the way, is that the three old gaffers
who sit outside the public-house and read about
the ’orrid murder are not allowed the usual table,
and have to put their beer on the floor. And why
these old gentlemen should be put to the indignity
of laying their beer on the floor this paper can’t
imagine.
** * *
It is an open question whether the Cart-
wright co. was well-advised when it commenced
its Sydney Criterion season with “ A Marriage of
Convenience.” That pretty and clever French
comedy will never catch on with the gallery or the
hind row of the stalls. If the Chevalier acciden-
tally sat down on a pie, and got a large beef-stain
on the rear of his green suit, or the pert waiting-
maid fell overboard with the crockery, it might be
different; but the subtle humour and old French
cynicism and the wicked but gentlemanly frivolity
of very long ago only appeal to a comparatively
small section of the community. That section
which likes to get a vast quantity of brown re-
freshment in a pot for 3d., and doesn’t care very
much whether there is a dead bee in it or not, will
probably stay away.
* • * *
The “ Marriage ’ is a story of two
people in good society, who had a loveless wed-
ding by arrangement of their parents. One
of them doesn’t care a damn, in a giddy velvet tail-
coat of the olden time, that the other is in love
with another man, and the other doesn’t care a
straw, in a costly silk train, that the first one is in
love with a different woman, and they explain
these facts to each other with the courtesy of that
bygone generation which Robespierre scragged
over 100 years ago. When they fall in love with
each other, to their own great surprise, the Chev-
alier, instead of being noisily ejected, is cour-
teously removed in a gilded chariot with perfumes
and lofty grovel and accessories. Dumas’ drama
works itself out without any loud bang-crash,
or any free-fight between a mother-in-law and
a policeman. There is a lot of French humor
in the “Marriage of Convenience,” and the
dialogue sparkles brightly in every line with a
kind of wit which is too refined and expensive for
every-day use, and the play gets through gaily
without much incident, and skates along the
jagged edge of awful possibilities, yet never once
falls in. There are situations in it which, in some
dramas, would make the coster sool the dorg on to
the missis ; and which, in others, would make
George Rignold throw his young bride out of
doors so that she would bump on 16 different stairs
in her fall. And they are all glided over politely
and morally, and the froth of threatened adultery
is blown so gently off the top of the pot that
hardly anyone really notices that it was there.
* • * *
Miss Edith Morley, the young bride
who is torn away from a weak, green lover to
make a marriage of convenience, is the member of
the co. who probably impresses the audience most.
She is tall, graceful, and swan-like ; she has a
sweet voice, and acts intelligently, and looks nice
and she is one of the few ladies who can run with
a train on. Arthur Styan, the mild, green lover,
is a highly amusing person, nevertheless The
Bulletin is glad Miss Morley didn’t marry him.
xor he is only a frivolous, shrill butterfly at best,
and the large, dreamy Soul (the writer presumes
it is Soul) which looks out of Edith’s pensive eyes
calls for something better. Charles Thursby’s
Comte de Candale is a fine-looking, manly noble-
man, and his rendering of the part is just about
perfect. The star allows himself a chance in act
111., but, though the fierce old General takes well,
Sydney hasn’t seen enough of Cartwright yet to
feel quite sure of its opinion regarding him. But
its early impressions are favorable, anyhow, and
its views on Thursby and Miss Morley are wildly
cordial.
*- * * *
That universal favorite and brother to the
human race in general, Bland Holt, opened the
rusted portals of Sydney Royal on Saturday, and
ht upits old-established gloom with the “Cotton
King.’’ And, concerning that vast drama, there
is nothing 1 at all the matter, except the drama
itself. The scenery is especially magnificent.
The fittings are intensely elaborate. When B.
Holt puts on a mansion as a background to Act I.
it isn’t merely a delusion and a painted door ; the
door is open, and there are long real carpeted
corridors, and the premises are furnished—merely
in order that the public may see the furniture
through the windows. The Othello who
blacked himself all over was nothing to
this ; Holt blackens himself inside also.
In the great mill-scene the machinery is laid on in
tons, and the air is full of big wheels turning
round, and the lift that nearly flattens the heroine
into a brighter world is a fine piece of mechanical
accuracy. Also, a very strong company takes
charge of the drama—and the drama itself is a
weak affair which hangs anyhow, or thereabouts,
and seems to be thrown together
with a fork. It suffers from
the incurable complaint that
too many things happen off
the stage, and are mentioned
afterwards. In Act I. Cos-
grove remarks casually that
he has ruined Baker, and be-
trayed the young affections of
Miss Gill. In Act 11. he states
that he has robbed the firm,
though the audience is allowed
to see him plant the stolen
notes in Baker’s cash-box. In
Act 111. remarks which drop
from various people reveal that
Baker was accused of the rob-
bery ; also that he mysteriously
disappeared in America with
another vast amount of the
firm’s dollars, and has since
been missing. Further, some-
body mentions that Miss Gill
and her aged grandfather are
both dead. Cosgrove after-
wards observes that he had
artfully shunted Baker into a
madhouse in America, and
Baker turns up later on to tell
how the madhouse was burned
in America, and how he
escaped. Barring the thrilling
crime by which Cosgrove tries
to scrunch Miss Watson’s bones
in the lift, most of the interest-
ing episodes happen in America,
and are explained afterwards.
That is what is the matter
with the “ Cotton King.”
* * *
There are quite a multi-
tude of deserving people in
Bland Holt’s co. Walter Baker
is the fine, manly actor he
always is. Miss Fitzmaurice
Gill steadily improves, and is
developing into a very finished
actress. Albert Norman is
always effective; Bland Holt
and Mrs. Bland are the same
favourites that they have been since Sydney first
made their acquaintance; and Miss Flora Anstead
is an elderly lady who is calculated to be an orna-
ment to any family. Cosgrove and Miss Elizabeth
Watson have certainly not improved since Sydney
saw them last, and the former is painfully de-
pressing at all times, but Charles Brown and Miss
Brightie Smith both deserve honourable mention.
And there isn’t one thoroughly effective character
to divide among all these people. Albert Norman
gets about the best show, inasmuch as he does his
work on the spot and hasn’t to report any lament-
able circumstance which happened in America,
and the next-best part is entrusted to the lift.
** * *
“ The Phantoms,” a play by Shirley and
Conquest, is to be staged next Saturday at Sydney
Lyceum, and until then the “Yorkshire Lass”
fills the aching void. Apropos of which The Bul-
letin presents this week a portrait of Miss Inez
Bensusan, the new Australian actress, whose ren-
dering of the wicked French lady in the evening
dress that is cut low has roused this usually ex-
hausted organ to sudden enthusiasm. It is a
considerable time since a new Australian actress
of promise rose in the eastern horizon, and The
Bultetin desires to impress on Miss Bensusan
that there is a large void to fill, and that it is her
duty to fill it. Also, it has some hope that she
will prove equal to the occasion.
** * *
The Kellino Family and a very promising
new songstress, Miss Lizzie Kirk, have been added
to the co. at Sydney Tivoli this week, and that
promising show is in its usual good health and
spirits.
** * *
Harmston’s Circus is steadily gathering in
the fleeting shekel at Belmore Park, Sydney, and
has fairly established itself as a show which
deserves public support. The cold, dank, muddy
nose of the baby elephant appeals tearfully for
public support, on the ground that the baby ele-
phant is an orphan, its large baggy parent having
died in its early infancy. And the appeal has
been liberally responded to. Hashinoto, the Jap.
who does amazing things on his loose wire, slid off
that precarious support once during the week and
bounced rather suddenly on the hard earth, and
this may be taken for a sign of good faith as
showing that Hash has no invisible supports, and
is really the Jap. he professes to be.
* *• * *
A benefit is being organised for Lachlan
M Gowan, a deserving actor, who has been before
the Australian community at intervals for nearly
40 years. He vanished from public view some
time ago, and very few people were definitely
he was still among the living. Lach-
lan M Gowan is very old now, hopelessly incapa-
citated by illness, and very poor, like most men of
his precarious profession in their declining years ;
therefore it is proposed to assist the aged mum-
mer and his family by a matinee show at Her
Majesty’s.
** * *
“Babes in the Wood,” at Melb. Princess’s,
has lost none of its massive splendor whilst grow-
ing a little brighter than at first in the song and
“gag ” departments. Anything that appeared to
pause has been cut out of the gorgeous show, and
the residue, plus latest editions, is always a rapid
joke or a moving spectacle, as the case may be.
Bert Gilbert’s inebriated baron grows more con-
vincingly drunk on further acquaintance. He
diffuses a sense of alcohol throughout the house.
One finds oneself thinking of what might happen
to the orchestra if the Baron were to fall over the
footlights and bless those fragile musicians with
his dying breath. And then comes the vexed
question as to whether he will carry his intoxica-
tion beyond the grave and be doomed to an
eternity of sobering-up. The Baron is a matter of
Miss INEZ BENSUSAN (Lyceum Theatre, Sydney).
absorbing interest. Miss Reeve sings a new song
that fetches them, as usual, and the two ruffians
have a topical duet to cheer them on their way.
Also, the cherubic ruffian has a joyful resurrection
every evening after his hoarse comrade-in-arms
has put him to the sword. Robin Hood and the
thirty-five other people whose names appear on
the bill are likewise doing well, which remark
will apply with equal force to the Wedding Break-
fast, and the forest scene, and the limbs of the
ballet.
#* * *
At the Alexandra an augmented co. is
mostly blacking its face and bursting into song.
Uncle lorn s Cabin,” as produced by Dan Barry,
seems very like a variety entertainment, wherein
the horrors of slavery are set to music and the plot
has to dance for its living. Even George Harris
can’t make a bolt for freedom until he has sung
‘ ‘ The Holy City ”in a decent tenor voice. The
writer has heard worse—much worse—concerts
than *' Uncle Tom’s Cabin.” A melodious Tom is
supplied by veteran John Matlock, who, probably,
acts as chorus-master, and undertakes to keep the
old plantation in tune. It was 11.30 pan. on
Saturday ere John ascended towards the roof on a
platform of cloud, nevertheless the bulk of the
audience waited to see him go. From this The
Bulletin augurs a fortnight’s run for the weary
nigger. _ Tom, under any circumstances, is an
institution who appeals to some people, and the
desire to see him walloped whilst saying his
prayers can be gratified for sixpence at the Alex-
andra.
** * *
The gaudy circus-drama at Melb. Royal
pegs away with increasing energy. It is now in
its third week, and, judging from treasury returns,
the last of Charles Holloway’s attempts to roast
the heroine and seize her inheritance will not be
baffled for another fortnight. As the baffled cap-
tain—no need to mention that the villain is a cap-
tain—Holloway deserves some sympathy, seeing
that he would gladly marry the girl in the first
instance, and never thinks of burning her until
she has ordered him to get out. Moreover, the
scheme of placing her on an altar (in an Exhibition
building), to be licked by the flames of a small
wood fire, stamps him as a man of genius. Any-
one who looks closely into the subject will see that
the gallant captain must have intended to hide all
traces of the crime by eating his victim’s nicely
browned remains.
** * *
“Maritana” was set up again at Melb.
Bijou on Saturday, and that interesting old speci-
men of British ballad opera looked capable of
drawing fair audiences for one week only. The
Vic. Operatic Society is managing the show in aid
of the Children s Hospital, the performance being 1
mostly amateur and gratis. Charitably viewed it
is a good performance pleasing in most respects
and never deplorable at any point of the struggle
Amateur Bonynge Kelly, for instance, although
handicapped by sickness on Saturday, gave a
mild suggestion of a capital Don Jose. The light,
but wiry, tenor, William Neilson, gets through
with honors. J. Goudy, as the King of Spain,
sings up to the average professional standard, and
the funny man, Lambcroft, fills his place humor-
ously. Miss Millie Chapman (Maritana) is a
sweet vocalist, also a capable leading lady for any
amateur co., and the perils of presenting a nervous
novice in a boy character have been avoided by
engaging Miss Nina Osborne to play Lazarillo.
Miss Nina in “ Alas! those Chimes ” doesn’t
equal sister Ida, but the imitation is satisfactory.
The Vic. Operatic Society upholds its character
for chorus-singing, and if some of the figures in
the background suffer keenly from their costumes
the circumstance is no laughing matter. A bank-
clerk, who tries to look like a Spanish grandee
whilst fixing an anxious eye upon the conductor’s
beat, is an object for compassion.
** * *
Festive Lottie Moore, and Bellman, like-
wise the Hulines, the Rockley Bros., and some
other people who haven’t been there before, came
into the Opera House bill on Saturday. It is a
pretty long bill, concluding with the Three Hegel-
manns, as usual. The writer can’t think of any-
thing new and original to say about the Hegel-
manns, but after witnessing those flying German
gentlemen for the ninth time he is still disposed to
accept them as the “ most wonderful gymnasts on
earth.”

The reappearance of Professor Kennedy,
at St. George’s Hall, threatens to be a great and
gruesome success. The crowd, on Saturday, was
trying to get into the premises half an hour before
the doors opened, and, by the time Kennedy had
brought his pale auburn hair to bear upon his
subjects, ’ the police were prohibiting any further
sale of tickets. The Professor’s display of hyp-
notism-CMm-humbug is just the same as last time.
Several of his old “ subjects ” are doubtless with
him once again, unless they have died during his
rather long absence from the awful effects of
Kennedy on the brain, and kerosene in the
stomach.

The Atacama Enquiry.
The old gentlemen of the effete and discredited
N.S.W. Marine Board nodded over the Atacama
enquiry all last week ; and this week—after The
Bulletin went to press—they were announced to
nod still further. The Atacama, as most Bulletin
readers are by this time aware, was a wooden
Chilian ship of 1572 tons register, 23 years old.
She put into Sydney harbour in October, leaky
and unseaworthy, and the Chilian consul-as he is
by law required to do—sent surveyors aboard
to ascertain in the interests of the owners if
she was worth repairing. They reported that she
was not; and that it would take something like
.£3500 to make her seaworthy, and the vessel, in a
condemned condition, was sold for <£7oo The’pur-
chaser repaired her, as he states, “at a cost of
■*lBoo, and after repairing she was examined by
underwriters’surveyors, who declined to recom-
mend her for any insurance which might
be applied for. The Atacama was then
sent to Newcastle, where she loaded 1778 tons of
insured coal, and sailed for an American port on
Jan. 29. She started leaking, and foundered
some 300 miles from Sydney. One boat, with the
captain and three others, reached port after losing
a boy who died of exhaustion ; the other two
boats containing twelve seamen disappeared,
and will probably be heard of next when the sea
gives up its dead. At previous sittings of the
Marine Board the captain, the shipwright
who executed the repairs, and others, have
declared that the vessel was thoroughly seaworthy.
Last week, Capt. Grainger (marine surveyor).
Robert Pollock (Lloyd’s surveyor at Sydney)’
Capt. Vine Hall (marine surveyor), Capt. Laid-
man (chief surveyor to the Underwriters’ Associa-
tion), Capt. Mackenzie (marine surveyor), and
Capt. Bentley (shipwright and surveyor), and
others, gave detailed evidence to show that the
Atacama was in a rotten and unseaworthy condi-
tion when she put into port, and that she could
not have been made assuredly seaworthy without
stripping th ■ copper and overhauling the bottom—
which was not done. Before the enquiry was
adjourned over the Easter holidays, Mr. Wilshire
who appeared.for the Crown, complained of the
remarkable manner in which the Marine Board
had conducted the case. That IMr. "Wilshire had
ground for his remarks is evident to any un-
prejudiced person who has watched the Board’s
singular attitude throughout the enquiry—
as if the issue was a foregone conclusion.
The Bulletin’s remarks about the Atacama’s
drowned crew, . however, have been rewarded
by a libel - writ claiming £2OOO damages at
the instance of Mr. M. C. Cowlishaw, owner of
the vessel ; so that the public will learn the facts
of the case when they are placed in full, not before
a more or less antiquated committee of shipowners’
representatives and others, but before the high
and unprejudiced tribunal of the Supreme Court.

Dear Bulletin, —How do you account for
the fact that some quarrymen work bareheaded
under the summer sun and know nothing about
sunstroke or headache ? The sun on a hot day in
a quarry is Sheol. I know one man who has
never worn any covering on his head, except his
mass of curly hair, tanned with the sun.—K.

That well-known old Melbourne identity,
Edmund Finn, died last week at Fitzroy, aged
79 years. He arrived from Ireland in 1841, and
lived in the city by the Yarra all his life. In his
early days he contributed to the Patriot and
Herald newspapers, and in ’45 joined the latter
as reporter. He remained on the staff under
Arehib :Id Michie, Dr. G. S. Evans, and others,
until lfc3B, when big John O’Shanassy, then Pre-
mier (who never lost a chance of giving a fellow-
irishman a billet), appointed him Clerk of the
Papers in the Leg. Council. He published an in-
teresting volume, “The Chronicles of Early Mel-
bourne,” and retired on pension in 1886. He was
a quiet, unobtrusive little man, and hardly ever
wandered beyond Smellbourne.

* * • *
Under the N.S.W. income-tax law, the local
holder of N.S.W. Government securities has to pay tax on
his interest, while the foreign holder is exempt. There-
fore if two men —one in N.S.W. and one in England—
have £lOOO each due to them, over and above the ex-
emption, as dividends on State securities, the Englishman
gets £lOOO and the Cornstalk gets £950. In other words,
the outsider gets £3 per cent, and the local investor re-
ceives £2 18s. 6d. It was claimed that this distinction
had to be made by way of “ keeping faith with the foreign
investor.” Britain, at all events, doesn’t keep faith with
its foreign investor in this fashion. For over 50 years it has
deducted income-tax from the interest paid to the foreign
holder of consols, ami if he doesn’t like it he has simply
to do the other thing. And though the foreign holder
has wailed very grievously outside Bull’s door about this
“breach of faith,” and has blown bitter lamentations
through the keyhole, the only answer was “ Rats ! or
something to that effect.
* » * *
The process by which the Vic. State Land Bank
raises money for the purpose of making advances is
degenerating into a howling farce. When it offers £24,000
worth of 3 per cent. State-guaranteed bonds the public
generally subscribes £3OO at par, and the Savings Bank
has to make up the balance. Yet the Vic. Government’s
3 per cent, securities are in strong demand locally at
£lO3 7s. 6d. The whole difficulty, apparently, lies in the
fact that the State Land Bank securities have no fixed
currency, and are liable to be repaid as any time. If they
were fixed for 25 years they would probably sell at £lO2
anj'how, and a lot of money is being ridiculously fooled
away under the present arrangement.
* • » *
The Victorian coal output steadily increases.
Between ’92 and ’97 it grew' from 30,000 to 240,000 tons,
and the imports of N.S.W. coal into Victoria diminished
in the same period from 739,000 to 527,000 tons. The new
coal finds which are opening up among the Otw'ay ranges
between Geelong and Warrnambool are expected to give
the industry another big lift.
Just when anew gas is too grievous a subject
for mention, and the partially-combusted Education
Minister Garrard has hardly recovered the hair that was
blown off him in the last sad experiment, new's of another
invention (which reads painfully like the one which blew
up Garrard) comes from Victoria. The Age, however,
sw'ears in a serious spirit that the little tow T n of Frankston
has been lighted with the new alleged illuminant since
last November. This seems convincing, though if an
inventor has been brilliantly lighting up a town w’ithin 30
miles of Melbourne by a new gas, which costs only Is. per
1000 ft., and Ift. of which is equal to 18ft. of coal-gas, and
has been at this w’ork for four months, he seems to have
attracted remarkably little notice. One would have ex-
pected that the roar of the new discovery would have
been heard all over Australia ere this
The Age’s description reads as follows:
“ First of all, Mr. Stephenson placed in the carburetter
(which is simply an ordinary iron box with taps for con-
veying air on one side and discharging the gas on the
other) a pint of kerosene, then he added about a couple
of ounces of powdered resin, next a certain proportion of
shale oil, about an ounce of salt, half an ounce of ether,’a
few drops of bi-sulphate of carbon, half a gallon of gaso-
line, a little camphorated chalk, and, finally, a small dose
of oil of cloves. Before these articles were placed in the
carburetter, that instrument was thoroughly examined by
the company present. It was found to be empty, there
were no compartments inside, and it was not attached to
any fixture except those connected with the apparatus.
The ingredients were purchased at a chemist’s shop by a
gentleman present, and the proportions used by Mr.
Stephenson would probably represent a value of 2s.
Having placed the various compounds in the carburetter,
it was well shaken, the air was turned into it, the tap
from which the gas escapes was turned on, and when a
match was applied to the burner a flame similar to that of
coal gas, but of greater brightness, was at once given
forth. This is the light that has been in existence at
Frankston for the past four or five months, and it has
given great satisfaction. The materials used were suffi-
cient to maintain one light five or six hours a day for a
period of six weeks. In order to obtain a light of
still greater brilliancy, Mr. Stephenson has made a further
discovery. Using a generator specially made for the pur-
pose, he produced another form of gaseous fluid, the
illuminating capacity of which is equal to some 45 candle-
power. The ingredients used in the manufacture of this
gas are a mixture of iron and low carbons, enclosed in
linen cartridges 8 or 9 inches long and 1 inch in diameter,
unslaked lime, napthaline balls, and camphor. The lime
coming in contact with the water sets up heat, causing the
carbons to discharge a gas. That proved to give a light
of extraordinary brilliancy, and, despite its power, the
consumption of gas is exceedingly small. The best results
are obtained from a combination of what, for convenience,
we may call the Frankston gas and the new aeriform. The
light was a clear, steady, beautiful flame, dazzling to look
at. Mr. Stephenson stated that his gas in yesterday’s
experiment was burning at the rate of only 1 foot an hour,
and would, in that time, give a light equal to 18 feet of
coal gas.”
But what the public wants is, to be allowed to
hump that carburetter into the middle of a 640-acre pad-
dock, and then put in the 2s. worth of ingredients, and see
the beautiful gas come out and burn for six weeks. The
community is dreadfully low-spirited about new gases just
now, and requires a lot of evidence.

« * • •
The Northern Permanent Building Society
(Melbourne) held a comic-opera meeting recently. The
manager, one Smith, reported that the directors had
ceased to meet because there weren’t enough of them left
to form a legal quorum, and the society had been practi
cally running itself for some time. Also, he explained
further on, the board had resigned, and there were now nc
directors at all. The accounts were not audited, because
there was no money to pay the auditors. The Bank o'
Victoria was suing for £ll,lOO overdraft, and a lady
depositor was also suing for £6OO and interest due her
The bank held all the securities, and had ordered the
creditors to make their payments to it instead ol
to the society. Assets, put in at their original papei
value, £24,000; liabilities, £27,000. Seeing that there
was no cash, and the bank had all the securities and was
collecting the money, the short way out of the difficulty
was obviously to drink the furniture and then lock up the
office and drop the key in the Yarra, but the meeting
decided to struggle on a little longer, if possible.

On what a thin foundation the financial reputa-
tion of any financial magnate may depend is shown by the
fact that a claim of a few pounds against one very “ big ”
man has closed the credit which, a few months ago,
would readily have been given (to anything in reason) by
any bank in his province.
» * * *
There appears to be good foundation for the
plaint about the intrusion of German and other mail
steamers into the Australian coasting trade. Now that
the German boats call at Fremantle, they compete all
round the coast from that port to Sydney ; and, as they
are assisted by a large German State-subsidy, and only
pay about half the wages paid on the local vessels, they
can quote fares and freights which the latter would find
utterly ruinous. And things were none too good in the
local shipping business even before this intrusion. This
is another neglected industry which badly needs protec-
tion.
* * * *
Every now and then something is heard in a
casual sort of way about the utter rottenness and insol-
vency of Australian friendly societies. A recent Govern-
ment return showed that the S.A. friendly societies had
undertaken £1,846,741 of liabilities, and had only
£1,092,443 of present and visible future assets to meet
them with, or a magnificent shortage of £754,000 odd.
There are 14 friendly societies in S.A., and only one is
solvent. The Victorian societies have £1,217,240 of liabili-
ties and £873,545 in view to meet them with, or a beautiful
deficit of £323,695. The trouble seems to be that the
average society promises too much in return for too little,
and is quite content as long as there is enough cash on
hand to pay the claim when Brother Smith dies.

The generous Tas. Govt, publishes the following
notice “ Applications will also be received from sufferers
by bush fires for a license to occupy Crown lands not
adjoining present holdings, at the rate of 10s. per 100
acres, for three years, on condition that such selector lays
the land down in artificial grass during the first year of
occupation.” Which means that the unfortunate selector
who would be fool enough to snap at such an offer would
have to lay out about £3OO the first year. A cheaper way
would be to buy the land straight out. The notice goes
on to say that “ no licensee will be entitled to any com-
pensation from the Crown for any improvements which
may have been effected on the Crown land during the cur-
rency of his license.” Go on the land, young man !
Very startling disclosures are promised in re
land transactions up North. The person chiefly interested
is a Southern magnate, whose generous family settlements
are sure to be upset when the scandal becomes public.

At a recent meeting of creditors of the late
George Hill (of Criterion Theatre, Sydney, and Marvel
fame), the statement of affairs showed liabilities exceeding
£300,000, and an apparent deficiency of £170,000. The
largeness of the liabilities is accounted for by the fact that
“Old George” had a weakness for giving guarantees
“ for people he liked ” —which, apparently means that he
reckoned nearly half the adult population of Sydney as
his bosom friends.

The Tarrawingee tram-line is to be pulled up by
the roots ; the flux-quarries are already abandoned, the
remaining B.H. Prop, furnaces on the mine having been
run down. The average Tarrawingee shareholder didn’t
get his money back ; but as he —or at least the biggest of
him—was helped into the Emu Bay spec, there is a chance
for him even yet if he keeps very wide awake.
* * * *
The diminutive Yuba appoints that good
man, Richard Piper, long ago mine-manager of the
Broken Hill Proprietary, its manager. What tricks
luck (or something else) does play ! Look at Piper
and Zeb. Lane. Ten years ago Piper’s prospects
were far the better. Lane was managing the North 8.H.,
struggling then as now to pay its first div., while Piper
was in charge of the big show and—his luck again—was
the first man to find the bugbear zinc in the workings.
Piper went to the South, Lane to Block 14. The latter
got into divs. ; the other couldn’t. Up went Lane, down
went Piper. They left for the West at about the same
time, equally undecided. Piper went to the Murchison,
Lane to Ooolgardie. Piper tried for reefs, and found poor
ones ; so he drifted. Lane tried a poor one and stumbled
by accident upon an exceedingly rich one. And so on.
Lane swims in champagne, if he wants to, and has
“ dooks ” for company ; Piper goes back to the recesses
of the awful Barrier range, and communes with two
workmen and the rabbits that have their warrens round
the poor little shafts over which alone he is “ boss.”
* * * *
The Barrier’s timber-bill will show for this year
a big falling away in proportion to quantity of stuff ex-
tracted. From North to South more attention is being
paid to filling up the depleted stopes with mullock than
has been ever done before. Several shafts and passes are
being made specially for shooting down surface waste ;
and generally, for the first time, the field has shaken from
itself the ’Murkan open-timbering notions which W. R.
Wilson trumpeted and general-manager Patton intro-
duced 10 years ago. It took a lot to knock them out, for
the square-sets looked so neat and reliable ; but. the
Junction collapse completed the work which the British,
and Proprietary, and Central creeps, and the big-nnne
timber-fires had carried on for years.

An amalgamation of B.H. Junction, Junction
North, and North is being canvassed.
* * » •
The British is suffering in the same way as the
South from the hardening of ore with depth, and the con-
sequent increase of concentrating difficulties. And, as for
Block 14, it dodges the trouble temporarily, and piles up
greater trouble for the future, by confining its stopes to
the shallower levels.
* * * •
Some nine months ago one Capt.. Cock, ex-
manager of many mines in Australia, including the
Golden Fleece, Bendigo, in its halcyon days, having
worked copper in Cornwall, and seen the big work of the
Cobar tributors, started to un water the old Blayney cop-
per-mine, N.S.W. Others—the usual crowd of represen-
tatives of Rothschild, Col. North, &c. —had had it under
offer, but made ho attempt to pump out. The old man
put his three boys on the job, and, finding a steam pump
impracticable, they turned the pump into a winding-
engine. That—probably the funniest winding-engine
N.S.W. has ever seen—has been working night and day
for eight months, raising at the rate of a 60gal. bucket a
minute; When they reached the 120 ft. level of Marks’
shaft, the Capt., having mortgaged all his worldly
possessions, and gained only an inch a day on the water,
decided to look for ore in the old workings about that
level. There it was in abundance. He repaired the old
reverberatories, and started smelting, and his first consign-
ment of copper went away last week. He is working an
ore-body over 20ft. wide, taking it to the smelter as it
comes, and is sending his copper to market. Tens of
thousands of tons of ore of apparently higher grade than the
average of the big Cobar mine have been left in sight.
Also, the veteran having tried several times to raise money
in Sydney on a share-basis, and being now pestered with
letters offering capital allegedly on behalf of Rothschild,
Hooleyand others, is in a fit condition to receive such men
with a shot-gun. He means to put up a blast-furnace, for
which the ore is well fitted, and otherwise imitate the
Cobar tributors, from whom he got his object-lesson. If
reports are correct, the richer ore-bodies of the old Blayney
mine are still under water.

* * • *
One asinine provision of N.S.W. law is that
which gives a license to a timber-getter to enter Crown
lands leased for pastoral purposes and cut thereon, but
denies him the right to unyoke his team while he is thus
engaged. So the squatter who doesn’t mind making
enemies most effectively preserves the timber on his run
by the simple process of impounding the teamster’s stock,
which in most cases simply must be unyoked.

Roughly, mining stocks of Queensland for ’97
may be put down at £10,000,000, and Westralian stocks
for same period at £2o,ooo,ooo—with dividends for year of
£480,000. Value of Tasmanian mining stocks for same
year £10,000,000, with dividends £231,000. Putting the
two together, we get Tasmania and VVestralia with market
value of stock £30,000,000, and total dividends £711,000 ;
while Queensland, with stocks valued at £10,000,000,
returns £1,000,000 in dividends.

• • * *
Mount Leonora (W.A.), the township which
has sprung up around the Sons of Gwalia mine, is (writes
a Bulletin correspondent) practically a one-mine place,
for the present, anyhow. A number of leases have been
pegged out, though nothing like what is reported in the
papers, and no attempt has yet been made to work them.
Bewick, Moreing and co. enjoy about, a monopoly of
pride of position in relation to the Sons of Gwalia. YVhen
you hear of this place booming, don’t be led away with
the notion that it is the townspeople’s doing. They
haven’t got enough energy to look outside to see a shower
when it comes. The peopie seem to sorrowfully anticipate
the coming of the boomster, because his millions might
galvanise them into activity. However, winter willalmost
certainly see a move here, as the possibilities of the
district are very inviting indeed. There are no police
stationed in the town yet, consequently everything is
orderly.
• * * *
The Critic deals with W.A. boomster Calvert.
Consolidated Gold Mines of W.A. reconstructed. Cal-
vert resigned, legal opinion taken as to prosecuting pro-
moters. Imperial W.A. Corporation Board reconstructed,
Calvert resigned, legal opinion taken as to prosecuting
vendor. Bayley’s South No. 2, Calvert resigned, mine a
failure, company in liquidation. W.A. Steam Packet and
Transport Co. “ This asset consisted of mere idea of Mr.
Calvert’s.” Details with the names of the directors who
controlled Calvert’s “ cats ” are given. They are jumping
on the boom and boomsters in earnest now that all is over
and the boodle has vanished. Nobody jumps on the
writer except it be an occasional reminder —“ What about
the Great Boulder ? ” Bayley’s No. 2 South supplies a
typical instance of promise and performance. “ From
the 200 tons at grass,” said vendor, when the mine was
being floated, “ I picked up pieces of rough and
shotty gold. Other pieces showed fine gold per-
meated right through. Mr. told me that
the whole heap then stacked would return from 5 to
7oz. per ton. I can conscientiously recommend this pro-
perty as a thorough and practically-developed concern,
and it is just in that state now only to require machinery
to treat the stone to turn it at once into a dividend-paying
mine.” Up to date, not a single W.A. boomster has been
struck by lightning, though Ananias was only a small man
in the business. Let the battery have a word—and “ the
machine can’t lie.” One hundred tons produced 290 z.,
and 62 tons of carefully picked stone, representing the
very best ore that could be found in the mine, yielded
230 z. Even this class of rock the directors “ regret to say
has dwindled away, and the reef has pinched out.” But
it will take more than this to kill Calvert. Cats have
nine lives—cat-mongers 99.

Premier Forrest, who usually displays more tact
than the average W.A. M.P., was a victim to his own
stubbornness in the hustling he lately got at Kalgoorlie.
His first mistake was in travelling with Moran, M.P., who
ought to have sense enough to see that his presence with
the Premier would incense the alluvial men —and if not,
he ought to have been told by Forrest to make himself
scarce. Next mistake was in taking to himself groans in-
tended for Moran, who is a shareholder in the Venture
lease, and whose political humbugging has been a little
too palpable of late. Other mistakes followed. Had
Forrest, after receiving the deputation, gone on the bal-
cony in response to repeated calls, the chances are he
would have appeased the crowd, as he has often done
before, in a few minutes. As it was, he kept thousands of
persons standing in a blazing sun and fierce wind for two
hours, during which time many had taken more shypoo
than they wanted, and the hustling followed. Chiefly the
result of the Premier’s own folly.

Black Flag Proprietary (W.A.) granted six

"** • *
Members of Vic. Mining Boards complain
bitterly of the way their suggestions are bluffed or
muffed by the Department. The Boards clamor for local
inspectors who would be able to be on the job soon after
the occurrence of an accident. As it is, a mine-manager
has time to fix things up beautifully before an official
examination is made, and, consequently, inspections, as a
rule, are farcical. Minister Foster thinks he knows better.
It is Foster’s lamentable conceit to regard himself as an
authority on mining because he once kept store in a
mining district, and has since poked at a reef here and
there with the ferrule of a walking-stick.
• • * *
From the Age report of a recent local sewer
fatality : “ Duncan M'Gregor adjusted a charge in a
tunnel, and, walking to the shaft, a distance estimated at
170 feet, lighted the fuse.” After all these years of mine-
reporting the Age still thinks that fuse is burnt by the
mile. Think of 170 ft. of fuse and the nice long holiday
the men would have waiting for it to burn down to the
charge ! Think, too, of the rank blasphemy of the boss of
the shift, who regards an extra six inches as a
extravagance !

shares rose from 9s. to 225. on the strength of a dis-

* • * •
The Ziman syndicate is active on the gold coast
of M.L. During the last two years, since it took over
the Globe and IT ess mines at Reefton, the Con-
solidated Goldfields Co. has spent £55,000, and perman-
ently employed 423 men, and erected an aerial line carry-
ing 4350 tons per working week. The operations start at
1700 ft. above sea-level; shafts have been carried down
945 ft. ; there are seven levels, in Nos. 4,5, and 6 of which
ore bodies have been found and gold is visible. It is the
opening out of these levels which affords the most inter-
esting, as it is the most recent, phase of West Coast
mining. In No. 7 level, just opened, the ore body, 30ft.
wide, has been proved for a length of 800 ft., showing a4J
years’ supply actually in sight.
* * » *
Dear B. —Your correspondent, “D.W.D.,” ad-
vances quite a unique theory re occurrence of the Mt.
Lyell ore-bodies when he sows broadcast this twaddle :
“ Somehow, the bigger the deposit the greater the con-
centrative ability of the one big mine to rob the forma-
tions surrounding it of their metallic contents.” “ Some-
how” is decidedly good and about explains “D.W.D.’s”
knowledge of what he is writing about. Somehow, the
one big mine has not had the concentrat.ive ability to rob
the surrounding country of its metallic contents, nor does
it seem likely that it lias robbed anything or anybody.
An extensive zone of schists lying on a platform of red
sandstones and conglomerates contains the ore-basins,
and where they are there they are. The big Lyell mine
is one of these deposits, and the N. Lyell another, but
“D.W.D.” would infer that although a mile of country,
hills and valleys intervene between these deposits, the.
one has been partly derived from the other. Again, in
referring to the N. Lyell co., “D.W.D.” states he saw
“ the alleged deposit.” He must surely mean he saw it
from one of the hotels in the township, for if he examined
the mine he could not write such balderdash. There is an
ore body in the N. Lyell, and one of undoubted extent and
phenomenal richness, as anyone in the district can verify.
Again “ D.W.D.” —“ the Lyell pup crowd call a lode, and
which, in the majority of cases, is a floater, as broad as it is
long, leached from a big body. ” Now, will “D.W. D. ” tell me
where he arrives at this bit of tall presumption ? Has the
big Lyell mine been leached from the North Lyell mine,
or vice versa, or what ? Or is it from some other big un-
discovered ore body, for, if so, it might pay “ D.W.D.” to
drop writing about it and go and peg the bonanza out.
Now, a few statements of fact in conclusion : First, the
new find of ore on the surface was made in Oct., ’97, and
in Feb., ’97—eight months prior—the deviation of a tun-
nel was made in that direction, and is now within 120 ft.
of the ore body, and has not been bent many degrees out
of its course, as “D.W.D.” states. Also, a tunnel at a
much lower level has been continuously in progress since
Dec., ’96, in a direct line for the new find and perfectly
straight; therefore “D.W.D.’s” remarks about the N.
Lyell workings are about as truthful as his theories on the
occurrence of the metalliferous deposits are ridiculous.
—H.

Chief Justice Way was married at Halin-
dorf on Easter Monday. Mrs. Blue had kept
school there since her husband’s death, and the
folk in the little German town knew all about the
coming catastrophe for weeks before the news
reached the city.

Chief Justice Way will be Acting-Governor
for six months during Buxton’s absence on leave
in England at the close of this year, so that his
newly-made wife will soon realise her social
ambitions.

and Ranfurly, attended St. James’s Church, Syd-
ney, and prayed side by side to the edification of
an admiring crowd. Australian Governors, look-

regular court to some ecclesiastical shrine. Dar-

without fail, the Pecksniffian profligate adding

Darling was probably the meanest scamp that
Edinboro’ Uneevairsity and a’m a Fellow of the

J. Murphy : “Passing a South Brisbane
Chinese fruit-shop on St. Patrick’s day was
astonished to hear a rapid rendering of ‘ Kil-
larney ’on (ye gods) a Chinese fiddle. Curiously
entering, I found the artist to be a half-caste girl,

render ‘ Sister Mary Jane’s top note ’ at the end,
there entered the slattern mother. ‘ Arrah, don’t
be wasting time wid it. Play “Wearing of the
Green ” for the gentleman—mebbe he’ll buy you
some lollies. ’ I hastily hid my bit of green and
cleared out. St. Pat. was always more or less
legendary. Perhaps he was a Chinee.”

Something quite novel in the way of wed-
ding-reports comes from the West. When Alison
Smith, the railway engineer, was coupled to
‘ Cissie, daughter of the late Mr. John Robertson,
of Tasmania, and niece of Mr. T. N. Fitzgerald,
the eminent surgeon,” at Albury, four-fifths of a
column in the newest morning paper was given to
the event; but the whole of this, less seven lines,
was taken up with a description, not of dresses
and presents, but of Alison D. Smith’s pedigree
and achievements !
* * * *
Once more a jury has shown the Judge
that he is not the whole court. In an assault-
with-intent case at the recent sittings of Walgett,
N.S.W., Quarter Sessions, the eternal Judge
Docker summed-up strongly against the prisoner
--in fact, he practically told the jury to find a ver-
dict of guilty; but the jury, after a short retire-
ment, found “ not guilty.” His Honor remarked
that it was a most extraordinary verdict, but it
wa.s the jury’s. “Now,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “ has a Judge power to in-
sult a jury simply because it doesn’t endorse
his view of a case? And if a juryman resents
said insult can the Judge legally send him up for
contempt? Most bush juries think a Judge can
hang them if they say boo.” [The Judge has no
business to make any remark whatever upon the
finding of a jury, and is legally powerless to re-
sent any well-chosen back-talk which the jury
may choose to give him. The Bulletin has
Jong looked forward to the time when a jury-
foreman, after some Dockerian or Windeyeresque
remarks from the bench about a verdict, will flatly
tell the Judge to mind his own business. —Ed. B.]

The parsons and others who have recently
been mouthing over the woman-missionary ques-
tion are all beside the point. Woman is probably
as good a manufacturer of surfaee-Christianity as
man, but she is subject to, and has repeatedly” un-

disgusting outrage of which man is not susceptible.
But possibly this is a view of the case which
doesn’t much concern the members of the Third
Sex.
* * * *
Strange that John Chinaman should esteem
European women so highly in Australia when he
thinks so little of his own feminine belongings at
home. Ask a Chinaman (in China) how many
children he has, and he will give you the number
of his 6c ys— daughters don’t count. On the boats
in the Canton river, a traveller will notice that the
boys playing on deck are tied to a block of wood—
girls not so. This is done in case any of the male
progeny fall overboard. As to the female infants,
they are not considered worth the trouble of fish-
ing out again.

The clergy quite discountenanced the Salvation

« » * *
H. : “A Melb. Salvation A.rmy female
officer has* just been airing her grievances in the
press. She says Herbert Booth discharged her
because of her ill-health, and that there had been
a persistent endeavor to drive her out by sending
her to hard stations in N.S.W. There may be two
sides to her case, but, nevertheless, it raises the
general question—what is to become of Salvation
officers, especially women, when they get old,
inactive, and unattractive? The Army has been
like a friendly society which presumes that every-
one is to be young for ever. Very well in the
time of health and vigor of a new thing, but there
is absolutely no supernumerary provision ; all is
hand-to-mouth. Head-quarters is said to be rich,
but the money won’t go that way.”

The great police - art of identification !
Lately, some kind of offence was reported to the
police, the alleged offender being “ a
youth. Thereupon the police went forth
and scruffed a youth into custody, and
sent for the prosecutor to identify him.
But they recognised that it wasn’t quite
a fair thing to show only the one sus-
pect, so, by way of giving the accused a
chance, they put an aged lunatic on one
side of him and an aged vagrant on the
other, and asked the plaintiff which
of the three he suspected. He im-
mediately spotted the 18-year-old
boy in the centre; in the cus-
tomary police-language, “he
picked the prisoner out
of a crowd of men at
wanst, y’r anner.”
As the prosecutor
was solid on the fact
that it was a youth
he was after, the
fact that he didn’t
fasten upon either
the aged vagrant or
the lunatic is per-
haps not so very surprising after all, provided a
fellow ties a wet towel round his head, and re-
freshes his intellect with strong coffee, and thinks
over it hard.

* * * *
The filthiest town in Tas., next to Hobart,
is Beaconsfield. There is no system of drainage
there, and the backyards are fever-traps for the
unwary. The hospital is cram-jammed with
typhoid patients ; and solid stinks walk the streets
and jostle you off the footpaths. Once a month a
slight whiff of sweeter surroundings comes along
and nearly kills everyone in the place. This is
when the scented Tasmania directors drive down
m their gilded coach for the little cake of gold.

M. HENRI GILBERT, a French pedestrian who
has walked 23,220 miles in 37 months.
Photo., Bond & Co., Adelaide,
A striking example of an enduring soul in
a not very substantial casing is the French jour-
nalist, Gilbert, who is now heading for Brisbane
on his “ walk round the world ” for a <£lo,ooo
wager, to which amount he expects to add another
<£lo,ooo by a book about his journey. Gilbert, a
slight, sandy man of sft. 4in., covered 48 miles on
his last day’s walk into Adelaide, and had on one
occasion during his tramp from Albany been 56
hours without water. In the three years since
his start he has worn out nine pairs of boots in
covering 23,220 miles, and he has still over two
years in which to complete the stipulated distance
of 41,500 miles. His record walk so far has been
41 miles in 11 hours.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 12, 1898.
Mv Dear Mtee—
A sunset extraordinary flashed out over this city
last week. No color was allowed in that was not.
wearing the brightest tints of its own order, and a
mighty army of fiery clouds marched over the:
skies in sensational raiment, with all lights lit
and all flags flying. Homeward going harbor-
boats were rocked to one side with the rush of
passengers to the western windows. And if you
think a sunset belongs more fittingly to the
chronicles of passing shadows, let me tell you that
this one was more than a sunset. It was a deep-
laid scheme of the gods to work up a renaissance
of art and literature in this stolid city. Watch
the poets’ corners for a while.
In shows terrestrial, the Agricultural was another
large illumination. Opening day knocked Brum-
bies and Smart People together with a scriptural
disregard of person. What I like best about this
particular kind of show is that the people who
know everything everywhere else, don’t know so
very much here. I like to see the Glass of Fashion
gazing awkwardly and stupidly where the shy-eyed
man from Alligator Swamp, whose mouth won’t
shut for God or man, has the light of supreme
understanding in his eye.
City and country together sent out a big crowd,
and all the bargain-sales of the century were
represented in the gowning. It is a strange and
subtle thing that the best-dressed squatteress in
the country cannot quite get the squatteress out
of her clothes. Other people’s raiment seemed to
remember that Randwick was at hand, and the
very special was not forthcoming. Mrs. Lassetter
was in a pretty pale-fawn costume, the silk bodice
tucked all over, sleeves tucked too. (I suppose
you know that to go jusqu’au comblc in fashion to-
day you must mount a ladder of tucks—nothing
else will admit to the summit). Mrs. Rennie’s dress
was a pleasing green, with the hand of the master-
builder in its mechanism. Green has made the
biggest bid for perennial popularity among colors.
London fashion-leaders long ago decided that it
should be their most favored hue, and set it aside
on a special shelf, where fashion’s changes never
affect it. Mrs. M!at. Mayoress was wearing
voluminous black, with something bright in her
bonnet which I can’t quite remember, but which
you mustn’t think was a bee. On the whole, the
black-and-white women were the smartest, a con-
clusion one generally comes to wherever one is.
Harry Hyndes, in grey alpaca, commonly conse-
crated to the slim, and the beautiful Hon. Alex-
ander, Ranfurly’s aide, were the chief items of
interest among the men.
The ladies’ jumping contt* , kept many people
in the. grand-stand all the afternoon, but the
horses went too slowly for my fancy. And the
riders wore haggard expressions, as though the
prize they might or might not win was the begin-
ning and end of life to them. Mrs. Stacy from
Walcha had an ugly-looking fall, and it was
an excitement to the crowd to see Lady
Ranfurly racing across the course with the
Mayoress gasping at her heels (for Lady Ran-
furly runs faster than any aldermanic lady
can ever hope to run). The Maoriland poten-
tate has made herself a golden name among
broken legs and scalded feet, and the true light of
a first-aid was in her eye when Mrs. Stacy was
seen kneeling in the middle of the field. The
accident came to nothing but a shaking, and Lady
Ranfurly led the thrown one away by the
arm. By the way, judges of fair equestriennes
will change places with any other judges who
offer. Their lot has no beer and skittles whatever,
and stone-breaking isn’t in it with giving a prize
to one lady when five others stand by and slang
you with their eyes.
Then there were the dogs, and if the rest of the
show was a little languid, life in the dogs’-house
was as lively as a French comedy. The kennel-
folk had arranged a grand choral ode, and I could
see in their eye it was about Federation, and the
rise in boots, and the run on dog-skin. The tenor
was clad lightly in spots, and when he was not
catching imaginary fleas, could give, all other
tenors points. The dramatic soprano, who was
one foot long, wore white fluff and a prize ribbon,
and went a mile above the upper C She was still
ascending when I left.
The Rev. Carr Smith closed up all holes in his
Passion Play the same night, by giving away its
funds to the widows and orphans of the Dudley
colliery miners.
But Walter Bentley, expounding “ The Cruci-
fixion” with fearful melodramatic vigor, was
really the best thing Easter offered. He lightened
his discourse with poetry, and I cannot die in
peace until I know if those poems were written by
Talmage. The only other nocent gas-spout who
could have loosened verse like that is away at
Rookwood—but only temporarily, alas ! You
must understand that I now speak of him purely
and solely in his poetic incapacity.
“For one night only ” crowded Her Majesty’s
everywhere. So heavy was the air with a Sabba-
tarian odor of peppermints, and sweet-williams,
and new suits, that the. few opera-cloaks in the
dress-circle had a blasphemous air I have never
noticed about opera-cloaks before. The house
was in a strictly religious mood, and refrained
from cheering the picture of the Crucifixion, but
I am bound to record a few hand-claps when
Judas Iscariot was thrown upon the canvas. The
Crucifixion was just as realistic as limelight will
allow, and handkerchiefs came into great play
suddenly. I did not look at it myself, but heard
Bentley in the wings describing, “ Christ, all run-
ning down with blood.”
Bentley talked better about himself than about
Christ. A genuine ring was in his voice when he
told I i >w those who had said certain things of him
(Bi d'' y) were liars, and those who believed the
liars were fools. It struck me that his enthusiasm
about Christ was of the stage, stagey, and yet I
heard a lady of Israel declare she was “almost
f>ersuaded.” Perhaps another Passion Play would
win her over altogether, but I would rather she
remained in Israel. That price is too high.
Alyce Leitch, singing in evening-dress before a
depiction of the Burial, made the hit of the even-
ing with Too late; ye cannot enter now.” Her
low notes were rich and true, and, though she was
Hat m the upper ones, she put a heavy dash of
oolor into the song that brought the house down.
Her rendering of “ He Wipes the Tear ” was flat
and so-so, but who would not have been flat with
that great picture of Ludwig’s Calvary in the back-
ground? Francie Adler sang an Ave Maria of
Leon Carons composing that one would like to
hear again.
Saturday at Randwick was a masculine day en-
“reiy> ,ut Monday was a blaze of sartorial beauty
that half-a-dozen showers could not put out. Rain
threatened m the morning, but nobody minded,
and wet-weather clothes were not disturbed to any
great extent. It is long since such a host of nice
iroeks have gone out to Randwick together. The
most particidar dress on the lawn was Mrs. Willie
Mitchells heliotrope silk, with tucks running
round and round her from the hem of her skirts
to her shoulders. Turquoise blue at her waist,
and a small, round yoke of turquoise, was very
harmonious when you had educated yourself to
the harmony. A hat trimmed heavily with white
lilac, with a white osprey, waving*
wide flags over crown and brim, topped this much-
noted costume. Pretty Mrs. Alfred Milson mixed
two shades of green rather subtly in her deep-
green velvet moujik and light-green floral skirt,
and wore a purple hat as to the manner born.
Warm purples, with more blue than red in them,
were popular. Mrs. Burdekin and Mrs. Harry
Levien were both fair enough to wear them be-
comingly.
When the rain came on, I studied the subject of
how to hold up a skirt in the way it should go.
And I learnt that the one fatal thing that Society
cannot forgive this year, because it did it itself
last, is to grasp your skirt at the side and pull
it round out of the mud. You must pick it up in
your fingers just half-way down the middle seam
tvt u e k ac k> then smartly elevate your hand.
.Nothing more. Oh, yes—cracked fingers and
wrist-ache extraordinary, and lots of mud on your
skirt, but you must do it, or you won’t be let in.
The vice-regal box held Lady Rupert Clarke’s
green velvet togue, and smart-braided black-and-
white frock, brightened with an original red belt
and red parasol. The Misses Brand wore sister
costumes of soothing blue, and very youthful
white felt-hats. Maud Jeffries was in braided
navy serge, loose hair, and a floppy tuscan hat
that shaded her beauty, as it were. 1 asked, but
could not find out, who it was that wore the
brightest blue-coat and skirt trimmed with the
brightest green that ever was seen on sea or
land. Another lady, whom nobody knew, was a
warm-faced fourteen-stoner, in marmalade silk
flounced from head to foot with bile-bean chiffon.
The parasol carried out the scheme of flounces
m pink satin, but her toque took a way of its own
among red and yellow velvet and purple
geraniums.
The cosy Cri. is itself again with the Cartwright
co. on the boards. The old Brough and Bouci-
cault sparkle has returned to drive away the
memory of equine performances and other desecra-
tions of this pleasing comedy-haunt. The brief
glimpse of Beatrice Lamb in the curtain-raiser
revealed a large, fair lady with a calm, kind style
of beauty that was an exact match for her
black satin gown, white fichu and bunch of violets.
Later on this calm, kind expression is borne out by
the fact that all the dresses of the piece fall to
Miss Morley’s back, and the leading lady
gets nothing but the one black satin afore-
said. Miss Morley changes her dresses everv
act, a feat that always tells. The wedding-dress
was a white satin structure with paniers looped
over a much-embroidered petticoat, and a huge
looped train that behaved admirably and never
once got in its wearer’s way, or perhaps I ought to
say that its owner never once got in its way, for
Miss Morley manages her long series of trains with
a diplomacy of a Maohiavelli. A white wig whose
cunning curls flopped round the front of her neck
didn’t interfere with the adage that only dark-
eyed women should wear white hair. The rose-
pink silk in Act 11. was a billowy thing of frills,
and paniers, and festoons of roses, and brocaded
train. These costumes were all modelled on the
same lines—paniers, petticoat, train, elbow-sleeves.
Changes of color let in the variety. The men’s
clothes make themselves heard more than once,
but ask for a leisured mention of their own.
Noni Rickards, who only appears in the opening
piece, makes you think of Harry Rickards toned
down and cut-away, and put into his daughter’s
clothes. Pa was leaning over the back of the d.c.
one night, hanging on her looks as if she were the
apple of his eye, as rumor says she is.
The first night of the “Silver King” saw a
highly-decorated circle at Her Majesty’s. Wilson
Barrett looked to have taken Lent seriously, and
was much slimmer than one anticipated in the
light suit and overcoat of the drunk - scene.
Maud Jeffries scored heaviest in widow’s-weeds,
when her largeness seemed to fall away from her
mysteriously. The dove-grey frilly frock of the
last act transformed her into the Gibson girl at
whose abundant feet the world is still worshipping
loudly. Miss M'Carthy, as the light-colored
Olive, quite put away the family likeness between
herself and the star.
Though ghost yarns are a bit off my beat, there
is a flavor of genuineness about the following
which might make it “go.” A Melb. girl, who
married and went to live in one of the suburbs of
a big M.L. town, awoke one night during her
second week of residence there, and saw through
the open door of the dressing-room the figure of a
tall man, whom she naturally supposed to be a
burglar, and whose face, in profile, she could
clearly see by the moonlight. She roused her
husband, and he, to prevent the intruder escaping,
entered the dressing-room by the door leading into
the passage, but found nobody in the room.
Nevertheless, so sure was the lady about what she
had seen, that next day the policeman on the beat
had a full description of the midnight visitor
given him. A few days later, when the lady was
paying an afternoon call, her attention was caught
by a large wall-portrait of a man in a military
uniform. “ Excuse me,” she said, excitedly, to
her hostess, “ but that picture has exactly the face
of the man I saw in my dressing-room the other
night.” The hostess looked startled. “Why,”
said she, “ that’s a portrait of Capt. S , who
blew his brains out some 18 months ago in the
house you are now living in.”
In the recent much-discussed Maoriland
skin-grafting case, Ethel Mere Tako Baker,
r °oi™i herdoctor for £5OO damages, is daughter
of Shirley Baker, of Tongan fame. He was
backstairs leader of the great missionary wars
which waged so fiercely there some years ago. A
Wesleyan clergyman himself once, he started
a new church in Tonga, and fought against
his brethren down there tooth and nail, for the
supremacy of old King George ; and for years the
name of Shirley Baker has been a nightmare to
Sydney Methodists. Now his daughter sues for
skin taken without her permission, and, poor girl,
loses the suit as well as the skin. Most unjustly,
people think.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
‘‘Wild rumors are afloat re the dot of Mel-
bourne s latest fashionable bride. Although papa
is rich in racehorses and mines it is not at all
likely that the pretty bride’s marriage-portion
came within coo-ee of the £.30,000 or £40,000
assigned her by sassiety paragraphists. Her show
of presents, however, beat all recent records in
number and costliness, and the atmosphere there-
about was heavy with eyes of envy—not to men-
tion hatred and malice. Much of the jealousy, if
analysed, would be found to arise from the fact
that the bridegroom (one of the most popular
men about town) has in his time been responsible
for many heart-flutters. His hair-breadth escapes
from matrimony reflect particular credit on the
petite sportswoman with the elegant hands and
feet who finally bagged the game. Talking of
the lavish and well-managed wedding breakfast,
oven generosity has its drawbacks when some
amongst the guests happen to ‘ lose count ’ (like
Sir George Orreyed, of ‘ Second Mrs. Tanqueray’)
in drinking the bride’s health.
When Bari tone Rebottaro warbled at a recent
Salon ‘ afternoon,’ his airs and graces set all the
girls a-giggling. The vivacious vocalist appar-
ently rather relished the innocent raillery, judging
by the quizzical glances he cast, en chantant, at
the more aggressive titterers. With Napoleon
Boffard away’ on his travels, Mr. Rebottaro is the
dressiest’ singer we have. Such tight little
curls, and every shape of hat you could mention
from a topper downwards, all worn rakishly
askew ! When the absent Boffard is billed for a
concert, his fellow-performers, so they say, take
quite an interest in his preliminary toilette-titifi-
cations. I couldn’t tell you half the things that
come out of that little black bag to plume M’sieu’s
feathers and prettify his plump charms generally.
“ The death .of the bride’s ailing father was the
sequel to that weddingpostponed in the first place
by her only brother’s fatal accident. The tragedy
recalls a delayed St. Kilda marriage of times
agone. The bridegroom, a well-known professional
man, while waiting for bis best-man, worked off
his restlessness by scaling the roof of his mother’s
three-storeyed house to solve the secret of a leak-
ing slate. Down he tumbled, broke his fall on a
greenhouse, and came to earth clear-headed but
helpless and paralysed. As soon as the bride
knew she came in her carriage, took him to
church, married him, conveyed him home and
nursed him back to perfect health.
“On Easter Sunday at the Bijou, the Vic.
Operatic amateurs, entering on their week of
‘Maritana ’ in aid of the Children’s Hospital,
slunk on to the stage for the opening chorus as
listlessly as so many truants lagging unwillingly
to school. Non-professionals lack lamentably in
briskness and for that reason make lumpish stage-
peasants. A short skirt only looks jaunty on a
wearer bristling with life and go, and beyond re-
proach in the items of ankle and shoe-leather. In
subsequent fdte toilettes, the quondam dowdy
rustics shone out as decidedly pretty girls. Trains
wigs, mantles, etc., cover a multitude of deport-
mental sins, but unless a lass twinkles with
vivacity from eyebrow to heel, it’s no good un-
veiling yards of inert anatomy.
Sweet-voiced Miss Chapman (handicapped by
an ineffective mustard-and-black gitana frock, its
waist bunched up with a brick-lined scarf, while
a saddle-shaped cap flattened down her sleek dark
hair) made an anxious, angular Maritana, years
older than her own everyday self. Why on earth
wasn't all that black hair tumbling over
her shoulders, covered by a sequined cap set
saucily askew? And where was the bright-
colored bolero, the floating gauzy scarf, the
armlets and anklets, anything and every-
thing to drift, billow, jingle —in a word
to generally disguise and tone down amateurish
stiffness and angularity ? In the prison-scene,
Maritana as a bride was still over trim and
prim, in white satin and passementerie, but at the
eleventh hour she gave her looks fair play and ap-
peared to advantage saying her prayers in a rich
sheeny orange robe. The part of Lazarillo found
a dainty exponent in Nina Osborne of the ex-
ceptionable anatomy.

“Premier Forrest, when in Melbourne, always
got his back up when the dowdy daughters of
other Westralian delegates told him they meant
to return West by the Lloyd line, because of its
convenient calling at Fremantle. However, if a
course of sauer-kraut on the Barbarossa was
ordered by his doctor to-morrow, more than likely
‘ John Bull ’ would * happen to be there.’
“ Talking of the West, wine-grapes, you know,
have grown there from time immemorial. In con-
sequence of this, maybe, local society, I hear, got
into the way of overdoing the sherry in its trifles
and pudding-sauces. To this day, no one raises
an eyebrow when dames and dowagers, seemingly
in rude health, summon lady friends to pour out
tea and hunt the slavey while they themselves
take to bed for a few days at a stretch.
“ Miss Mac——, a young Melb. lady wtio was a
very active missioner, and most zealous in pursuit
of the unregenerate Chow, has retired from the
good work in disgust. In her class there was a
particular Wun Lung who promised remarkably
well, and was being drawn carefully into the
straight and narrow way, when suddenly bis
teacher was disconcerted by the advent, the other
Wednesday night, of a tousled young female with
a black eye, and the unmistakable smoked odour
of everything Chinese. This riotous creature
loudly abused the mission lady and raged in the
restraining arms of a startled elder, clamoring for
the blood of Miss Mac ,whom she accused of
attempting to lure away her beloved John. The
fearful things she said led to the removal of Miss
Mac in shrill hysterics, and that young lady
no longer teaches the heathen ; her pa says she is
to attend Chow-class no more.
“ Musicians, either high-class oi popular, seem
to be dead to the slightest sense of humor. At a
swell wedding in Melb. the other day, the million-
aire parent of the bride had a brass band dis-
coursing popular airs on the lawn during the
reception and breakfast, but directly the happy
couple stepped out in travelling attire to drive off
for their honeymoon, the unthinking bandmaster
tapped his stand and started the suggestive ditty,
‘ I ain’t agoin’ to tell ’! Luckily the whole com-
pany was unanimously Scotch, and hardly any-
body noticed it.

circle, were cold-shouldered so shamelessly that
had onlookers chanced to have a cracker left over
from last Guy Faux season wild horses couldn’t

snobocracy.

a R° by the twin-daughters of the marquis will not

who married young Tom Brassey, and was out
here a year ago, was, like brother Dick, thin,
angular, bony and. aruemic. The mind of the
heir of the Neville family, the Earl of Lewes, is
permanently deranged. An unfortunate family
altogether.

A former Sydney quarantine detenu makes
the astounding assertion that, so far from any
pains being taken at that establishment to sterilise
the vaccination-instruments, he has seen an in-
strument used on one person while actually wet
with the blood of another !! ! Inexpressibly
monstrous, if true, and the most grotesque mix-
ture of science and savagery ever heard of even in
the land of the convict system. Before accepting
this story, however, The Bulletin would like to
hear it sworn to on a cartload of Bibles. The tele-
phone which has been recently grudgingly conceded
to the quarantine establishment has to be used, by
detained passengers, in accordance with the pre-
vailing prison-tradition, within ken of the superin-
tendent ! At this rate, Sydney Health Board is
apparently anxious to divide the honors for in-
capacity and wrong-headedness with the dodder-
ing old Marine Board.

“ Alphi ” to The Bulletin :
We have to stand some lively horrors in W.A., but the
present state of things at Fremantle cemetery is too
much even for the three S’s. That horrible repository is so
crammed with corruption that the grave-digger has to
probe around with a long iron rod in search of a compara-
tively unoccupied spot—and, even then, as many as
seven graves have been dug before space was found into
which to squeeze the new arrival ! The old bones, also,
which have thus continually to make room for the new,
are planted away again in a casual kind of way “ beneath
the cemetery walls, or in any convenient place.” Thus
are the older slumberers hauled unceremoniously out of
bed, after a temporary rest, to make room for other
wearied sleepers, and dumped away again, higgledy-
piggledy, with nothing for a record of identity but the
memory of a man who has been at this gruesome game
for 16 years. No records are kept 1

where one of the officers, in after-dinner incaution
at. the Governor’s table, let slip a hint. It was
acted on so promptly that, when the expedition
got to Perim next day, the meteor-flag was already
in/possession.

* * * *
At Warrnambool (Vic.), the other day, a
woman who had attempted to drown herself by
jumping from the pier into the sea was rescued
with difficulty. She was brought before the P.M.
privately and related how, a year ago, she had
taken her three children to join her husband in
Westralia. There two of them died of typhoid,
and on her return to Warrnambool the third died,
also of typhoid. The P.M. silently let her go
with her husband.

Nothing could be more futile than N.S. W.
Postmaster-General’s long-ahead caution to the
public against Christmas-boxing letter-carriers.
The most inclined to give are the least inclined to
talk about it, and if the postman can’t manage to
keep his half of the secret, he’s a much bigger fool
than The Bulletin takes him for. Mr. Joey
Cook might have saved his breath.

A simple mountaineering anecdote from
the top of Mt. Egmont, Taranaki (M.L.). A
party of climbers ascended the topmost rock of
the sleeping giant, planted thereupon a 9ft. pole,
and hauled aloft the inevitable Union Jack. A
fortnight later another party, prepared to shin up
the pole and so claim a 6ft. higher record than the
first, ascended and found that the flag had frozen
whilst blowing to the breezes; there it was em-
bedded in 4in. of icicle, the colors standing out as
in a looking-glass. Not only this, but that icicle
extended right down to the ground. All this in
the summer-time ! Doubters must not snigger,
for the party photo’d the phenomenon.

The inclemency of meteorologist Wragge.
From his Weather Guide :
To secure an offending “ blackfellow,” and thereby
teach him a lesson, tie the thumbs and little fingers
together, the hands being back to back behind him.
Women attached to a camp should be given their full
share of duty. Mere needlework, fancy-work, and such
like are, in excess, degrading and conducive to bad health.
The natural woman loves active work and exercise, and is
constitutionally fitted for carrying- moderate loads.
The “ black-gin ”is an admirable illustration. If
ever Wragge advertises for a lady travelling com-
panion he had better do so in this paper.
