* * * *
Truly they are a conscienceless crowd in
Westralia. Recently two luscious brown dots
from Japan came from Norseman to Esperance
Bay. They were on their way home to the
Chrysanthemum Land with the pile which they
had made by lending their charms to the local
miner, and they asked the bank-manager to change
their heap of notes into gold. The notes proved
to be all expired miners’ rights, and two sad
brown girls have postponed their return home
indefinitely.
* * * *
Kati-Kati : “Much has been said about
the diminished liability of young men to get
married, and there have been many wild guesses
at the cause of this miserable state of affairs. The
true reason has been hitherto overlooked. It con-
sists of the increase of old maids, and in this way:
The chief cause of marriage has been the great
fear of mice, from which women suffer when
they have no one to protect, them. As the
number of old maids has increased so has the num-
ber of cats. As cats have increased mice have
diminished: As mice have diminished, women
have become more independent of men. Being
more independent of men, they have married less,
and there has not been the same liability to get
married among men as formerly. Which was to
be proved.”
At a recent big Melb. religious
function, a heathen daily pressman
of the ordinary sort sat at the repor-
ters’ table, next to the representative
of Church Chat and Pious Bits
The latter was fervent and ostenta-
tious in his piety, rising to sing
hymns, and kneeling to pray, while
his lost brother put in the time
“writing-up.” At last the godly one
began to ranji: he had not, he re-
marked, seen any report of Rev. Mon-
keybeard’s splendid sermon the pre-
vious night. “No,” curtly responded
the other. “Well, I wouldn’t like
the Ageggs to miss it,” observed the
crow scribe, “so I’ll give it to you
for to-morrow.” The daily pressman
groaned, but sat nonchalantly doing
nothing while the Church Chat man
opened : “ You see, it was like this,
there was a young woman who lost
her virtue ” —here he paused to let it
soak in ; whereupon the bored scribe
raised his eyes for |the first time to in-
quire blandly, “ Did she advertise for
it ?.” Then the pious one dropped a
long, cadaverous jaw, slowly gathered
up his notebook, and departed to take
a seat among the anointed.
* * * *
A great question of stale buns
and State-fixed holidays and Sabbath
observance has risen in South Aus-
tralia. By Act of Parliament most
holidays in S.A. are observed on
Mondays, wherefore the parsons say
that, much as they would like to have
their bun-worries on Tuesdays, they
are practically forced to have them on
Mondays, as these contrivances rarely
pay except on holidays. But, if held
on Mondays, Sabbath labor is invol-
ved, else the buns are deadly stale.
The difficulty has already exercised
the mind of a couple of “conferences ”
and the bakers’ union, and is now pre-
sented for the prayerful consideration
of outsiders in the hope that a solu-
tion may be forthcoming.
• * * *
‘ ‘ The hospital, ” writes Lancet
to The Bulletin, “is a place where
the poor are experimented upon for
the benefit of the rich. Even the tab-
bies of the Women’s Hospital (Melb.)
only want young sawbones to spoil
common earthenware before being en-
trusted with their porcelain.” But
why do they object to giving the fe-
male sawbones a chance to qualify her-
self to handle their porcelain ?
During a divorce-case in Sydney, while the
delinquencies of the wife, the respondent, were
being mercilessly exposed, one of the jurors, an
elderly, bald-headed man, mopped his eyes with
his beefy knuckles and suddenly burst into violent
sobbing. The Judge looked up sharply from his
notes, the witness giving evidence paused, and the
lady whose character was being pounded up
giggled till her shoulders shook. “ What ails
you?” snapped his Honor. “A man of your years
should have more self-control. These shameful
details are unfortunately too common. What ails
you?” “Toothache,” replied the juror, taking
the sausage fingers from his eyes, “ but I’m better
now, your Honor.” Then the case proceeded.
* * * *
Another man was killed in the Punt Hill
(Melb.) sewer-tunnel, the other day. The miners
had put a charge in the face, and retired to the
shaft. One of the men, M‘Gregor, remained in
the tunnel, 170 ft. from the face, waiting for the
blast to go off, and, when the charge exploded, a
lump of rock was blown straight along the whole
distance of the tunnel like a cannon-ball. This
rock struck M‘Gregor in the stomach, and he died
in a few minutes. The odds against a lump of
reef being thrown out in that way were about five
million to one, but poor M ‘Gregor happened to be
the five-millionth.
Ed. Bulletin. —Talking with one of the
smartest men of the force about John Norton’s
strictures in “ Police v. People,” he said : “ Bah !
After all, Norton knows little or nothing of the
doings of the police. Not long ago a policeman
arrested two people for assaulting him, locked
them up, brought them before a magistrate, had
them convicted, and sent to Darlinghurst prison.
Yet these two people were clearly innocent, and
were not in the vicinity at the time of the assault.
A little fuss was, however, kicked up by the
prisoners’ friends, and a little light let in on the
facts of the case. Result: A message was sent to
Darlinghurst; the two prisoners were hustled off
down to the police-office, and, while still in a flab-
bergasted condition as to whether they were to be
hanged or transported to Siberia, they signed cer-
tain documents, received £2O, and were fired out
free.” “ Robert ” gives me names, dates, and all
particulars. Was my informant telling the truth?
Kindly fetch along that Royal Commission.—
A.E.P.

Soudan officers ask a Guardsman what he would
take for some captured spears and swords ? I can
imagine the horror of the haw-haw fellow. I find
that Pursey Chirnside’s battery-gunnery-instructor
had also to milk and kill for the mansion of the

* * • *
Bendigo (Vic.) bench has just fined a
certain Cornelius Dalton £5 for being found
underneath a Presbyterian church looking up
through a crack in the floor at the stockings of the
lady worshippers. The charge was “insulting
behavior,” and the bench said very jagged things
to Cornelius about his conduct. Most people,
while fully agreeing with the bench, would prob-
ably like to add that Presbyterian churches should
be kept in better repair, and that ladies’ clothing
requires reorganisation. In Ballarat just now,
Mayor Shoppee proposes to spend .£3OO in altering
the local post-office steps, the present steps
being so steep that a lady can’t go up them
respectably in windy weather if there happens to
be a man standing on the pavement below. And
if the weather is very windy it is difficult for a
lady to be thoroughly respectable even on the
pavement; and going up a ladder into a hay-loft
is practically being an abandoned character.
There are a few women, like Lottie Moore, of Rick-
ards’ pantomime, who can turn a somersault with
such absolute respectability that there is no earthly
reason why they shouldn’t do it every Sunday in
church, but that is a matter of extraordinary
dexterity. And, even then, the reason why big
audiences roared themselves hoarse over that
somersault was the knowledge that, if the operator
had her attention distracted for a second by
putting her hands on a tack, and there was the
smallest fraction of a pause when her shoes were
going up, anything might happen. Woman’s
dress, as it is now, can only be made respectable
by hanging 12 bricks round the lower hem of the
skirt, and, if the floor of the Presbyterian church
is in disrepair, it wouldn’t be respectable even
then. Probably Cornelius Dalton will retire from
the business now; but then, just consider the
immense number of other people under the floor
who have hitherto escaped discovery.

The matter with N.S.W. Mines Depart-
ment’s bravery medals, issued to the men who
slaved after the Stockton disaster, and talked of
in connection with those who first explored the
Dudley mine after the explosion th ere, is that, so far,
no inclination is shown to award one to the man
whose bravery, let it be ever so pronounced
saves the life of a single mate. We have to wait
until a big actual disaster makes a sensation ; in
which case the handing out of the medals is more
or less a part of the hysterics of the occasion. It’s
just the same with the killed mine V’s widow and
family—-nothing is done outside a small circle of
usually poor friends unless an accident makes
half-a-dozen or more widows at the same time.
Wherefore the need of. a permanent fund, urged
here many times hitherto.

“ Roll up to the bar, boys ! I've got a
fiver to cut out to-night! ” was the sort of thing
which, in a recently-tried N.S.W. case, sufficiently
accounted to the jury for the fact that a bush-
worker, who had won over £4OO in a Melbourne
Cup sweep, hadn’t a penny of it to receive from
the landlord when the inevitable jamboree was
over. This typical “knocker-down,” when he
once started filling ’em up all round, wouldn’t
let any man turn his back on the booze without
leaving his hat as security for speedy return to
the cheque-slaughter. He didn’t have any hats
left on his hands, either.
CROSSING THE LOG.
From bank to bank of Gowrie Creek
There stretched a black-butt tree,
And for the letters once a week
There crossed a fair ladie.
It hapt that one was fishing there
With rod and line unseen,
Who saw this gentle one prepare
To walk the shores between.
From dainty feet two dainty shoes,
And creamy hose were stripp’d,
With flashing of delightful hues
Upon the log she tripp’d.
Half-way she slipped and straddled it,
Slid round, and hung below,
And as her hat and gingham quit,
She screamed and let it go ;
A sullen miss ’twas faced about,
And left the task to me
To fish some little items out,
And deck the black-butt tree.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

Of the Q. N. Bank defendants Drury is
supposed to be dead. Palmer is dead ; Morehead
and Hart are invalids ; and Webster is in Eng-
land —and the writs have not been issued yet.
Arthur Orton, the Tichborne claimant, was
buried in Paddington cemetery, London. The
Bulletin presumes this interment to be only tem-
porary, pending search for the tomb of Ananias.
In view of the persistent rumors of whilom
Q.N. Bank-manager Drury’s appearance in the
flesh “on the Continent,” will Attorney-General
Byrnes order the exhumation of the coffin of the
Man that’s Moderately Dead ?
Arthur Palmer, the latest of the front-rank
dead, adds another to the list of Australian ex-
Premiers who have slipped out by one door while
the debt-bailiff or criminal or civil summons-server
has been trying the handle of the other.
Ben Tillett remarks casually : “ The aver-
age English member of Parliament is as big a cad
as the ordinary Australian mayor.” Broadly
speaking, of course, no gentleman was ever a
mayor and no mayor was ever a gentleman.

songstress sent word at the last minute that she
was too ill to appear. The names of two sub-
stitutes were considered by the committee. That
of the rejected one, to-day, is Madame Melba.

sion duellists, G. H. Reid and Tom Slattery, and
both entered N.S.W. civil service in the same
year, 35 years ago. Now each wants to choke the
other out of public life.

When John Norton makes his threatened
attack in Parliament upon Judge Docker, he
ought to manage to do it in committee, so that
Speaker Abbott may assist. Ten years ago the
now great man made upon Mr. Docker a vitriolic
attack, which is still remembered by old hands in
. the House—and by Docker.
There are some long-dated members in
S.A. Assembly. Catt and Kingston have been
in the House for If years without a break ; John
Downer for 20 years, and Landseer for 23 years.
The last three have never changed their elec-
torates, and old Landseer has survived eight con-
secutive elections at Mount Barker.
Clever Donald Macdonald, of Melbourne
Argus, lately sent ’Ome the MS. of a book which
had cost him two years of hard work. It was
Bent at the invitation of a first-class firm of pub-
lishers, but they subsequently denied receipt of
it, nor could any trace of it be found. Hard luck,
which should make other authors more careful.

Thomas Griffiths Wain-
wright, wife and sister-poisoner,
hero of Bulwer'|: “Night and
Morning ” and vi/lain of Charles
Dickens’s graphic ‘Hunted Down,”
friend of Charles Lamb and the
elder Hazlitt, was, when trans-
ported to V.D.L., employed as
Sistrnan for some time at Hobart.
e also painted portraits—old Chief
Justice Stephen, of Sydney, owned
a Wainwright picture. The wife
of a leading Hobart doctor was to
have sat to him. She was short,
thick-set and ugly, and Wainwright
(vyho worshipped beauty and poi-
soned his sister-in-law “ because her
ankles were so thick ”) started
back when he saw her ready posed.
“ Madam,” he cried, “it is impos-
sible! Where are your legs? I
cannot paint a woman without
legs.” For this he was gaoled
and got 50 lashes, and soon after he
was employed in the hospital as
wardsman. A prisoner who had
offended him was dying, and Wain-
wright bent over him and said :
“Do you know that in an hour’s
time your soul will be in hell, and
this arm”—baring his own—“will
be up to the elbow in your carcase! ”
An infernal rascal, and an inveterate
opium-smoker. Yet he made an
“edifying” end.
The other day The Bulletin thanked
Heaven that Kernel Lassettah had resigned his
military command, and was going back to the
grocery to attend strictly to business. Alas ! this
paper’s paeans were premature. It proves that the
Kernel resigned because he had been slighted by
the Major-General in the interests of a soldier
named Smith, and that Sydney is to be bored by
the publication of endless columns of official corres-
pondence on the question. Lord help us !
Australian cable-fakers seem quite at sea
over the frequent French practice of awaiting the
convenience of convicted persons before incar-
cerating them. Re Zola, S.M. Herald jumped
to the conclusion that “ M. Zola has consequently
been released,” when the novelist had probably
failed to set foot in prison. And when Rochefort
recently went up for five days, the Herald man
was obviously puzzled to find a mob accompany-
ing him to the prison-gates fully a week after he
had been sentenced.

versity, but now physicking the Glades ville

James Edward Geake, the pious solicitor
from Daylesford, has been liberated from Mel-
bourne Gaol, and is now once more “ on his own ”
having served only a small fraction of the sen-
tences inflicted upon him and dodged nearly all
the punishment he so richly deserved. Geake was
let loose last Monday week, and Justice is still
holding her nose.
James M‘Sharry, the big arbitration vic-
tim or hero, would, if he could only get the
wrinkle-making case over, stand for a seat in Q.
Parliament. He knows and is known all over the
North, his reputation among railway workers
being particularly sound. He was, for years, a
partner with John Macrossan, the local patriot
and Australian Federalist.

Ex-Police Magistrate Beddome, who died
in Adelaide April 6, aged 81, was for 33£ years on
Adelaide bench ; this after spending 15 years as
police-court clerk. He was as straight a man as
ever sat on any bench, and even those whom he
punished had always a good word to say of him.
“ Jim ” Gordon, brother of Conventionist John
Gordon, is Beddome’s son-in-law. He retired in
’9O after making much money out of Broken Hills.
Travelling Australian to The Bulletin :
Saw the Queen drive out in Hyde Park last week —a
short, plump, motherly, well-preserved old lady : one can
see a hundred such every day in Melbourne. No fuss, no
cheering, no escort. She had not been in London for a
year, and I wondered whether my grovelsome fellow-
countrymen would hang round and gaze so casually as
did the people in the Park. The Princess of Wales tries
to remain up to traditional form, but the “ get-up ” is
obvious — and pathetic—paint, powder, padding, and wig.
And the good soul a grandmother !
Premier Kingston is unanimously voted
by Australian public men the champion nuisance
as a travelling companion. At night, when all is
quiet and some tired-out Parliamentary party is
enveloped in sleep, the S. A. Premier suddenly
bursts into “ melody.” His favorite dirge is
“Rocked in the Cradle of the Deep,” and politi-
cians aver that his rendering of that song—added
to his trumpet-like snore —will yet wreok his
Ministry.

The proposal to celebrate ’9B in Sydney
by erecting a monument to certain patriots who
were sent out to this part of the world may be all
right. But it is curious (remarks a Bulletin cor-
respondent) that in the discussions upon this
matter the name of Anthony Brady has not been
mentioned. And yet, of all the ’9B men who were
expatriated, Tony Brady, Cock of the North, was
surely the truest and most unselfish patriot. He
was tried and cast for death by Lord Norbury, the
hanging Judge, and let off with transportation.
Pining to see his young wife, he escaped from
Botany Bay in a French whaler. Arrived in Ire-
land, he found his wife married to an old enemy.
She betrayed him to the authorities. He was
tried again before old Norbury, and sentenced to
death. Once more he escaped the halter, and was
sent out to his former quarters. Lived to a great
age. When St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, was
burnt down in ’65 he was dragged from his lodg-
ings in one of the crypts beneath the church. A
grand old man, who, with his dying breath, cursed
the Sassenach and all Orange dogs.

In the halcyon days of the long-defunct
Melb. Standard, manager Jimmy Thomson (now
running Murchison Times over West) delighted
to trot theatricals of the gushy Brown-Potter type
over his evening printery, showing machines,
stereo, plant, &c., with a suave “ This-is-how-the-
modern-newspaper-is-produced ” expression on his
cherry-ripe visage. One day he was ciceroning
several ladies of a London Gaiety co. round and
ran them into the barn-like reporters’ room. Look-
ing up at the wall he saw thereon pictures of
many lurid-limbed females “ totally unfit for pub-
lication.” This so embarrassed him that he only
just saved himself by rushing out of the room and
up a flight of stairs shouting, excitedly, “ This
way for the job-printing department, ladies ! ”
Perrier brothers (Frenchmen), once squat-
ters in New England, N.S.W., quarrelled, and
agreed to divide their run. But there was no
boundary fence, and the sheep didn’t keep the con-
tract. So there was more row. And it came to
this, that the brothers used to keep watch along
the boundary line, rifle in hand. If a sheep
strayed across, the man whose land was trespassed
upon would pot it. Then he would raise his hat
to the other, make a bow and exclaim, “qui a
terme ne doit rien, Monsieur.” “ Tres bien,”
send the other, “a chaque saint sa chandelle,”
and then he would knock over a stray mutton.
But one day, one of the brothers did not turn up.
The other sent to enquire if anything was the
matter. Alas! the brother who was absent had
run out of cartridges. “ Tut! ” said Perrier, the
younger, “ why did he not say so ? ” and he sent
over a supply. Yet these asses loved each other.
When one died, the other nearly followed, from
sheer grief.
“ Scribe,” to The Bulletin :
Last night, I heard Ben Tillett for the first time. He is
not unlike Bellew to look at, but closely resembles the late
Edwin Booth, American tragedian. In a brown tweed
suit, with white neckcloth, he made me think of a young
bishop in tourist garb, or even a highly intellectual'rising
member of the House of Lords, such a one as might do
Piccadilly and Green Park arm-in-arm with Wosebew y—
but he doesn’t talk so. Nevertheless, his language is quite
that of the University-cultured type, and doubtless his
enemies point to the fastidious Robespierre, with the
strange hauteur of lordly persons who have identified
themselves with the people. However, he has distinction
without hauteur, or a trace of affectation. The real
ehivalrous Benjamin Tillett shines through. When he
•peaks, it is as Paul says, not so much that he appre-
nends, but is apprehended, seized upon, transformed into
another man, making me think of the energy, the furia
of Mark Hambourg, or Barry Sullivan in the tempest of
Hamlet, for the theatrical idea is ever present, although
in an involuntary way, so that he is convincing by force
of conviction. I declare no oratory ever moved me so
much, especially when his full soul went out upon the
wrongs of woman. No wonder the audience leapt up,
electrified. Then his rich comedy ! Indeed, there is
nothing better in the land of Lauri and Coleman than his
description of how the cheap German clerks came and
stole the English manufacturers’ secrets, and founded the
manufacturing greatness of their nation. Then how
forcefully did he urge that German progress was due to
to socialistic care for the toiler.
Charles A. Pridham, F.R.G.S., who died
the other day at Prahran (Melb.), of which he
was an old resident, was born in England in 1817
and was a fellow under-grad, at Oxford with Gov.
Bowen. He was a man of pronounced ability and
a good linguist, and during the ’4B troubles in Ger-
many he was one of the first of the war-correspon-
dents representative of Lond. Times; as such he in-
troduced the patriot Kossuth’s name and the Hun-
garian cause to English people. His Times letters,
published in volumes, giving information about
those then unknown countries, were much read.
He wrote also two large works on Ceylon and
Mauritius, and came to Victoria in ’52, practised
at the bar, represented the Argus on the gold-
fields, and became P.M. at Bet Bet. He kept to
the Western circuit during the last years of his
bar practice, and for long regularly contributed
a most interesting Melbourne letter to Hamilton
Spectator. By an early investment in Melb.
suburban land he made money, and left his family
in good circumstances. His friends remember
him as a kindly cheerful man with a great trick
of Greek quotations
A N.S.W. Minister who loudly protests
against subletting on Govt, contracts, lately per-
mitted subletting in a contract on liis own private
property. In this he contrasted badly with Bruce
Smith who, though he makes no profession of ultra-
Sooialistic virtue, expressly stipulated that there
should be no subletting in the building of his
house at Bowral.
The late David Buchanan was retained
once, in the 70’s, in a horse-stealing case at Forbes,
N.S.W. The coach broke down on the way
from Orange, and, as David was pushed
for time, he made for the nearest hotel
to borrow a saddle - horse. Mrs.
Public-house was also a passenger,
and told David that, if there
was a horse in the place, he
should have it. Now, the
stolen horse, of which the
police had charge, had been
re-stolen from them, and left
at the pub., the landlord of
which was a friend of the ac-
cused. But, as Bung had
himself started for Forbes,
Buchanan was lent the stolen
gee-gee to ride to the defence
of the thief. On the way,
Davy fell in with the police,
who actually praised his nag,
without dreaming that it was
the very animal which they
were afterwards unable to
produce. Davy accordingly
supplemented his ride with a
“ walk-over ” in the case, and
the astonishment of the land-
lord may be imagined when he
recognised Davy’s mount, and
knew what a narrow escape he
had from being “ lumbered ” as an accomplice.
A Melb. paper states that the late Arthur
Orton’s object in impersonating Roger Tichborne
was “not the baronetcy, but the inheritance of
the large family estates and <£24,000 a year.” Not
so. The fat man’s ambition in the first place was
merely to get from old Lady Tichborne a draft for
.£5OO wherewith to buy a Sydney pub. He didn’t
then dream of going to England to push his claim.
The expansion, so to speak, of the imposture was
forced upon him by circumstances. Strange people
persisted in recognising him as the “real Sir
Roger; ” he found the fraud growing bigger as it
rolled along, and when he began to perceive that
the law’s delay would enable it to roll for a long
time he made up his mind to enjoy himself until
the inevitable crash should come.

politician. Since then he has been a pressman
in the States. Returned, he was once again elected

Aboriginalities
There’s nothing like doctoring yourself.
The other day a Queensland lengthsman fell off
his tricycle and hurt his leg. His mates gathered
round and decided it was a case of sprain. Six
bottles of painkiller were lavished on the unfortu-
nate man ; same number of bottles of Jacob’s oil;
several poultices were applied—also a pint bottle
of lotion. After a week or more of this kind of
treatment, they put him on crutches and carted
him off to the doctor, who found both bones frac-
tured. Doctors be hanged! I always treat
myself!
* * * *
S.S. : “One still sees some curiously-
dressed figures in the burnt districts of Gippsland.
A returned holiday-maker tells of a family he dis-
covered living in a lean-to built against a rail
fence. The mother wore an old pink silk evening-
dress, cut extremely low, over her every-day
bodice; the father had on a worn-out Salvation
Army rig, and one small boy was in a patched
Eton suit, whilst another, a lad of 11, was clad in
skirts. But the baby was the curiosity, and the
stranger discovered that it had its head in a very
ancient lawyer’s wig. “Why, what’s this?” he
asked. “Shure,” replied the mother, proudly,
“it’s a-wee bit av a bonnet some kind body was
afther sendin’ up for the babby ! ”

Years ago, Defore the days of paddocks,
the “ Flash Jack ” was in evidence on every cattle-
station, in white moles, cabbage-tree hat, silk
neckerchief, and long spurs, and slouching on the
back of a raking brumby. He was quite a little
god in his way. One of his customs was to dis-
card the end of every plug of tobacco used. The
man who cut up the last bit was despised. Some
would throw away as much as half a plug. Many
of those poor fools to-day are worn-outs battling
on the roads, and make for the old huts where they
played euchre years ago, and search for the quids
that were then disdainfully cast on the floor.
* • * *
The ‘ ‘ whirligig of time! ” A few years ago,
writes “R.H.,” shearers roused, struck, fought,
and burnt against squatters’ rules compelling
them to shear wet sheep. To-day the squatter
often has to stop them from shearing soaking jum-
bucks. One often sees the water run out of wool-
presses ! Also, but a short while ago men were
proud to be unionists and “ flashed ” their tickets
(some would stick them in the rim of their hats);
to-day, they’ll plant ’em in their boots so’s the
other fellow shan’t see ’em and tell the boss, and
perhaps get them the sack for being alive.

An emu-story : A South Australian man
could not understand how it was he was never
able to keep a fence round his garden. When he
got up in the morning the palings were always
scattered, no matter how well he had secured
them. One night he sat up and watched that
JACK OBJECTED.
B ILL: Why not collar this old horse and put our swags on ’irn? ”
JACK: “Not me j I’d rather hump mine than pull it !”
fence, and just at dawn a dozen emus trotted over
from the plain, drew all the nails out of the
fence, and swallowed them. The same emus had
been picnicking off his fence for a month, the set-
tler being a slow-minded man.

H. O. Griffith: “No Bulletin corres-
pondent has as yet told of the jackass being the
victim of the snake. When in Rosewood scrubs,
three years ago, I killed a big carpet snake, which
I found upon opening to have swallowed two
jackasses. Only on two occasions have I seen
jackasses struggling with snakes, and in each case
the snake was not more than 2ft. long. . I have
seen a brown hawk (Hieracidea Orientalis, Schlege)
swoop down on a sft. brown snake, kill it, and
Carry it to its nest, where it was torn to pieces
and fed to the young ones. The crested hawk
(Baza Suberistata Ola) also kills snakes. One of
this kind I have seen kill a whip-snake by seizing
it just behind the head and rising, with the snake
wr igg]i n g i n vain to escape. These hawks are far
more destructive to snakes than are jackasses.
Another Australian bird I know to kill young
snakes. Upon opening a Podargus Strigoides
Lath (popularly known as the tawny-shouldered
mopoke) I found two young snakes about sJin.
long.”
* * * *
R. : “ It is true that for the first 200yds.
or so a rabbit has the heels of a hare. But, much
as bunny detests a bolt of more than this distance,
he will always make for his own particular bur-
row, no matter how far away. When a lad, I used
to block up the holes in a stretch of stone wall, and
then quest the neighboring bracken with my dog.
The rabbit would course gaily to his accustomed
hole in the wall, and, finding it blocked, would sit
simply dumbfounded for a second or two, by which
time the dog was generally settling his account.”

Alone : “ Sorry to notice ‘ Flong’s ’ reflec-
tion on the veracity of our jackass-snake experts.
‘ Elong ’ is too casual an observer and reader;
doesn’t notice that none of the—experts, I mean
—say Jack swallows his victim right off like
a worm. Jack swallows his worm dead-slow ; and
the head of it no sooner reaches the end of his
sesophagus, than it is digested off by a fluid
stronger than aqua-regia. I cordially invite
‘ Flong ’ and everybody else to notice what Jack
does immediately, everytime he alights (be it only
after flying from one branch to another) as cor-
roborative evidence of the rapidity of his diges-
tion.”

Dear B.— Apropos the yarn of a recent
correspondent about the discovery of a crayfish in
a load of suspiciously damp hay ; an old squatter
friend used to tell a companion to it. In the early
“ forties ” he settled at Lake Victoria on the
Murray, inside N.S.W. border, and between two
and three hundred miles from Adelaide, the
nearest big settlement. All his supplies came
from Adelaide, along the river bank, hauled by
teamsters, by weary stages occupying weeks on
the journey. On one occasion he ordered a keg
of rum from Adelaide, and, in overdue time, a
bullocky swore his team to a standstill and un-
loaded at the station. The man of wool invited
the man of oaths to join him in sampling the fire-
water, and the latter, though strangely loth, could
not decline, and got ready with the pannikin.
The squatter pulled out the bung, when out
hopped a live frog which staggered severely in its
jump and hic’d badly every time it croaked. The
squatter’s yarn always stopped there. — Kookoo-
burra.

Dear Bulletin.—“ Werrimee’s ” Dingo
aboriginal in a recent issue is all moonshine. It
must have been snakes and not maggots that his
dingo wrestled with in obtaining a piece of putrid
bullock-hide. Writer has watched the gaunt and
hungry one, after being driven by snow and the
want of live stock from the Cooma side, disdain-
fully pass flesh, newly killed, and whereon there
was no “human aroma.” The dingo doesn’t
“ round-up ” a flock—he goes straight at ’em :
besides, he never performs in the evening. He
invariably waits till night and very often till early
morning. Ripping sheep to give him an appetite
is not the dingo’s game. He rips for the blood
and chances the appetite, which seems to be
always keen. As to the calf-killing strategy :
well, perhaps it was “Werrimee” himself tliat
half-dozen dingoes had their eyes on.—J.G.

* * * *
Bill and Tim, “old hands,” had been
mates for forty years. They had come out in the
same ship, and stuck together ever since. At last
they were separated on the big sheep-run. Bill
was a shepherd at the head-station, while Jim was
ditto at the out-station. They corresponded regu-
larly by dilapidated English papers that had been
through many hands. Both were getting old, and
when Jim, who was a year or two the younger,
saw Bill tottering away, his shoulders bent and
his sheep-dog at his heels, he would shake his iron-
grey head: “Poor old Bill! he’s gettin’ old; I
must look after him. ” And Bill, watching Jim
start “out back” with his water-bag in one hand
and his “extry ” clothes tied up in a red “ han-
kercha ” in the other, would lean heavily on his
stick and murmur : “ Jim’s lookin’ powerful old,
and he’s gettin’ quite shaky. I’ll have to do some-
thin’ for him.” He did not dream that, as
Jim trudged along in the heat, amid dust
and flies, he was thinking of asking the boss to let
him work at the head-station, so that he might
help Bill; but his thoughts took another turn as
he suddenly remembered he had left his new,
bright billy in Bill’s hut, and taken the old one
from force of habit. It was too far to go back.
When, a few months later, Bill was found dead in
his bunk, everybody shrank from telling the news
to Jim. It fell to the lot of a young Englishman
•to take out rations. He'was a sentimental young
fellow, and felt keenly for the old David robbed of
his Jonathan. He entered the old man’s camp at
nightfall. Jim was at supper. “How’s Bill?”
were his first words. The young man stammered
out the truth, and the pannikin of tea fell with a
great splash from the palsied hand. “ Dead !
dead ! ” Jim muttered, his dim eyes following the
steam rising from the spilt tea. “My God ! I
wonder what he done with my bright billy ! ”

Childers (Q.) can boast of a newspaper
office (Isis Standard), grocery, feed-store, har-
ness shop, furniture emporium, and hotel—all
under one roof, and run “by the same editorial
staff.” The editor “points with pride to the
mammoth aggregation of business, all edited by
one central head.” The imagination of this paper
reels in contemplating the possibilities of a news-
paper-man editing a whisky-mill. Who can be
surprised at the Standard accusing Premier
Nelson of having given “10 evasive answers in
reply to 11 questions ” ; and of having solved “the
odd one by calling the other man a liar.” Also,
the Standard treats a Maryborough inkslinger to
some patent adulation :
Dyspeptic antediluvian scribbler . . . mis-directed eow-
kicks . . . crocodilian condolence . . . tenth-rate scrap-
iron spoiler . . . fearsome caligraphic convulsions . . .
literary Torquemada.

London Tit Bits prints that the nest of a
certain Australian bird weighs, on an average, a
trifle of five tons. The removal of one such nest,
it is added, involves employment of a team of
horses and manycarts.

Dear Bulletin. — He jackass-cwm-snake,
one positive assertion is worth 50 negatives. Near
Brisbane once I came upon a small boy watching
a jackass sitting on a stump. “ Don’t disturb
him,” said the small boy. “ There’s a black snake
in a hole in the stump, and Jack’s layin’ for him.”
Nearly half-an-hour passed ere the snake began
cautiously to emerge, but, before a foot of his body
was outside the hole, the kookooburra was on him,
soared straight up in the air a hundred feet or
more with the reptile, let it drop, followed it
quickly to the ground, picked it up again, and
repeated the process till the snake was very dead
indeed. Then Jack flew to a tree close by and pro-
ceeded to pull the snake to pieces and make a well-
earned breakfast. Jackasses are as bad as hawks
when they acquire a taste for chickens. I don’t
let them live near my poultry-yard. My friend,
“ Steele Rudd,” could tell M‘Cooey some yarns

about jackasses and snakes that live on “our
selection” at “Shingle Hut.”— Tramp.
* * * *
The belief that cannibalism is a thing of
the past with Australian blacks is apparently
not quite justified. At Townsville, the other day,
three blacks were arrested and charged with the
murder of a black boy at Cardwell. A piece had
been cut clean out of the carcase, and circum-
stantial evidence suggests strongly that somebody
lunched on the fragment.

Let us missionise the heathen ! Among
some classes, and in some parts of the bush, the Deity
is simply known as “ Old George ! ” During last
big drought one old lizard was heard to say to
another : “Dunno wot Old George can be thinkin’
of to leave us like this. He can’t ’ave no ’eart.”
His mate explained : “ It’s not Old George’s fault.
It’s them jackeroos of His. They’re playin' cards
somewhere, ’stead o’ doin’ their dooty.” Which
shows how firmly planted is the boss-and-man idea
in the mind of Billjim.— Wer.
* * * *
In ’9l a man was flood-bound on an islet
on Buffalo Downs (Q.). For 42 days he had noth-
ing to eat except two small fish which he managed
to catch with a bent pin. These he ate raw, then,
losing his improvised hook on a snag, dragged out
a miserable existence on grass. Sid Kidman, of
Kidman Bros., going down with cattle, happened
to camp near the spot when the flood was subsid-
ing. Some of his horses strayed to the island, and
the blackboy who crossed after them in the morn-
ing, noticing a concourse of crows, went over to
investigate, and was terrified at the sight of an
emaciated man, who could not stand or speak.
The poor wretch was driven to the station and
recovered after all. His experience stands alone
in the annals of the bush.
» * * *
Yet another correspondent :
Someone told you the other day that he’d seen a rain-
bow at night, perfectly shaped', but sails color. In 14
years in the Darling country I’ve seen four—three very
distinct, and one just traceable. In one, four years back,
the colors were all there, but faint. In the others the
colors could not be distinguished. They were all in the
west— i.e., the phenomenon was produced by a rising
moon, though there is no reason why a setting moon
should not produce similar effects. Possibly, if I had
been about as much between midnight and sunrise as I
have been in the early part of the night I should have
seen some lunar bows in the east. Certainly in this
Darling country, where, generally speaking, we get some
of the most remarkable sky effects to be had anywhere,
our most brilliant rainbow's are in that direction.
* * * *
F.C.K. : Back in the seventies a carrier
was drawing wheat from near Manilla (N.S.W.),
and had for company a small boy. One morning,
when the teamster had gone for the horses and
the youngster was cooking breakfast, a big New-
foundland dog walked up to the camp-fire holding
a dead and naked baby in his mouth by the thigh.
The lad was terribly frightened, but presently,
when the beast dropped the infant gently and re-
treated into a creek near by, the boy picked up
the little creature and put it in the teamster’s
“bunk.” When the latter came with the horses
he was astounded. There wasn’t a camp or a
house within 20 miles. To have -reported the
matter to the authorities would have meant an
inquest and a loss of time, which the teamster
couldn’t afford, so he cautioned the lad to say
nothing of the matter, and buried the body near
the waggon. Cautious inquiries by the carrier
never threw any light on the mystery, which
must now remain one of the weird secrets of the
lone Australian plain.

“ Viator” to The Bulletin :
To say that the amazing Salvation Arnq' procession, in
Melb., the other night, made me think of Barnum’s show
entering Cincinnati, is no disparagement. Barnum’s
advts. ran somewhat like this —“The great moral show.
Patronised by the clergy. We have Behemoth. On earth
there is not his like. See ‘ Book of Job.’ Our caravans
are beautifully and respectively painted with scenes from
the life, parables, and miracles of the founder of Chris-
tianity. General admission, 50c.” Barnum was head of
the procession, in his natty buggy, with his young wife,
in hat and feather, and the inevitable baby, as real as the
Manxman’s. Mr. and Mrs. Herbert Booth, without a
baby—great oversight-last night rode in the finest
landau, with the finest pair of chestnut horses, and
most imposing coachman, that the tip-top livery-
stable could furnish, and if a hired trap could
possibly equal Lord Brassey’s this would. Now, merely
as a piece of effect, I think it would have been more
appropriate for the Commandant and Commandantess to
hire the two humblest donkeys which Melb. can afford,
and ride upon them, emblematic of their great model
Of course Mr. Booth’s miserable screw of £3 10s. a week
would not, like Bishop Goe’s £4O, furnish a landau. The
self-denying soldiers, who do really pinch themselves to
provide 1 22,000 at one pop, triumphantly paid for the
landau, and gloried in it. Commandant Booth wore a
red cap. No other Australian Salvationist is allowed
to do so. Is not this the red hat of the Car
dinal? Consider what it signifies—the sacred blood
Our two most successful religious organisations,
the Catholic and Salvationist, go essentially on the same
lines. Just as Constantine’s acceptance and patronage
of the Church involved Luther’s appearance many
centuries after, thus does the secularisation of the Army
involve coming rebellion. However, meanwhile Herbert
Booth is the aspirant General, Herbert the First, suc-
cessor of their Emperor William the First. But to my
muttons, Booth’s sheep. I watched the phantasmagoria
and paraphernalia pass between Parliament House and
the Princess Theatre, standing for the world and the
flesh. Whereupon a clergyman at my elbow puts
in that the Harmy stands for the devil. Tut, tut,
man, they subscribe for other things than the baby-
linen of the teeming parson ess. Look at this tower-
ing triumphal car, which represents the Workmen’s
Metropole, with a real officer punching a real mendicant
with real bread, which he munches all along the road.
“ Why,” says the parson, “ that’s the biggest humbug of
the whole lotJ The Metropole gets all it can, but gives
absolutely nothing. It is on the principle of Cardinal
Farnese, who gave all his money to the poor, but made
them build him a gorgeous palace, and laud his good-
ness.” Thus, too, is the parson sarcastic on the shikery
tableau of the labor farm, which rumbles by. “ And this
is absolutely pagan!” he exclaims over illuminated
lorries filled with boys and girls, in white gymnasium
dress, the girls in their long hair, without hats.
These are the Bands of Love, showing how
they go through their exercises. “Not Heathenism,” 1
reply, “ but the healthy Hedonism of Grant Allen.” The
wardrobes of the second-hand dealers in theatrical clothes
were ransacked for Make-ups on the various Tableaux -
CHEWING THE QUID.
SEZ I TO 'IM : “ I won’t vote agin me conscience, and I can't afford to
vote agin me bread-and-butter; so ’e ’ands me a quid and
I votes for ’im."
cars. I know these good old props, when I see them.
For instance, the Tableau of All Nations included the
utilities of many plays. The Irishman was the per-
fect stage-man, pre-Grattan Biggs, in belltopper, frieze
coat, red waistcoat, and green knee-breeches. What the
Men in Armor signified, except that there was Bignold’s
Henry V. stock on hand, I don’t know. A number
of pious soldiers were dressed and made up as Indians,
the cortbge of a gaily-rigged-up sedan chair with nothing
in it. Another suggestion of cunning props. Had Crum-
mies’ pump been in stock, it would have been worked in,
fetching up the water of life instead of the miners work-
ing at a windlass. I don’t know whether the accessories
of a Maori drama recently produced in Melb., being on
hand, led to the dressing up of a lot of soldiers as ferocious
Maoris. Two big girls walked in full Highland garb with
an inexplicable, humorous personage, partly Highlander,
partly Maori, and partly Ally Sloper. Had his face
been blacked, I would have been sure Will Whitburn
had escaped from the Opera House. Oh, the comic
gospel ! “ Here’s the bally,” said the Princess
Theatre hands, as the Lady life-boat crew came
looming up, a dozen of them, seated in the boat, with
oars in the airs. The beauty of this party was that you
might imagine they were in tights, and they were selected
with tact for their good looks, the offices of the toilet not
being neglected. The biggest blue-bottle in the ’intment
was a monster advertising illuminated tower about the
holy tea, while the bicycles of the Army trade were adver-
tised by samples trundled in bedeckment of all the colors
of red, yellow, and blue, supposed to embody sacred
mysteries. Brass-bands and banners were furnished
galore. Seriously, the thing was overdone in a ghastly
manner, the grand principle being to rope in every pos-
sible theatrical adjunct. Corybantic religion stank.
Melb. sneered. Barnum used to cover up his elephants
and camels in the procession with gorgeous scarlet gilt-
edged cloaks reaching to the ground. Then the wild
beasts were shut up in their cages, except one toothless
old lion, which pretended to mangle an acrobat in its
open den. The Army understands all this too.
A VERY BAD SIGN.—ON THE ROAD TO AN OLD
GOLDFIELD.
OLD BATTLER : “No one’s gettin' any gold there; the field’s worked
out.”
NEW HAND : “ How d’ye know; have you been werkin' there?”
OLD BATTLER: “No; but I’ve been hang in' round the pub.
Saturday nights with the other chaps and no one
shouted.”
The sweater’s gospel: ‘ ‘ Thou shalt earn
my living by the sweat of thy brow.”
How far the “ drunk ” who’s led to roam
excels the “ drunk ” who stays at home !
Old Jack : “ Passing through a place
called Opipi (M.L.) a few months before the last
war broke out, I sold an engraved signet ring to a
Maori. I valued it, as it had belonged to my
father, but it had to go. Well, some four years
afterwards, I saw that ring in a pawnshop on
South Head Road (Oxford-street), Sydney. Went
in and bought it. Ascertained it had been pledged
by a soldier of the 18th —when in Sydney. Went
to the barracks, hunted up the red-coat and found
he had taken the ring from the finger of a Maori
whom he had bayoneted at the Gate Pah affair.
And more by token, as Pat says, that ring is in a
pawn-shop now.”
Bishop Goe, at a Melb. church meeting, on
the allurements of the Bar: “ Recently a man,
charged with being drunk and disorderly, pleaded,
by way of pointing out cause and effect, ‘ You
see, your Worships, it’s my wife; she nags, and I
drink.’ Probably that is the history of multitudes
of drunkards. She does not know how to make
fiis home a comfortable one; how to put on that
smiling, winning face ; how to produce that sweet
silvery voice, when he comes back from work, and
off he goes to the smiling landlady, who does
know how to make her place seem very attractive
to him.” Dear brethren, let ns moralise upon
this domestic matter :
Cabbage-tree : “ The
yarn goes that a man once
awaited hanging, in Beech-
worth gaol, Vic. Cobb
wouldn’t take up the
hangman. Quandary. The
prisoners were appealed
to, with £5 dangling be-
fore their noses, but all
had the fortitude to|refuse
except Sammy, the par-
ticular chum of Johnny,
the hangee. They had an
affecting final good-bye in
the cell, before going on
to the stage, and fell weep-
ing on each other’s necks.
Next afternoon, Sammy
went stealing softly out of
town. He wore Johnny’s
crabsliells’, and carried
Johnny’s other things in
a red wipe.”
H.M. : There are
certain well-known Syd-
ney spots where the de-
caying commercial dere-
licts of the boom days fore-
gather to swap lies over
past opportunities, and
wail for the dismal present
and gloomier future. They
are a frayed-looking lot,
clinging tenaciously to the
seedy belltopper and faded
frock - coat — relics of
brighter days. They haunt
the footpath watching to
waylay, “ with some sure
thing in the way of finan-
cial venture,’’any flourish-
ing acquaintance who
chances along. The pal
of former times generally
escapes by handing over
the price of a meal. Pass-
ing one of these dreary
gangs recently, the writer
accidentally dropped a
whole live sovereign. The
yellow metal clinked joy-
ously on the pavement,
and lay glistening right at
the feet of a hungry
group, whose glare at the
gladsome sound was a
study. Yet no one jumped for it. A certain
seedy and sun-yellowed dignity remained among
these fallen overdrafters and forbade them to rush
in their broken boots for the spoil.
“ Two-ales ’’—mixed English and Austra-
lian—is not so understood in some parts of Vic-
toria. Not long ago, when a traveller asked for
this compound in a Gippsland bush pub., the
landlord, after scratching his head in perplexity,
filled out two vast long-sleevers, and placed them
before the astonished customer, with the observa-
tion that he “ couldn’t see what in the divil anny
man wanted wid a second dlirink before he’d
dhrunlc the first wan ! ”
A Hawke’s Bay (M.L.) beak recently
found his own name on the charge-sheet, the case
being one of allowing a cow to wander in the
streets. He was equal to the occasion. He said
that he deeply regretted to see a person of the
accused’s high character and standing, and one
who belonged to so eminently respectable a family,
in such a painful position. Then he went on to
say that owing to the universal esteem in which
the accused was held, he would not inflict any
penalty beyond a reprimand, and before the
accusing constable had time to gather up his lower
jaw, which had dropped right on the floor, his
worship had discharged himself without costs.
The husband of a nagging spouse
Must drown his sorrows in the bowl ;
An incidental gay carouse
Provides distraction for his soul.
The winning face, the sil’vry voice
That make a married man rejoice,
He wonders where they are
Until, whilst flying round at night
In search of sweetness, also light,
He finds ’em at the Bar,
He says,
The comfortable Bar.
In rowing down the stream of life
He struck against a snag—
In other words, he got a wife
Who’s always “ on the nag,”
So, being naturally kind
And, therefore, very disinclined,
With Mary Ann to spar,
He merely breathes a gentle “D n 1”
And walks away, or takes a tram,
To Tottie at the Bar,
He says,
To Tottie at the Bar.
She nourishes his sham belief
That Mary Ann’s to blame,
She feigns to share his bogus grief
(All barmaids do the same),
And in t)je smart commercial minx
The married humbug almost thinks
He sees a guiding star.
Alackaday ! good Bishop Goe,
How little does your lordship know
Of practice at the Bar,
By Joe 1
The unjudicial Bar.
For temptress Tottie, all the while
Her “ mash ” is “ on the flute,”
Is thinking “ Men are mostly vile,
And this one seems a brute ;
To hear the creature whine and brag
Would make a blessed angel nag ”
(She lights his last cigar
And gives his hand a parting slap);
God help the wife of any chap
Who boozes at the Bar,”
She says—
Whilst cleaning up the Bar.

London, Monday Night. From
Tientsin it is reported that a Censor
jof the highest rank has presented a
memorial to the Emperor, accusing
Li Hung Chang, the Imperial Vice-
roy,of having received 1,500,000 taels
(about £250,000) in consideration of
his forwarding the designs to Russia.
Poor old Li ! He is sure to be Stripped of all his yellow
things again—even down to the very porous plaster on his back
—and will have to stop in bed the rest of his life.
Later. —A report that “Jake” Garrard
was seen jumping from the rear platform is with-
out foundation. The gas used on this occasion
was not “ Atmospheric.”

Some hints o£ value to all denominations
of the Christian church were contained in
an address as to the passing of the plate
given Yesterday by the Rev. S. G. M'Laren,
at the Presbytery of Melbourne ISiorth.
“Experience,” said the rev. gentleman,
“shows that exhortation is useless, because
so'many people are afflicted'with shortness
of memory that they forget all about it
before the plate at the door is reached,
whild others who do not forget have defects
of vision which cahse them to lose, sight of
the plate-altogether. The better way is
to Jpass thb plate directly along each pew;
well within 'range of the shortest vision,
accommodating oneself to every failing of
human nature and lingering- lovingly in
front of the absent-minded until the pur-
pose has been accomplished.”

Public Notices.
To the Members of the Australian
Mutual Provident Society.
LADIES AND GENTLEMEN-My second
term of office as a Director of your Society
will terminate at the ensuing Annual Meeting,
and I now respectfully beg to intimate that I shall
be a Candidate for one of the seats then vacant.
I shall be glad of a renewal of the confidence
which I have so long had the honor to hold, and
assuring you that no effort on my part shall be
wanting to justify a continuance of the same.—
Yours faithfully,
JOSEPH ABBOTT.
March 31st, 1898.
To the Members of the AUSTRALIAN
MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY.
LADIES AND GENTLEMEN,-I beg to in-
form you that I am a CANDIDATE for
RE-ELECTION to the Board of your Society at
the coming Annual Meeting, and, if you think
that my past services as a Director justify me in
asking for your support, I shall feel honored by
your giving me your vote. j HILL
SATURDAY, 30th APRIL, 1898.
AUCTION SALE at LISMORE, RICHMOND RIVER.
Two Dairy and Cane Farms, one mile from Billenudgee
Railway Station. 640 acres Rich Scrub Land, partly
improved ; 200 acres Splendid Scrub Land, divided by
a main road only from the foregoing property. Good
soil, well situated; must be sold ; unreserved sale;
easy terms.
W. MACKAY has been favored with instructions
from Thos. A. Powell, Esq., to sell by public auction
at his Lismore Auction Rooms, on Saturday, 30th April,
1898, at 12 o’clock, the undermentioned valuable pro-
perties, all that piece or parcel of land in the parish of
Billenudge, county of Rous, being C.P. 81.21, taken up
by T. G. Hugh, portion No. 6, and containing 640 acres
(more or less), together with all improvements thereon..
All that piece also or parcel of land in the same parish
and county, and being C.P. 81.19, taken up by W. D.
Small, containing 200 acres. These properties adjoin the
holdings of Messrs. Gerald O’Connor, P. Nelson and
Russell Dowling, and are watered by Marshall’s and
Wilson’s Creeks. The soil is of good quality, and the
property is for absolute sale. The auctioneers will be
glad to show intending purchasers over the property, or
communicate any required information. Remember date
of sale is SATURDAY, 30th APRIL, at Noon.

Haresfoot : “ In Melb. I find the stage-struck rampant,
and, amid this horde of youth, the girls are far the more
sensible in caving after a brief ‘Julietism.’ The young
fellows strutting about, under a supposed brand of the
Scot Inglis, are innumerable. Their current mistake is
overlooking the need of executive ability. The furniture
of the mind is comparatively a small thing beside the
endowments of stature, voice, form, and beauty required
in the good actor, a combination answering to the single
endowment of voice needed by the singer. The mental
inferiority of the physically-splendid Brooke has often
been pointed out. Compare him with an ardent Shake-
spearian like Hoskins, hampered by an extremely narrow
range of stage power. In Australia the stage-stricken
have not even the resources of England and America,
where managers use these fools, on the cheap, for a little
season, and cast them aside as sucked oranges.”
Melb. : “To-day I met a worthy little painter and
grainer who, 40 years ago, was ‘ Master Pole, the Austra-
lian Roscius,’ and acted Macbeth, at our Theatre Royal,
to the Lady Macbeth of Mrs. Vickery ; but Neild extin-
guished him. A lucky thing for Pole, saving him from
becoming a stick in old' age, or long before."
An In vercargill (M.L.) man was persuaded to go and see
the biograph pictures, but his disappointment was great,
as he expected to see something new. Said he : “ Mon,
ah ken weel hoo thaat’s din ; I sawt in Glesca thairty ’ear
back ; they bodies is what they ca’ marinetts ; just wee
dollies, an’ there’s yin o th’ actors ahint the blind pu’in’
the strengs."
A great “ eomique,” who infested these shores years
ago, fell out of work in due time, and disappeared. Some
alleged he was up-country wood-chopping, others said he
was shearing. He turned up six months later flush of
money, and said he had been to “ Amurrika ” “ knockin’
’em, gittin’ £5O a week at the Bella Union, me boys.” As
he “shouted” copiously every time, all the time, no-
body ventured to doubt, the tale. One day he had
rounded up a crowd : “ What’ll ye have, boys? it’s my
treat, dura'the expense.” The boys replied variously,
whisky, beer, &c. “ What are you having, Charley?”
“ Less-see, what’ll I hev, barkeep? I fergit the names o’
the drinks in this gosh-darn country.”
Per contra : “There have been many sneers about the
incompatibility of the thespic avocation with man and
wife. On the other hand, may not the going forth every
night to the show be more conducive to happiness than
the Darby and Joan of the fireside, where you get sick of
each other? Theatricals live so much in the glare that
the rifts in the matrimonial wicket come out as plainly as
those at the test-match. Yet where there is suitability
there is happiness. Especially in the variety business —
what a succession of loving couples I—for here the prob-
lem appears to be solved of giving employment to both,
instead of the familiar managerial ‘either, but not both.’
But, one fancies, it is perilous when the song-and-
dance people, who now so exude with affection behind
the scenes, get a bit older. The mischief is that the
woman ages so much more rapidly than the man. Busi-
ness—and the **ivvle—seem to urge him to ring the
changes.”
Ship’s Stewau.: “I abhor actors as passengers. Pro-
tected by much travel from sea-sickness, descending on
ships like locusts, gormandizing and quaffing as if for a
wager, ‘ jumping’ the seats of less wide-awake passengers,
striving tooth and nail for ‘ pick ’ berths, and enlivening
the voyage by falling out, chiding, fighting and playing
‘ not speaks’ with their own professional colleagues.”
There is a well-known Vic. singer whose name no
genuine native-born Australian can pronounce properly
at first try. Ida Osborne is the name. Idarosborne is
the way in which it is popularly pronounced. And not
without, reason either. No two vowels unconnected by a
consonant should I** allowed to come together in this fair
and sunny land.
The ushers at a cwtain Sydney theatre are exceedingly
well up in the art of removing rowdy persons, and, no
matter how innocent you may be, if o e of them requests
you to leave it is best to depart immediately. “Blue
me! they pass a bloke out quick and lively ’ere,” re-
marked a gentleman who had been “passed '*ut” for
merely denying that he had made a noise.
Editor Bulletin.— Wilson Barrett, “actor, author,
dramatist,” may have struck your reverential inter-
viewer as “a charming man of business,” but he cer-
tainly doesn’t strike your playgoing readers as a compe-
tent judge of his own theatrical work. One of his state-
ments is a staggerer. You report him to have said —“ I
dramatised Caine’s ‘ Manxman ’ according to my lights.
I make Philip seduce Kate ; not Kate seduce Philip, as
is the case in the novel. From my dramatic standpoint,
it would never do for the woman to seduce a man.”
Thus “author, actor, dramatist” Barrett —but how does
his play put the case ? In the first act we have Kate,
alone with her Philip, turning the conversation to the
subject of love. He asks, “ Why, what do you know of
love ? ” and she replies, “ More than you think,” or words
to that effect. Then she proceeds to speak con-
temptuously of Pete, because he is her “slave.” “A
woman loves her master," says innocent Miss
Kate —or words to that effect. And then, ere
Philip has offered an endearment, she rises from
her seat and flings her arms around the man’s
neck, with a soulful “O, Philip ! ” Which, from
the audience's dramatic standpoint, appears highly
seductive. In the second act we have Kate and Philip
discussing their al fresco amours, and she says, “ It was
all my fault—or nearly all”—or words to that effect.
The audience takes it for granted that Kate was the
leading partner in the first love transaction. They have
seen the girl volunteer the inaugural hug, and heard her
admit a 90 per cent., responsibility for the closer embrac-
ing which ensued. Mr. Barrett’s “lights” must surely
be deceptive. He merely thought he was making the
man seduce the woman. He did the thing that he never
intended to do, and has not yet discovered his mistake.
Or perhaps he is too much a man of business to remember
the details of “ The Manxman ” play, and, in that ease,
he should read up his subject before the next interviewer
comes along. As an author he suggests Percy Popjoy, of
whom Mr. Wagg said to Pendennis, “ The great fun is to
examine Popjoy about his own work, of which he doesn’t
know a word.”— Phlippant.
Melb. audiences were strangely dull to the epigrams
which bristle in the “Tree of Knowledge” dialogue.
Few points evoked a laugh. You see the smartness
wasn’t uttered by a recognised funny man; had Dot
Boucicault or Elton repeated the witticisms, were it only
“ Pass the mustard!” the house would have roared its
ribs out.
Adelaide School of Mines carries on its work in the
Exhibition Building, in the main hall of which Albani did
her singing. Instructions had been given beforehand
that the students were to make as little noise as possible;
but, as some of the members of the board of control had
loftily asked whether operations were to be interfered
with for the sake of a person who came round singing for
money, the students probably winked when th*e instruc-
tions were issued. Anyway, after the first concert, Albani
besought the chairman of the School’s council to stop the
row; and thenceforward the classes were suspended on
singing evenings. But a buzz of disapproval has followed
this step, for, somehow (partly, no doubt, through il-
liberal treatment of the choir which helped in the
oratorios), the Albani crowd didn’t achieve any great
popularity in the holy city.
Sophie Harris, the tall Adelaide girl who has made a
big name in burlesque in the States, attained the dignity
of having her shapely legs displayed in a recent Sketch.
And Sophie’s legs are useful as well as ornamental, for
she it was who, surprising a burglar in an Adelaide pub.
in dead of night, bounded from her couch and chased the
man of jemmies several blocks, eventually securing him
and giving him over to a blushful constable.

H.M. : “The humor of the lady pro. It was a yawn-
ful Sabbath after tea, and I sat on the balcony at my
hash-house, facing the room of a leading lady. She
was tall, divinely fair, and carried clusters' of glittering
jewellery on her soft white fingers. She looked as tired
as I felt, but at length an inspiration seemed to seize her.
Disappearing' for a moment, she returned with a thread
and a sixpence at the end of it. In the street
below the godly public had now begun to wend to the
neighboring church. Dangling the thread over the
balcony, the stage syren dropped the coin with a tinkle
upon the pavement just behind a holy passing-pedes-
trian. It was a study to mark the right-about turn and
anxious attention that the good folk displayed. A futile
search, and they departed feeling their pockets, appar-
ently afraid of having dropped the collection money.
The manoeuvre was successfully negotiated for some time,
until a contingent of the inevitable small boy, catching
wind of the proceedings, congregated below and boraked
the mirthful lady. She plaintively pleaded to be allowed
to continue the pastime in peace, but the city imps were
inexorable, and she temporarily retired. Stepping out
again, however, she slowly lowered the sixpence, arid, as
the eager crowd gathered underneath to rush for it, the
footlight fascinator deluged them with a jugful of cold
water, and finally retired. Altogether it was a moving
occasion ”
An old actor reminisces that, playing in Adelaide with
a big star years ago, it was noticeable that the latter, who
was doing Romeo, was drunk. At the passage “He jests
at scars who never felt a wound,” the great man lurched
against the balcony and nearly brought it and the fair
Juliet to the ground. Matters grew worse as the play
progressed, and it was excruciating to see Romeo, who
had taken the fatal drug and should have been dead, still
retaining on his face a beery leer. Impressive were the
appropriate words of Juliet. “What, here a cup closed
in my true lover’s hand? Poison I see hath been his un-
timely dnd.” Just there Romeo loudly hiccuped, and
Juliet almost collapsed. Romeo had that day assisted too
heartily at the christening of a new brewery.
Melb. : “ Dan Barry’s ‘ Kelly Gang ’ play is not quite so
good as journalist E. C. Martin’s, called ‘ Ostracised,’ and
acted at the old wooden Princess's while.the thing was
hot. Dan B. is richly up to date. He makes Ned Kelly
exclaim : ‘ What! a cannon and regiment of militia
brought up against four men ! Why, they had better get
the Federal Convention. Lord Salisbury ought to send
the whole Imperial army.’ But this goes perilously near
guying the part. Schiller makes Joan of Arc die from a
wound in battle. Similarly does Barry’s Ned expire,
after a tinned-salmon fashion, outside the Glenrowan
incinerated pub. The sentiments are neatly ladled out
to suit all tastes. For instance, after the murder of Ken-
nedy, Scanlan, and Lonergan, Byrne asks, ‘What remains
for us?” Ned: “The gallows. I will show the world
how an Australian native can die.” (Whereat a godly
explosion.) But when it comes to the last he has'a
George Barnwell tag, about the lilies and langours of vice,
and raptures and roses of virtue. Sherritt is murdered
by shots from all four of the gang, while two comic
troopers are in agonies under a bed. The fact was
that Sherritt opened the door to Byrne, who
shot him. Another constable was under the
bed. I attended the Parliamentary inquiry, pre-
sided over by ‘ Chief Baron ’ O’Hea. Supt. Hare
detailed the extraordinarj’ physical hardihood of
Sherritt. He saw Sherritt, though only in shirt-sleeves,
calmly go to sleep under a tree in pouring rain. Mrs.
Byrne, mother of the outlaw, had the keenness of a lynx.
She discovered that the police were in a particular dis-
trict only by seeing the sun shine upon a sardine-tin on a
hill half-a-mile off. Somebody ought to cooper up a
play out of the yellow-covered novel, ‘ Ned Kelly, the
Ironclad Bushranger, and Lola Montes,’ who is repre-
sented as his guardian angel.”
A Sydney church-organist was lately visited by a
woman who said she had a phenomenal voice, and was
consumed with ambition to sing an Ave Maria in church.
The sample she gave the musician on the spot was such
as to make him bow her out with all speed; but, next
Sunday evening, when he had played the congregation
out and was shutting up his organ, he was confronted by
the fair screecher with the offer of a sov. to let her fire
off a solo then and there in the empty church.
“ I have to kiss your husband in the next act. Do you
object ? ” “ Not if you don’t.”

T. H. Nott, the Melb. confectioner, who died the other
day, leaves a musical family. The eldest son has long
time played the organ at toney Christ Church, St. Kilda ;
the second son is a basso in Scots Church choir, and the
third was organist at St. Jude’s, Carlton, when a mere
boy. Since then he has played at Strong’s church and
several times in public. Quite young still, W.E. is already
a representative Australian master of the big instrument.

T.D. : “Children's concerts (id. admission) are upset-
ting the mental balance of my suburb. Any decent-sized
shed is availed of, and all the reciting and skirt-dancing
talent of the local schools, together with numerous
small-girl pianists, give services gratis. Proud parents
and admiring companions roll up in force, and the
youngster whose father owns the improvised concert-hall
gluts himself next day with fruit, ice-cream, and summer
drinks.”

Librarian: “ Turning over my favorite vols. of
London Athenasum, I have just lighted on a most sym-
pathetic par, written in Sept., 1870, re the death of
that popular young French comedian and singer, M.
Marius, in battle, outside Metz. He is regretted as a
London favorite, with a fine career before him. Somehow
Marius managed to go on with it. In the opera of
* Madame Favart,’ he fascinated Florence St. John, a
humdrum lawyer’s wife. How they used to make love
in the piece, when Marius came up through the trap!
They played in Divorce, off the stage, not only once, but
twice. That substantial ghost, Marius, came up in court
so beaming, with the freshly-blown rose in his button-
hole. But Metz came at last. Gone for ever this time.”

A certain minor theatrical manager of the malaprop
species was scornful, the other day, at the expense of an
actor who didn’t know a magnolia flower by name when
he saw it on a dinner-table. “Everybody in the pur-
feshun,” remarked the manager, “ ought to remember
the name. Have you never heard of—er—Shakespeare’s
‘ Magnolia and Galatea ’ ? ”
V. : “ The reason why tungstate, or other chemical, is
not used to make ballet dresses flame-proof, is that it
takes off the snowy gloss, gives a dead color. It is not
managers who oppose it, but the public, and the insuper-
able objection is that of the dancers themselves.”
_ Marchesi, the world-renowned song-mistress, has pub-
lished her life-story. Her career is an interesting example
of an unconscious choice of the better part. Born in Ger-
many 72 years ago, she early developed a voice, but gave
up a stage-career to please her parents. Had she shone
as a vocal star, she would now be a has-been—the
memory of a “ voice, and nothing more whereas, after
half-a-century of teaching singing, she is still easily first
in her power of imparting “ do, ra, mi.” Her method is
avowedly formed on Garcia’s, who had original ideas on
training the female voice. In her list of famous pupils
are included Calv 6, lima de Murska, Antoinette Sterling,
and Melba. The Marchesi method is the hall-mark of
success. She tells an interesting yarn of a mild wrangle
she had with Wagner, who held that the vocalist must
yield to the composer, while the lady’s theory is that the
music was made for the singer and riot the singer for the
music. “ Wagner remained true to his principle, and so,
alas! many voices have been ruined through his music. ’
Ada (Jrossley, Frances Saville, Lilian Devlin, and Nellie
Rowe are, after Melba, Madame’s front-rank Australian
pupils.

The resignation of Macdonald-Cameron —
the valorous Highlander who proposed to popu-
larise the kilt in Australia —from the Deputy-
Mastership of Sydney Mint, and the elevation
of Mr. E. H. Yon Arnheim to the post, look
like the first fruits of the visit to these shores
of Messrs. Rigg and Thompson, as representa-
tives of the London Mint authorities. Some six
months ago these officers arrived, commissioned
to visit the Mints in existence in the various pro-
vinces and to examine claims for further estab-
lishments. Several changes have been recom-
mended in Mint-management in Melbourne, espe-
cially as regards the safeguarding of bullion. Mr.
Arnheim makes the third Deputy-Master of
Sydney Mint, Mr. Ward, the first D.M., having
held the position for a couple of workin life-
times.

A CURE FOR DRUNKENNESS.
Q. Labor-member Jackson : “ Dawson
admires Byrnes, Glassey admires Tozer, Hoolan
admires Philp, and I admire Dickson.” What
Tozer, and Byrnes, and Philp, and Dickson
think of their sycophants everyone seems to know
but themselves.
Twenty-nine more Japs, (four women)
landed at Thursday Island, March 30. The Tozer-
Nelson lie about diplomatic stoppage of Jap. im-
migration is wearing very thin. Nothing but hot-
and-strong legislation will do the job—and Q.
white legislators are black inside.
Charles Morris Russell Dumas, a native of
Sydney and proprietor of the local paper, is the
Liberal and Kingstonian candidate for Agent-
General Cockburn’s seat at Mt. Barker (S.A.).
Dumas was Cockburn’s chief supporter when he
was first elected for Mt. Barker in ’B4.
It is no secret that a vote of want of confi-
dence in the N.S.W. Ministry, on the ground
that it continued to include Minister Carruthers,
was to be proposed at the opening of Parliament.
And under the circumstances (as any stick does
to beat a dog with) it might have been carried.
What the absence of compulsory education
has done for Tasmanian Democracy. Of twenty
Hobart wharf-laborers who met to form a union
the other day, only nine could read and write!
Three others could read, but had never learned to
write. Not a man of the score was over thirty-
five years of age.
Richard Butler, the new Education
Minister of the Kingston (S.A.) Govt., is a clever
but unreliable politician, and since entering the
Assembly in ’9O he has been on all sides of the
House. He is unpopular, and his promotion will
weaken the team, but he received a pledge of the
first vacancy when he became whip on the selec-
tion of O’Loughlin to fill dead Lands-Minister
Gillen’s place.
Commissioner Mathieson, in an elaborate
report, reckons that the Victorian railways may
lose £15,000 a year revenue through Federation—
he isn’t certain that they will lose even that.
Consequently the Age has given up talking about
“ the Railway Ruin” as a reason why Victoria
should stand out, and its howl, at time of writing,
is about the alleged unworkableness of the dead-
locks proviso.
The Age gave a full column to Ben Tillett’s
recent anti-Federation lecture. Any ambitious
person willing to rave at large in condemnation of
the Constitution Bill is sure of Syme’s blessing
and long reports; meanwhile, the Age leading-
columns are silent on the subject of Federation.
David has concluded that the infant Bill is not
likely to die of exposure, so he is trying the effect
of judicious neglect.
The Bulletin hears from an apparently
reliable source that in a recent political contro-
versy from the Conservative Leg. Council point of
view, waged in the columns of Melb. Argus
and supposed to show the trend and force
of public opinion in a certain direction, twenty of
the letters, out of a total of less than thirty, were
written by one man, and that man a clever paid
organiser of the Tory Party.
That fallen Cold-tea crank, G-. D. Clark,
writes to the papers to state that the Federal
Constitution will “ place the entire government of
Australia in the hands of lawyers, landowners,
and commercial men.” But poor Clark’s drink-
statistics have so wrecked his reputation that
nowadays, when he cackles wildly to show that
he has laid another great inaccuracy, the public
only shoos him off the premises.
Mahan, junior : “ Australia has to create a
navy, on the example of the United States. We
don’t want a big army. Twenty thousand does
for the U.S., and ten thousand or so will be an
ample Federal establishment, with militia. But
we must be a maritime nation. Let us undertake
the education of sailors. Peace-loving as we may
be, a smart Australian national squadron, of about
Sen ships, is needed to safeguard our coasts.”
The Federalist (?) Salomons, Q.C., con-
demns all schemes that do not recognise the Senate
as paramount. Right of taxation a sine qua non.
Sydney Daily Telegraph won’t hear of a policy
that permits the Upper House to reject a money
bill. Labor-party won’t have a Second Chamber
at all. Problem : When and how are we to have
a scheme that will reconcile the views of even two
of these sections of “ ardent Federationists? ”
Are the N.S.W. “prudent Federation-
ists” who stumped the country a few months
back all dead ? When the present readers
of Sydney D.T. remember the merciless
criticisms on L. F. Heydon, Geoffrey Eagar,
Nugent Robertson, &e., in which their journal
delighted some few months back, how they must
marvel at its sudden conversion to the “ prudent ”
view of things ! Barton, 98,000 ; Robertson, 2000!
Brisbane’s two influential dailies—Courier
and Telegraph—are in a painful state of anxiety
over the impending political crisis. Neither has
the courage to strike out for anybody or anything.
The yellow-pup Telegraph won’t swallow R.C.
Byrnes, yet cannot take Dickson neat; and the
Courier is simply waiting to see which way the
kangaroo jumps. Those mighty moulders of pub-
lic opinion will doubtless pour out oracular homi-
lies when everything is settled.
Stated on good authority that Van Dobson
is about to proceed against the Braddon Govern-
ment for having, by the negligence of its servants
(a prison-gang warder on Mt. Wellington is alleged
to have set the blaze agoing), caused the recent
destructive bush fire whereby Dobson’s mountain
chalet was demolished. If the suit is successful,
the “ responsibility of the State” will be converted
into a bogie, big enough to block any socialistic
legislation in the Isle of Dreams, for at least a
generation.
S.A. Federation campaign looked like
being a dull affair indeed, until it was announced
that Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C., intended to stump
the country against the Constitution Bill. Ward
is a peripatetic politician, with long hair and a
thick voice, and, when wound up, keeps going
like a musical-box. Ebenezer, who has provided
some of the stormiest scenes in S.A. Legislature,
can be relied upon to supply the humor of the cam-
paign, although he lias no more sense of fun than
a stone image.
Quite a large number of highly respectable
papers have risen to state that, owing to the
little scuffle which took place in Kalgoorlie, Capi-
tal will never again feel safe in Westralia, and is
going to depart from the country immediately.
But if the capital that is sunk in the half-dozen
good mines goes it must leave the mines behind it.
And the other capital is only represented by wild-
cat in a fearful state of decomposition, and any
attempt to disturb the decayed animal would be
worse than sewer-gas.
A point that illustrates the fatuousness of
Jake Garrard’s connection with the costly atmo-
spheric-gas hoax is that in his capacity as Minister
for Education he is in official touch with two re-
cognised scientific authorities on illumination—
viz., Prof. Warren, of Sydney University, and
Mr. Bruce, lecturer on sanitary engineering at
Sydney Technical College. A word to either of
these experts would in all probability have led to
Jacob’s disillusionment before the smart Yankees
had got away with the swag.
Dr. Cockburn, who resigns from the King-
ston Govt, to become S.A. Agent-General, will
find it hard to follow Playford, and Kingston
could hardly have found a man less fitted for the
billet. Cockburn has never been a business man ;
he is a long-haired dreamer with a lofty scorn for
details, and made the great mistake of his life
when he originally surrendered a splendid country
medical practice for the uncertainties and disap-
pointments of politics. S.A. General-Agency is
no longer a diplomatic billet; the occupant has to
hustle around and dispose of consignments of wine
and apples, and “bustle’’the latest shipment of
f rapes before they become mildewed. This suited
’layford, who was born on and still owns a
potato-patch.
