BELATED HOBBY: “ It is I, dear. Jusht bee'll to
mosht int’reshting free lecshure.”
ANXIOUS WIFE: “I know. ‘I and Alcohol.’
Come inside and have another.”
The Bills of the last session of N.S.W.
Parliament illustrate the relative gravitation of
the two Houses to public and private weal. Out
of 116 Bills introduced in the Assembly 102 were
public, while out of 32 Bills introduced in the
Council 13 were for private objects.
The Adelaide Labor M.P.—an ex-con-
stable, by the way—who was fined at the police-
court, in the head polling-place of his district, for
taking too much liquor, early this month, would
hold a very prominent position but for his failing.
He is clever, well-read, solidly fluent of speech,
and full of human kindness; and is always listened
to with attention in the Assembly.
A MELBOURNE CHARACTER.
Sydney D.T. is fighting the Convention
Bill with all its hands, feet, and teeth. On the
principle that no newspaper ever publishes a letter
that it doesn’t want, it prints an epistle from
“Progress” pointing out that it is proposed to
call the Federation the Australian Commonwealth
and that —
About 250 years ago a bloodthirsty ruffian, by name
Oliver Cromwell, after having planned and brought
about the murder of Charles 1., created in England that
which is known in history as the Commonwealth. The
amount of misery and bloodshed he was then guilty of
is well known to everybody in Australia who has had any
schooling at all. At the present time Australia and Tas-
mania are endeavouring to establish a grand Federation,
or, in other words, to build up a united nation out of five
colonies. Who first used the odious word “Common-
wealth” in connection with this splendid undertaking ?
Well, it was old man Parkes who chose “Com-
monwealth ” as a title.
No wonder “the great majority” of N.S.
Wales M’sP. hate Federation. For them it mean*
joining the still greater major-
ity. For them it practically
spells Obscurity, Extinction,
Now, they reign in Hell; then,
if permitted to live at all, they
would have to serve on the
door-mat or in the fowl-house.
The institution of Federation
would mean the sudden pouring
of kerosene over a whole brood
of political bugs. Fifteen out
of every twenty members of
Assembly are pitiable objects
which have crawled and re-
crawled into Parliament on
their bellies through lodge-
rooms, and town councils, and
sectarian organisations, and
other paltry and depraving
avenues —and by begging for
billets and roads-and-bridges.
Under Federation they would
be relegated to their true posi-
tions as lodge tylers and aider-
men of the deepest dye. Men
of brains big enough to have
achieved a national reputation
will then attend to the affairs
of Australia, while the brood
of King Cabbage - tree and
General Geebung will find suit-
able occupation in seeing, as
it were, that dead cats are not
allowed to remain over-long in
the street gutters.
N. : “ Why did not the federal
Convention adopt a National Flag? Say
the Southern Cross of the Eureka Stockade.”
But maybe it’s time enough to get the Flag
when we get the Nation.
N.S.W. Labor-member Hughes alleges
that the Federation party is “ praying for a war ”
to scare the Australian people into accepting the
Constitution without question. And the Pro-
vincial party, on the other hand, is praying for
cholera, and hydrophobia, and plague, and the
burning-down of Sydney and Melbourne, and the
death of the Federal leaders through hydatids
and leprosy, in order to scare people off
Federation. The Bulletin only mentions these
trifles to show that it could be quite as irrelevant
a liar as anybody else if it really wanted to.
The Kalgoorlie men who aimed the over-
ripe egg at Premier Forrest, have had their
reward. Not only has the illegal 10ft. minimum
been withdrawn, but the four imprisoned miners
have been released. They weren’t formally par-
doned, or their sentences commuted ; the Kal-
goorlie magistrate who put them in gaol simply
signed an order that they were to be fired out of
gaol again, and, in the off-hand W.A. fashion,
they were fired out accordingly. Westralian jus-
tice seems to require only two printed forms : one
reading “ put this man in,” and the other “ heave
this man out.” These seem to constitute its entire
stationery.
“ All day the smut-laden rain had fallen, ”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ and with night the snow came steadily down,
but in Fleet-street, long after midnight, the crowds
stood ankle-deep in the slush, cheering each gain
of a Council seat to the Progressive party. ’Twas
a great Unionist campaign, and they had meant
to capture London and split it up into so many
weak and straggling, easily-intimidated vestries.
Salisbury and Chamberlain had decided politics
were to be the party-ticket, and about 40 barristers
and nine peers went forth to battle for ground-rents
and general boodle. John Burns, the ‘ Black
Blister,’ was to be ousted hard and heavily, and
Earl Denbigh was selected as the * knock-outer.’
But John beat him by 1500 votes, and doubled his
own majority of the previous election. Thirteen
seats gain to the Progressives, with four aider-
men, and 10 more to be elected by the Council
should mean a comfortable working-majority of
27 —and now for a rate on the .£16,000,600 of
London ground-rents that at present pay nix ! ”
That disastrous mining tipster, the Sydney
Sunday Times, reports:—
The lowest estimate of Customs duties whieli will be
payable by residents of N.S.W. under the proposed Com-
monwealth will be 30s. per head. Thus the head of a
family consisting of himself, wife, and, say, five children
would have to pay a total, in addition to the present taxa-
tion, of ±lO 10s annually towards the cost of the Federal
Government, and very probably considerably more.
The melancholy tipster here puts up a beautiful
record for sheer, hog-like ignorance, or misrepre-
sentation, or something. In the first place,
present N.S.W. Customs taxation is about £1 per
head, therefore 30s per head would only mean £3
10s per annum, in addition to the present taxation ,
on the family of seven. And the £lO 10s per head,
as the Sunday rag ought to be fully aware, doesn’t
go towards the cost of the Federal Government, for
£7 17s 6d of it goes to the State Treasury. The
chief weapon of the N.S.W. parochial crowd
seems to consist in pointing out the imaginary
horrors in the Constitution, and asking the people
to consider the dreadful consequences of the thing
that isn’t there.

Cartwright’s return brings back memories of pansy-
eyed Olga Nethersole. That dark-eyed beauty had two
assets—tear-dim eyes and deeply-heaving breast. Leaning
forward, the sidelights would scintillate in her liquid-
brown orbs, and an imperceptible forward movement of
the olive shoulders, with gaspy inhalation, would alter-
nately throw the chest into light or shadow. Then, in
the hush, some woman’s tremulous sniff would start
everybody rubbing for “ a bit of dust that got in their
eye.” It was all in her shoulders, though, for she was
scarcely 19 at the time. Her suppression suggested more
than ever her expression could —and that is the truest
Art. Hers was the “silent system,” and, from her latest
successes, it seems that the silence has proved golden.
“Scenic-artist Walter Spong,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, “ has had two pictures accepted
by the Royal Institute of Water Colors—the R.A. of clean
paint. Both are Egyptian scenes, studies for which W.S.
made when coming from ‘ out thar.’ Pinero’s crinoline
still contains Hilda at the ‘ Court,’ and is likely to for a
long time, as ‘ Trelawny of the Wells’ has become
fashionable, and the tiny auditorium is nightly packed.”
London critics call Mark Hambourg to account for the
lack of tenderness and depth of feeling in his piano touch.
Australians found him mightily improved in this respect
between the times of his two visits here, and Mark is still
very young. His weakness just now is sport—he adores
Clem Hill and Bill Martin. As soon as his soul begins to
soar girlward Mark’s instrumental work will range from
the dreamy softness of reciprocated affection to the pas-
sionate punch of unholy desire. Give him time. Mark is
precocious only in musical talent.
S.S.: “ The Bulletin correspondent who expressed su t
prise when the people at the Princess’s laughed at Lauri’s
affected discomfort whilst sitting in a kilt, has evidently
not been patronising burlesques and variety shows
recently, or he would know that it is the season for that
kind of joke. The joke referring to underlinen has gone
out, and the child-bed joke is resting ; it is the joke on
the ‘aft’ that now knocks pit, stalls,and circle. Witness'
Coleman, who was shot in the ‘you-know-where’; the
kilt comedy, and little Ada Reeve warbling of sliding
down the stairs, and a nail scratching her ‘ where it didn’t
ought.’ ‘ Where’s the humor in these indecencies ?’asks
‘ Mac.’ It is because they are supposed to he indecencies
that they are considered funny. They would not be the
least bit comic to a naked Papuan, who had not come
under missionary influence. Society agrees to ignore the
existence of certain portions of the human form divine,
the comedian outrages the convention, and society,
acknowledging for the moment that it is a humbug, splits
its sides laughing. Society is for ever guffawing at its
own hypocrisies.”
“The Melb. cricket pictures of Stoddart’s team,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “have caught on
at the ‘ Alhambra,’ and the audience cheers lustily to see
Ranji and Hayward diffusing ‘ fourers ’ at the rate of
two a second. The snaps between balls appear to have
been left out; so the batsmen are never at rest and
Trumble suffers cruel treatment. Outside the hall,
though, cricket is stone dead, owing to the English ides
of March being so well tanned at Sydney.”
Melb. ; “The Bijou play, ‘The Tree of Knowledge,’
reminds me of a Sydney friend of mine The most estim-
able fellow ever I knew, but he fell into one indiscretion
through a mischief which befalls nearly all of us fora brief
period in life, that of possessing too much money. He
was in his shop, with his newly-made wife, when the
Woman came in. She had no legal or moral claim, and,
being one of the generous, merciful sort, she went away.
I believe he never saw her again. The innocent bride
asked ‘ Who’s that ? ’ ”
Dear Bulletin. — Re recent P.P. statement that the
Salvation Army was likely to buy Ballarat Academy of
Music, let me say that the Academy was bought the other
week for £6500 by a draper, a butcher, and a brewer, a
combination which has already been dubbed “Rags,
Bones, and Bottles.” — Vindkx.
Dear Bulletin. —By way of footnote to a query anent
Albani, you ask me whether it isn’t the “sin of tight-
lacing that finds all lady-vocalists out ? ” Perhaps ; but
one of the first things a singing teacher impresses upon a
promising pupil is the necessity for abjuring close-fitting
corsets; and, to judge by the matronly bloom of most
prominent songstresses over forty, the injunction is
generally obeyed. In the same way, the male singer
should banish all thoughts of Piccadilly collars.

The Bulletin is informed by a townsman of Durward
Lely that the tenor’s real name is James Durward Lyell.
G.B. ; “Kyrle Bellew left Melbourne in June, 1875, for
England, working his way home. He told me that, he
meant to go on the stage, and that he should make em
sit up. His father, the ‘ ladies’ parson,’ was Thackeray s
Rev. Honeyman, in ‘The Newcomes’—a good-looking,
meek man, with a plaintive, sad, soulful look, that used
to burst up the staylaces of his congregations.”
Haresfoot : “ Modern dramatists do not invent great
situations, but only stage those their predecessors were
afraid to use. How many have itched to bring on the
scene between Mrs. Tanqueray and Capt. Ardaile, her
step-daughter’s affianced ! When Pinero read to manager
Alexander, the showman protested : ‘ The public will
never stand that.’ ‘ Oh, yes, they will,’ replied Pinero.
He knew the public had grown up to it. So this parti-
cular situation is reproduced, with variations, by Cart-
wright and co. in the ‘ Tree of Knowledge.’ ”
T.D. : “ Recently, visiting male-vocalists have all fol-
lowed the rigid hirsute fashions of the profession. Ac-
cording to tradition, the tenor wears a moustaqhe of
light growth, whilst the basso must have the heaviest of
lip-adornment. Baritones used to let their beards grow,
but now they all clean-shave, and assume cupid-lips and
soul-piercing eyes. Latter-day baritones, seem to have
entirely supplanted the tenors in feminine regard.
After all, when you come to think of it, women are
beginning' to know a thing or two.
Sarah Berry, of the Albani quartette, would really be
one of the very best contraltos yet heard in Australia, but
for her stolidity and deficiency of expression—every note
of hers is pure music. But that is the common fault
among women with voices of the sympathetic contralto
quality. There are one or two contraltos in Sydney with
fame easily within their grasp if they could but let their
souls find freedom. But, then, contraltos are nearly all
very fat, and it is doubtful whether the very fat lady has
any Soul to speak of.
Hinemoa Hill, the newly-appointed conductor of Syd-
ney Liedertafel, threatens to introduce all sorts of inno-
vations. He complains of the unfortunate way in which
instruments of the bassoon and oboe type are neglected
by amateurs. The latter, however, wish to know of what
service these uncouth instruments would be on ordinary
occasions.
The Pleyel Chamber Concerts will shortly be revived
in Sydney. Stael will lead the attack, and Sydney Moss
will be sole pianist. The other performers will include
Vollmar, Hill, and Carter, so favorably known to the
musical world of the city. In acknowledgment of the
success of the last series, Pleyel Wolff and co., of Pans,
have sent out a splendid instrument to the promoters as a
reward for the advt. given to the firm.
Reported that a bevy of Jewesses, who are prominent
worshippers at Sydney Synagogue, are working up a sub-
stantial testimonial for presentation to the conductor of
the choir there. Herr Vollmar, the Dutch celloist who
trains the choir, is immensely popular with the woman-
kind of the Synagogue —though he does not presume
upon the glances thrown at him by frivolous girldom.
Snazelle writes that he is about to publish in London a
sixpennv book of his recitations which will include
Edmund Fisher’s “The Billiard Marker’s Yarn ” (“ by kind
permission of The Bulletin”). He has made the aforesaid
Yarn very popular all over the world, he says, and his
programmes have reminded the Universe at large that he
borrowed it from this journal. Snazzy otherwise speaks
of himself as a perambulating advt.. for the Sunny South.
One of the items in his monologue entertainment is
“ Ten minutes in peaceful Australia, ’ with limelight
views. For the Natio al Sunday League he has brought
Australian peacefulness under the notice of “ an aveiage
of 2000 people every Sunday since September 1; ” also, he
will shortly publish an illustrated volume of “ Tales of a
Traveller,” now appearing in Lloyd’s Newspaper at the
rate of three cols, per week. Snazelle, who talks of see-
ing us again, is evidently doing well.
A theatrical manager, now in Melbourne, states that he
always prefers to play in very moral townships when he
can find them; and invariably inquires into the ethical
status of a place at which he has never previously opened
before making his fixtures. The very worst he ever struck
was in a Goulburn district township, and a leading citizen,
condoling with him in a bar next morning, said : “You was
a fool to open ’ere, anyhow. I could a-told you it was no
go.” “ Why, how’s that ? ” asked the thespian, “ there’s
no competition, and there are a good many people in the
district.” “No competition!” squealed the leading
citizen. “ I tell you no theatrical show what comes here
can compete with Sloan’s paddock.” Sloan’s paddock was
the local lovers' camping-ground.
One of Sydney’s leading musicians puts his hair in curl
papers every night, and sometimes in the morning appears
in his “ crackers ” before his pupils.
Cockaigne : “I would like to ask what became of Mrs.
Charles Young, afterwards Mrs. Hermann Yezin. Is she
dead ? I don’t think so. But it must be nearly 20 years
since I saw a notice of her acting anything.”
Stated that a notorious stage amateur, now said to be
on her way to Australia, asked a Parisian theatrical
manager what terms he would give her to appear mid
nodings on but a few bracelets. The manager requested
her to call for examination. She did, and after a critical
inspection was offered a few francs to appear at end of
matinees to help empty the theatre rapidly in order to
facilitate the preparation for the evening performance.

with very small parts at Melb. Royal.”
Sidelight: “ The modesty of the ‘ wings ’ is something
for convents to copy. Stage etiquette requires that you
see nothing, hear nothing, and, above all, say nothing.
The ballerinas hear no rudeness, for there is none to hear.
Everything spells bread and butter. Once only did I
hear a remark from a super recruit, but he was promptly
squelched by his co-supers. One young and Don Juan-
esque stage-manager be-dears the girls and directs them
with curse or caress, but, as I said, he is young, and as
butcher, or baker, or bottle-merchant would probably be
that other thing. This only of the ‘ perfesh ’ at the
theatre. Outside—well, that is outside, but the ‘ wings ’
make them angels.”
Theorbo : “ About Nicolini. It was a matchmake of
Empress Eugenie, to couple Patti with the Marquis de
Caux, a decent little fellow, as the Tuileries and Biarritz
went in those days, and wealthy. Patti was to be kept
respectable, but he was oldish, and never congenial.
Presently Nicolini turned up, such a tenor as they used
to send to Australia in Lyster’s time, but Patti would
sing with no other. The Paris badauds said that, in
Gounod’s ‘ Romeo and Juliet,’ he kissed Patti 21 times
in the balcony scene, whereas the regulation number was
only five, showing an excess of 16. And such luscious
ones ! All Paris went to enjoy them, by deputy. Caux
squirmed and ‘ sacred,’ but without avail. Patti coolly
went and lived with Nicolini, though he was wedded and
familied, and they lived together 10 years ere they were
married. The R.C. church would never recognise the
marriage, which had to be done by some amiable, well-
paid Kinsman. Bui Patti had found her affinity, and was
devoted to Nicolini till death. Nicolini’s daughters lived
with them. When Nicolini was slowly fading away Patti
would always rush home, by special train if need be, to
Craig-y-Nos, after giving a London concert. In-
deed, it was only by his persuasion that she
would leave the room to which his illness confined
him. Caux had disappeared into cash-salved obscurity,
after a scandalous scene between him and Patti, in
St. Petersburg. Nicolini partly won his way to Patti's
love, or perhaps somewhat retained it, through her
stomach. He was a remarkably skilful cook, knowing all
her fads. Nor would he suffer her to talk on the days
when she was to sing. The feature of the evening was
the little supper, which he cooked with his own hands.
The suitable balance of fat was always the crucial thing.
Patti had eleven doctors for Nicolini during his last ill-
ness, including the most distinguished obtainable in
Europe.”
Albani finds the responsiveness of Australian audiences
positively inspiring, she says. Baritone Pauli, whose
encores are quite as loud as Madame’s in a general waj,
would probably make the same remark. And still there
remains the fact that two-thirds of the crowd at an
Albani concert are more or less disappointed, ana nean,
all of the “ inspiring ” uproar proceeds from the compara-
tively few people who have come prepared to enthuse
over everything.
Paradis : “ When I rode bareback, before the Prince of
Wales.”—Gag of the veteran equestrienne in “ v\ bile
London Sleeps.” A point in this play is that the curtain
comes down upon an audaciously absurd climax betoie
one person in the audience starts to move. It almost
seems like a joke at melodrama, in the guying spirit of
Lemaitre’s Robert Maeaire. The circus business of the
first act ts fresh and lively, done in an acceptable spirit of
burlesque, especially by O’Neill, Gourlay, North, Miss
Dearwyn, and Miss Leighford. Chalmers is versatile, a
most unctuous funny rogue in this show. Miss Eugenie
Duggan has common stage faults of pronunciation, in
giving one letter for another, both of vowels and conso-
nants. She is a nice little girl, but why does she say her
lover’s “ vooyce is mewsick to her soul ” V
Melb. : “ ‘ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ’on the board again. It
is inexplicable why the 80 nights’ run of this piece, at the
old wooden Melb. Princess’s, was the second longest Aus-
tralian run on record, the longest being ‘ Tambour Major,
100 nights, Melb. Opera House, with Pattie Laveme,
Nellie Stewart, Harwood, Mervin and Wallace. J- B.
Howe, familiar in Australia, relates, in his ‘ Autobiogra-
phy,’ how he acted in ‘ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ’ for a booming
12 months’ run in New York, but it ruined the manager.
This was a comedy-theatre, the habit*u6s were all broken
up, dispersed, and nothing could be got to follow on.
How about a “grand complimentary ” benefit to George
Titheradge, who will soon be leaving this couni ry in
search of an engagement ? The Bulletin has a notion
that either Melbourne or Sydney, or both cities, would
take kindly to a Titheradge testimonial performance at
advanced prices. Which manager speaks ?
“Begone Dull Care” Maccabe is contemplating a
journey abroad. The airy veteran’s health is not keeping-
pace with his spirits. Maccabe must by now be verging
on 80.
About high time that Albani gave up worrying “ God
Save the Queen ”on every possible occasion. At Adelaide
she got through that inane and wearisome dittv much to
the disappointment of the listeners who serenaded her
and expected something tangible in return. Had she
only stuffed the warblers with lager beer, or kissed the
ugliest duckling of the lot, some enthusiasm would have
resulted; but to think that for repayment she should
have pounded such a hideous chestnut as the Unnatural
Anathema argues a ghastly indifference to the feelings
of her auditors. Can the Albani really be laboring under
the hallucination that Australians do want to hear
Gorsavequeen ?
Last week, for probably the first time since New-
castle existed, a new play made its first Australian
appearance at that coaly city. The play in question was
the “ White Squadron,” a drama by the American, J. H.
Harkins, and dealing in a large and gory fashion with one
of the many Cuban insurrections. Every play has to be
tried on the dog somewhere, and MacMahon Brothers
tried this one on the coal-begrimed dog at Newcastle with
good results. Everything is more or less black at New-
castle, and the “ White Squadron,” with its gay banners
and the bright red blood it sheds promiscuously, probably
went well by force of contrast.
The coming theatrical sensation will be the trial of an
action shortly in one of the Australian provinces, in
which plaintiff (a manager) sues defendant (a much-
married squatter) to recover damages for the alleged
abduction of a burlesque beauty, who, he claims, was
under a professional engagement to him, the manager.
Haresfoot: Australian playgoers are mostly honest, uh
romantic bourgeois with no pasts. These past-y dramas
appeal to the idle, moneyed, melancholy, half-guinea
London stalls, in full dress and chokerdom, a glazed
whitey sepulchre of shirt-front covering the dead men’s
bones. Such wise fools see their own spectral selves in a
dramatic Rontgen, and drink dramatic absinthe.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
In the judging at Sydney Show soundness does
not carry due weight with the judges. . A horse to take a
prize in a show-ring should be sound in wind and limb.
A lady’s hack prize-taker was lame ; a stallion which re-
ceived a prize had bad hocks, a big knee, and bad splints ;
a trotting mare prize-taker had a hip knocked down,
which should have proved fatal to her show as a brood
mare ; and a pony with a shoe-boil as big as an orange
was placed before a large field of sound animals.
* * * *
The high jump at Sydney Agricultural Show
was too long-drawn, occupying the whole of Monday
afternoon ; and in future events of the sort it would be
well to start higher, as by the time the horses work up
to the crucial point they are pretty well played out. At
6ft. loin, there were three in it: Bland Holt’s Young
Highlander, J. J. Garvan’s ’Frisco, and W. Riddle’s
Streaklight. Six feet threw Highlander out; Streaklight
failed at 6ft. 2in.; which ’Frisco cleared in gallant style,
thus winning.
• * • *
As usual, the trotting competitions at Sydney
Agricultural Show proved the most exciting of the ring
events. Unfortunately, the handsome imported Tuxedo
showed temper and declined to make a start, which threw
the smart little pacer out of it. Great things were ex-
pected of Economy, a pacer from M.L. with a good
record, but whatever chance he had was discounted by
bad driving and a too-free use of the whip. Pickaninny
showed foot and a taking style, but when it came to a
trial of speed Knight's pair, Syren and Harold 8., fairly
outpaced the field in both harness and saddle trials, the
pair dividing honors. Syren finished first and Harold
second in harness, the places being reversed under saddle.
The suggestion here made that the events be open to
pacers and trotters was adopted. The only fault with the
trotting was that there was not enough of it, and writer
feels safe in saying that he is only voicing the public
sentiment in asking Mr. Webster to give more trotting
events in future exhibitions. However, it must be con-
fessed that owners of trotting sires are not as forward as
they might be with assistance in the way of prizes.
The favorites asserted themselves at the A.J.C.
meeting. That tough old warrior, Battalion, starting at
6 to 4 just managed to put down Mirella in the Autumn
Stakes, Charge a long-off third. Oxenham’s Syerla, at
6to 1 had an easy three-length win in the Doncaster,
Merlo’olas (12 to i) second, with Tramp at his girth.
Gozo’s moMMi is going up by leaps and bounds. In the St*
Leger Amberite (at 100 to 7 on) beat The Tola hollow.
The Chief (a Gozo gelding) beat a big field in the Flying
Handicap, for which he started at 12 to 1; Vedette, the
Maorilander, who ran second, was disqualified at the
scales W. R. Wilson’s Bobadil (10 to 9 on) annexed the
Champagne Stakes; Merloolas, the runner-up in the
Doncaster, started at 7 to 3 for Sydney Cup, and beat
Amberite (4. to 1) by three lengths, War God (25 to 1)
third. , * » *
Battalion, somehow, has not yet caught on with
backers. In the light of his Australian Cup performance
it was 10 to 1 on the old horse winning the Autumn
Stakes at Randwick last Saturday, but the “ books ” were
glad to take 6to 4 when the flag fell. Mirella, at sto 2
against, was comparatively a much hotter favorite,
although she hadn’t been good enough to beat Eleusive
(a non-staver) for the V.R.C. Oaks.
V * * * #
Syerla, who was overworked for the first half of
his two-year-old career, ran only once during the next 12
months or more.
Bv the triumphant Gozo, from Cerise and Blue,
a good many old punters must have backed Syerla’s
breeding. Nobody who remembers the winning rushes of
his game little dam can help fancying Syerla when Oxen-
ham is sweet on the chance.
• • * *
Donald Wallace took a little trick again on
Saturday (at Caulfield) with his useful mare, Moonlyong,
a daughter of Mentor. She started at 7 to 1, for there
are no followers of the good old magpie jacket nowadays,
but the sporting section of the public didn’t quite forget
to give Moonlyong a cheer.
By visiting any metropolitan training track in
Auslraliajustpriortoan important meeting, anyone versed
in racing matters can soon assure himself why so many
horses turn rogues, or “ welshes ” as ’tis termed at ’Ome.
The heart-breaking gallops against the watch, in which
the wretched nags are ridden out to the bitter end under
cruel punishment at the hands of ignorant and inex-
perienced stable lads, are enough to wreck the docility and
“manners” of the best behaved and stoutest animal
extant. The unnecessary severe preparation that so
many horses now undergo too often renders them “ duck-
hearted,” and their subsequent in-and-out running brings
odium to their owners when not really deserved.
* » * *
.£4OO was lost in bets on one hand in a poker-
machine in a Pitt-street pub. (Sydney), the other day.
* * * *
Last year Melbourne post-office collected £4OOO
for registration of letters addressed to “ Tattersall,” say-
ing nothing of commission on money-orders and postal
notes, postage on unregistered letters, and business done
at country offices. “ Tattersall ”is worth about £16,500
a year to the Tasmanian postal department.
* * * *
The Tas. father of the winner of one of Tatt.’s
big prizes hanged himself in a coach the other day. No-
thing much in that, except that there appears to be a
horsey flavor about it.
* * * * *
For years past, racing has been practically dead
at Broken Hill; but an autumn-meeting, with the Silver
City Cup as a centre-piece, and a Tattersall’s sweep as an
added attraction, has just given promise of a revival.
The winner turned up in an outsider —Fair Saxon, an
animal which, a few months before, had been picked up
in Adelaide for £l2. His owner had £3 on him !
* * * *
The Mikado of Japan, who has an agent in
Australia just now on the look-out for likely racehorses,
is a great patron of the turf, but allows no betting.
* * * *
Still another starting-machine—Tait’s—tried at
the Broken Hill Silver City Cup-meeting, and worked
without a flaw. The barrier is jerked out, away from the
horses, instead of rising perpendicularly.
* * * *
A Pitt-street, Sydney, sight on Saturday night
is the crowd yarning outside Tattersall’s early in the
evening. As the clock strikes seven they disappear like
so many rabbits in their burrows, and the street is
deserted. Seven o’clock is the hour the “shops” pay-
out. What a show if the police really meant business !
* » * *
When the Maori first took to racing, he dis-
dained the idea of his moke being allotted less weight
than others ; now he scrutinises a handicap as a sin-shifter
does the collection-plate, and yells oaths if given an
ounce too much. But some country-farmers, who only
race once a year, still think it a stain on their prad’s
character to be placed at the foot of the list. At Ainuri
(M.L.), the other day, one put up 2st. 121 b., another 3st.
121 b. overweight, to have a cut at the good ’uns on even
terms.
* * * *
The nefarious electric whip device has given
way to the electric pad in Murkan race riding. The pad
(imitation lead) holds an electric, battery and is so
arranged as to stimulate the speed of a slow ’un. Three
jocks at New Orleans were fined SOdols. each for being
possessed of the new speed-device.^
As compared with the wretched fees given to
Australian cross-country jocks, George Williamson, by
no means England's best rider, was engaged to ride
Timon in Liverpool Grand National, his remuneration
being fixed at £5OO, win or lose. Timon didn’t win the
Liverpool G.N., but the jock’s fee shows what money
must have been wageied on his mount.
* * * *
Takings at the matches of Stoddart’s team :
S.A., first match, £1200; Victoria (first), £2222 6s. 6d. ;
N.S.W. (first), £2730 12s. 4d. ; Newcastle, £200; Glen
Units, £l2O ; Brisbane (first), £750 ; Toowoomba, £l5O ;
Arniidale, £l5O ; first test, £2300 ; Bendigo, £250 ; second
test, £3340; Ballarat, £150; Stawell, £120; third test,
£2160 ; Hamilton, £l5O ; fourth test, £2282 ; N.S.W (re-
turn), £1450; University, £150; Brisbane, £135; fifth
test, £3250; Vic. (return), £662; S.A. (return), £414;
total, £24,836 ss. 4d. The cost of the tour was £12,000.
Some of the grounds took percentages, and about £9OOO
was left for division by the promoter*.
Each of the pro s of Stoddart’s team received
£3OO clear of all expenses, and they divided amongst
them £l5O as “talent money.” They were also given a
bonus of £25 apiece by Melb. C.O. The Sydney trustees
declined to join in this part of the business, as the pro’s
had done more than well on the trip. Also, Stoddart
gave them £25 each out of his Reaper-sweep win.
Richardson, in addition to his whack, got £lOO, for ser-
vices rendered during the previous tour. Another £5O
was allowed the team for “ tips” on the boat. No mem-
bers of an English Eleven were ever treated more hand-
somely. than were the pro’s of the late team. Per-
haps this was responsible for the sorry failure
of one or two of them. At ’Ome, they have
to rest content with beer and bread and cheese. But
they were not in Australia a fortnight before one or two
of their number began to criticise the various brands of
fizz with the air of men who had been suckled on it. On
one occasion, in Sydney, a pro. ordered two bottles to be
put on ice to celebrate his birthday, but in the evening
he countermanded the order by saying “We don’t want
none of that ’eer wine I hordered, as Ranji has come to
my reskew.” The dusky talker had sent round word to
Roberts’s that wine should be provided all round at his ex-
pense. An open-handed potentate is Ranji.
• * » *
In a test-match, some of the players were hang-
ing their tongues out for soda-and-champagne ! Bill
Howell (log.) “ They want to dig post-holes in the bush
to feel a thirst. Then water would be good enough for
’em.”
* «. * * *
Copy of a letter dated Dec., ’97, and received
by an Australian country resident:—“ Dear Sir, —I under-
stand the arrangements in connection with the English
cricketers are in your hands, and as you may be planning
out something for them to do on Monday and Tuesday
next I write to tell you that I have written to Mr. Sheri-
dan asking the whole of the team out here (a station) on
Monday principally to see the machine-shearing, and in
my letter I said details could be arranged after their
arrival (in the district). I have not yet had any reply
from Mr. Sheridan, but expect one this afternoon when I
send in for the evening mails. Of course I included him
in the invitation. I propose to bring the gentlemen out
here on Saturday or Sunday afternoon, whichever they
prefer and puttingthem up ; then to charter a four-horse
’bus on Monday morning to bring the players out, they to
bring their own provender. After doing the woolshed to
picnic on the creek under some of the willow trees when I
would supply them with liquor and cherries. They could
return to town in the evening.”
* * * *
Ranji’s final Review of Reviews article was
dictated by him while lying on the broad of his narrow
back on the Ormuz. He was dreadfully seasick between
Adelaide and Albany, and would readily have embraced
the worst barracker in Australia in return for a cure.
Ranji and Stoddart are strongly in favor of
Storer in connection with the Bannerman affair. In Ade-
laide Ranji said, “I only regret that when Bannerman
gave me out in the last test I did not W'alk round to him
and say, * You are a cheat.’ ” Had he done so, of course,
Bannerman would have driven him for about four there
and then. What a loss Ranji’s-discretion was to the
evening papers 1
* • * *
H. : “ Two rank absurdities to imagine :
Brassey’g return, and Ranji fetching out an eleven:”
For the past ten years umpire Jim Phillips’
travelling has averaged 33,000 miles yearly. He has car*
ried that rotunda hat of his 130,000 miles. Yet we don’t
hear whether he has approved of the Commonwealth Bill
or not.
» * * •
Writing in the Manchester Courier, McLaren
(quoted by S.M. Her aid) thus sums up the causes of the
Englishmen’s defeat at the hands of the Australians. He
wrote during the progress of the third test-match : —“ I
will try to explain how the Australians bowl out here.
Trumble bowls just outside the off stump a very good
length, and varies his pace. At the same time he can
send down a ball that goes away with his arm. That
means that the ball will go away a couple of inches, and
instead of it being played back to the bowler, up it goes
in the slips off the edge of his bat. M'Kibbin breaks
both ways, and puts in fast ones continually. Noble
disguises his slow ball very well, and he often
makes it swerve in the air. He is always trying
dodges. Jones relies on his great pace to get men out,
and he is wonderfully accurate for a fast bowler. Now
take our bowlers. Hearne, Hayward, Hirst and Richard-
son scarcely ever vary their pace. The first three never
do, but Richardson does put in a slow one occasionally,
and Briggs is always trying dodges. Ido not wish in any
way to run our bowlers down, but, when a batsman has
played two overs on a good wicket against them, he has
then nothing to fear, and appears to play all with the
greatest of ease, excepting Richardson and Briggs. Were
our bowlers at home, it would be a different matter, for
there they get assistance from the wicket, which they
never get here except when sticky, and, so far, we have
not had a wet wicket. The good bowler out here is the
man whose ball has a peculiar flight in the air, and who
is mixing up fast and slow, and always trying to break
the ball. This is an understood thing with every Aus-
tralian bowler.
“ In my humble opinion English bowlers of to-
day are below the standard. Let us take the last three
seasons in England, and what is the result! Century
after century is made because we have had batting
wickets, and during the last Australian tour in England,
when over a hundred centuries were scored, only three
were scored against Australian bowling. That takes a lot
of getting over. We throw so many fours at the bats-
men out here with our fast and medium pace bowlers.
The slow bowlers are the successful ones, and the Austra-
lians evidently believe in them, for Noble, M'Kibbin,
Trott and Trumble are all slower than Hayward. M‘Leod
is medium pace, and to me is the easiest of the lot, in
spite of his having got a wicket or two at Sydney in our
large score of 551. The Australians make us get our
fours; that is, we have to force for fours fre-
quently, by pulling and lifting over the fieldsmen’s
heads, whereas the runs came much easier off
our bowlers, and it is certainly not owing to
their batsmen being better than ours. I mentioned
before we started that we lacked variety in our bowling,
but my words were evidently not heard during the noise
in the railway-station, and I read next day that I thought
we were a strong side, especially in bowling. Take a
great bowler out of a team, and the side at once becomes
a moderate one. lam thinking of Peel, for with him in
this team we would be a fine side. He knew exactly how
to bowl out here, and the Australians, too, feared him.
Briggs, you will be glad to hear, is bowling as well as ever
he bowled in his life, and with by no means the best of
luck ; but that is only one bowler in form. Richardson

can see had it been Yorkshire pitches instead of these

either, when one looks at the names on paper, and I for
one do not think we are at the present moment playing

There has been a lot of puerile paragraphing of
the “ record ” score (1094) made by Melb. University
against a suburban club-team; also photos, of the 11
heroes concerned in this “record” achievement were
published last week. But what more is a bloated batting
score than conclusive evidence that the bowling was
weak? Previously there had been a “record” put up
against a University eleven.
Owing to the high rate of scoring, the cricketing
world has of late years been talking of narrowing the bat
or widening the wickets. However, it does not appear as
if the back-blocks of Maoriland are in need of any such
assistance just yet. In a match, Canvastown v. Cullens-
ville, recently, the former eleven went down for 9, eight
players making blobs. Their opponents did better,
scoring 13 and nine blobs.
* » * *
In the third heat of the international mile at
Melb. Druids' Gala the quad captained by Browne came
into touch with a relieving quad, and a long-looked-for
“ cropper ” was the result. Bike-meeting multitudes had
often wondered what would happen to any eight-legged
crew who might get capsized whilst racing against time,
and here they had an object-lesson. The four men didn’t
come down with a sickening crash upon their respective
skulls. They shot sideways off the machine, and were
scraped, rather than smashed. Browne, who seemed to
come off worst of the lot, had to be borne off the track ;
the others gathered up their fragments of missing flesh
and retired dreamily.

Hard-working Porta is the latest cyclist to pass
painfully under the doctor’s hands through over-training.
Also Kux, the Adelaide to Melbourne record-holder, fell
an easy victim to typhoid, from which he barely re-
covered ; and overlanding Coleman is still tottery. Not-
withstanding which the long-distance, rough-riding-
against-time idiocy continues, Virgin making a “ record”
from Broken Hill to Adelaide.
* * * *
Tom Relph, the well-known Swift wheelman,
bears a striking facial resemblance to Virgin, the over-
lander who cycled from Perth to Brisbane. There the re-
semblance ceases, for Tom, in bulk and speed, would
make three of the overlander.
* • * *
The Chow in his time plays many parts, but the
Chow as athlete is a new departure. The champion
sprinter in Normanton, however, is a Mongol, who has
successfully run down the flower of local cyclists. John
avers that he can “ lun mo’ betta as kangaloo,” and issues
a challenge to the whole of the Gulf country to come on
and beat him, if it can. The Celestial is pretty much in
evidence at race meetings and athletic gatherings in the
North, but only as a speculator and gambler; never
(with this exception) as a performer.
* * * »
Wrestling and boxing, which have had strong
supporters on the Westralian fields in R. B. Pell, Morgan
D’Arcy, and others, are there going the way they go
everywhere. Pearce’s Hall, the scene’ of most of the
“goes,” was rushed the other night after a three-fall
bout between Pearce and the Afghan Cumbo, which the
former won ; and the sport, which had been degenerating
for some time, is now declared to be no longer respectable.

* * * *
Australian-bred animals have made their mark
on American coursing as well as the turf. Some 15 years
ago a dog named King John—breeding unknown—was
imported from Australia to the States, and, after making
a name for himself, left numerous progeny to perpetuate
it. About three years ago, Mr. Lopez, well-known in Syd-
ney racing circles, took across a dog named Waratah, by
Livingstone out of Taxation, and a bitch, Wattle Bloom,
by Welcome Dan out of Judy, and the produce of the
pair are carrying all before them in California.
•*» . . •
Two S.A. rod-fishers recently had nearly 1J
hour’s struggle, in the river Glenelg, with a huge mulla-
way, which got away in the end, after towing the boat
over two miles, through the hook fairly sawing its way
through the jaw. The only glimpse obtained of the fish
showed it to be about 4ft. long, and probably 601 b. in
weight.
• * * *
A feature of the recent Wanganui (M.L.) re-
gatta was the Maori canoe races, 30 to 35 men in each
boat. Two ladies’ (white) crews finished desperately after
a j-mile go. The strokes were mother and daughter, the
latter’s four winning by a spurt, or a skirt, or something
of that sort.

Foxes are plentiful in the S.B. of S. Australia ;
Tantanoola Council, in March, paid for 31 scalps, or just
one aday. - How the hair of the conventional British land-
owner would stand on end at such a fact!

T.D.: “Never knew until the other day why
nearly every Chinese ‘merchant’s’ shop has a clock
which agrees virtually to the tick with its next-door
neighbor. Faro banks are drawn exactly to the hour,
and the Chow is extremely' punctilious in the matter of
half-a-minute. And these clocks indicate that some hun-
dreds of shops in Goulburn-street and elsewhere are
devoted exclusively to faro and fan-tan (the latter behind
pine-board partitions, of course); and yet the police
make a tremendous fuss if they raid one of the dens after
perhaps four or five months’ surveillance.”
• * * *
Gerald Quealey, the massive Irishman who is
assistant-registrar of mines at Bendigo, was a few years
back a reporter on the local Independent. Quealey on
one occasion incurred the wrath of the sub-editor, who
left a scathing note for him in the office-rack. Quealey’s
reply was deemed highly unsatisfactory, so the following
day, on receiving a second missive, the big bhoy opened
it, glanced at the nasty remarks it contained, and, turning
down a corner, wrote —“This correspondence must now
cease.” Quealey left the Independent owing to his
pugilistic ambitions. A middle-weight amateur com-
petition being projected at the local athletic club, he
entered, despite the “office” objection. The eventful
night came, and Quealey, after fighting three hard
nuggets, was left in the final with a local blacksmith.
The go was particularly “ willing,” and, as none of the
officials could distinguish the men at the end of the six
rounds, it was given a draw. N ixt day Quealey turned
up at the office all sticking-plaster and bruises, and the
sack followed. However, he soon landed in a much
better billet, and has since blessed the day that he was
pummelled to a pulp.

Sydney was to have seen a lacrosse boom in
July, S.A. and Vic. teams having decided last year to
make the trip this season. The Adelaideans have now
decided, however, to back Out of their engagement.
Lacrosseurs, unlike footballers, have to pay their own
way.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin. —That Russian scientist’s theory
that no amount of exercise will increase the muscular
development of a weak body is nonsense. Is every man
who does laborious work, such as wood-chopping or pick-
swinging, strong by nature ? Not a bit of it; yet compare
the physique of any hundred navvies of 22 or 23 with that
of a hundred clerks of the same age. As boys of 15 the
clerks had probably the advantage—you will see it every
time by comparison of public-school boys with the
children of poorer class, the result of better feeding, cloth-
ing and housing—but the exercise at a time when the
muscles are most modifiable tells every time. Take my
own case. As a child I was a delicate, pallid, consump-
tive-looking weed, but with swimming, football, rowing,
and so on, I have turned myself, though only sft. Bin. high,
into a 12st. 71b. man, and not much of it fat, though I
eat three big meals a day when four and five are not
obtainable. When I was twelve, three doctors prophesied
that I would not reach 14, 16, and 20 years respectively,
yet at 24 I get a first-class life at two different insurance
offices. Of such is the kingdom of exercise.— Bondings .

In the latter part of 1854 a consignment of
50,000 so vs. was sent from London to the Union
Bank of Australia in Melbourne ; the boxes, for
greater safety, being addressed to John Brown,
and not to the Bank. It so happened that the
advice despatched several weeks in advance of the
sovereigns was sent by one of the slow old tubs of
the time ; whereas the coin was forwarded by one
of the White Star clippers, with the consequence
that the gold arrived before John Brown or any-
body else in Melbourne knew anything about it.
When the clipper had discharged nearly all her
cargo, ten strong but ordinary-looking, boxes re-
mained still unclaimed, and the captain had no
small difficulty in prevailing upon one of the
lightermen to take them, as nobody knew any-
thing of “ John Brown,” and there was not only
the risk of losing the freight but of being
put to trouble otherwise over the matter. .Also,
it so happened that a short time previously
a quantity of slates had been imported m exactly
similar boxes. At Melbourne, as was expected,
there was no one to receive and pay freight for
the boxes, which were accordingly dumped con-
temptuously on the wharf in their supposed
character of “ slates ! ” and while they
were afterwards being shifted to make room for
other goods two of them fell off the wharf into the
Yarra without anybody bothering about them.
One morning, several weeks after the vessel that
brought out the gold had left port, several
bank people, accompanie4 by a number of con-
stables all evidently in a state of great excite-
ment, arrived at the wharf and anxiously inquired
about ten small boxes, eight of which were soon
discovered and removed, the other two being
fished out of the river at next low water. What
a gnashing of teeth there was amongst the wharf-
habitues when it transpired that each of the do-
spised boxes held .£5OOO in gold !-
TO A HOLIDAY GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What was it that God forgot
When you were created ?
You, the Lady of Shalott
With Pelagia mated ?
Pair you are, with wondrous eyes,
And you murmur lyric-wise ;
But there was—l know not what—
Something that the Lord forgot.
Was it that He had in view
Beings ultra-human,
So that He forgot that you
Ought to be a woman ?
But I know that more than laughter
Those bright lips have trembled after ;
Pretty, petulant, and hot—
None of these hath God forgot.
Some have called you over-bold
(Little has it hurt you),
Other girls, whose hands were cold
With the grip of virtue.
But your kisses are the flowers
Only of forgetful hours ;
Careless innocence was not
By the careless fates forgot.
You are such as Eve was framed
In the garden olden
When she wandered unashamed
Past the apples golden.
God forgot to give the devil
Power to make you look for evil;
Sight to see an ugly spot—
This it was that God forgot.
Vic.

Prof. Marsh, of Yale College, U.S., has
declared that the average weight of the Japanese
brain is greater than that of any other race. And
brain-weight, loosely speaking, has three-fourths
of the say in the matter of brain-power. The
other fourth is said by hrsdn-quality —number and
character of convolutions and their concents.
With regard to Cardinal Moran’s theory re
De Quiros’ landing-place, it may be worthy of men-
tion that, many years ago, the remains of an old
Spanish ship could be seen on the shores of Pt.
Curtis. Colin Archer (of Fram building celebrity)
dug about it in search of treasure, but in vain.
Remains of an old stone forge, of two timber-lined
wells, and of some strange stone carvings in the
rocks were also to be seen at a date comparatively
recent. Also, about 50 years ago, some curious
old brass cannon with Spanish inscriptions of the
sixteenth century were found on the shores of the
same harbor, and afterwards removed. So, after
all, there may be something in Card. Moran’s
theory.
Thirty years ago, at Rockhampton, a man
bought into a public-house, and, as he immediately
became his own best customer, his more prudent
wife set about providing against the inevitable
smash. She made a prisoner of every pound-note
she could lay hands on, and, withdrawing the
bones from her stays, inserted her appropriations
into the space thus provided. This went on for a
year, and, as she wore the stays day-and-night all
the time, and was a fat and spacious woman, when
the bust-up came along she badly needed a change.
But when, in the privacy of a new home, she
ripped open her store-house, not half-a-dozen notes
were worth their face-value. The rest were pulp.
For the proper understanding of this yarn
it is necessary to know that at American hotels
celery is served at table with the green head on,
and that in America the generic name for all
sorts of insects, big and little, is “bug.” Well,
Rooney, who couldn’t read, arrived in New York
from very far out West for the first time, and sat
down at the hotel dinner-table. “ Oi’ll tek some
of that,” said he, pointing in an indefinite kind of
way with his big thumb to the first three or four
lines of the menu. The waiter sized up the situa-
tion, and brought Patrick some thin soup.
“Now,” sez Pat, pointing a bit further down the
bill, “ Oi’ll have some of this.” “ This ” happened
to be celery, and when the Rooney had devoured
a full head thereof, he proceeded to indicate a line
further up the bill-of-lading. His choice proved
to be a lobster ; he had never seen one before, and
didn’t like the look of it. “ Tek it away at wanst,”
said the Rooney, “ Oi dhrank yer hot wather, Oi
ait yer bokay, but Oi’m damned if Oi’ll ait yer
bhug.”
A Bananaland scientist wants to change
the name “barramundi”—by which the giant
perch of Centralia is known—to palmer, a term
used in Mackay district. The true barramundi is
found only in the Dawson and may be recognised
by a brown spot on each of its large scales. The
more common “barramundi,” or giant perch, is
found in the sea, in the Fitzroy and its tribu-
taries, also in fresh-water lagoons whither it is
sometimes’ borne by the floods and left by the ebb
at spawning time. Strange to say, neither Bris-
bane, Mary, nor Burnett is ever traversed by the
barramundi.
John Meagher, merchant, of Bathurst,
who has been banqueted on leaving for a trip
to his native-sod, once opposed F. B. Suttor for
Bathurst, and frightened Frank much. Suttor
spoke first, and complimented John on his “ well-
known eloquence.” When Meagher spoke, he
disclaimed any acquaintance with oratory, “ but,”
said he, “ I’ll tell you a story. A priest met a boy
once, and enquired if the youngster said his
prayers. ‘Well, plase, yer riverence, I don’t
know much about them, but I have a brother who
is a divil at them.’ It’s my brother Martin, Mr.
Suttor means, not me.”
Turner and Sutherland’s so-called “De-
velopment of Australian Literature ” met its
deserts unexpectedly in London. Pall Mall
Gazette, of March 3, justly praised the biogra-
phies of Gordon, Clarke, and Kendall, and
Turner’s introductory essay is as justly damned.
Daily News, of Feb. 28, remarks that “page
after page of laudation is given to the writings of
authors who have no Australian celebrity what-
ever,” while “irritating and pompous patronage
is extended to well-known writers.” And other
papers follow in similar strains —with a truth con-
trasting surprisingly with the usual publishers’
puffs.
In “ Human Fads, Foibles, Fallacies, and
Fallibilities,” Dr. Knaggs, of Sydney, returns to
the discussion of the less obvious causes of
erroneous observation and mistaken judgment.
He reviews and reinforces, to begin with, his con-
tention that, putting aside deliberate imposture,
there is nothing in so-called spiritualism that can-
not be accounted for by such an infectious self-
deception as lay at the bottom, for instance, of
the belief in witchcraft and the various hysterical
religious “ revivals ” which have punctuated the
history of the world. Much stress is laid upon
the power for error of preconception, “ expectant
attention,” and the like ; while, with regard to the
responsibility of unsuspected sensory modifica-
tions for disaster on a large scale, Dr. Knaggs
makes the eminently reasonable suggestion that
all persons employed in connection with passenger-
transit should be submitted to a periodical testing
of their faculties, both physical and mental.

INVALIDS
AND
THE AGED.
That same familiar girl — this time only 10
years old—turned up in Melb. last week, and spun
a tale to a sympathetic copper about a man and a
woman having poisoned her with some “ white
stuff ”in a glass of brandy at a city pub. The
youngster was treated at the hospital, and, after
being pronounced out of danger, inquiries were
started. It "was found that she peddled soap for a
living, and was addicted to chewing the stock-in-
trade as an aid to bogus fits whereby she ex-
tracted sympathy and cash from passers-by. Try-
ing this game in a city pub., the fit was accepted
as genuine, but, instead of money, a humane
barmaid had given her a gratis glass of brandy
and peppermint (the white stuff) to pull her round.
When the liquor was suddenly poured into her
she naturally swallowed the soap, and the brandy,
of course, produced the same effect on her that it
would on any other healthy person. The suffering
creature, whom the policeman found against a
wall, was simply a little tight and dreadfully
bilious owing to the soap.
“ Miles,” to The Bulletin :
As to what Russia may do auainst English commerce,
turn up Capt. Semtnes’s book, on his voyages in the
Alabama, almost a sister vessel to Gov. Brassey’s Sun-
beam. Both were engineered, by the Lairds. Semmes
captured a ship of some kind, on the average, for nearly
every day of his cruises ; some days he took two or three.
Whenever practicable, Semmes burnt the captive, but
was careful to do no personal injury. Nineteen years or
so back, just after the greatest war-scare, the Czar sent
three of his fast cruisers—the Vestnik, Plastoun, and
Afrika —round to Sydney and Melbourne. While in Hob-
son's Bay, the Afrika took a lot of citizens on a pleasure-
trip. Sem mes’s book, “Adventures Afloat,” was carelessly
left on the cuddy table. A Russian officer told one of the
guests that it was supplied to every ship in their navy.
Semmes’s choicest adventure was with Commodore Van-
derbilt’s fine steamer, the Guiding Star, which carried gold
from ’Frisco to New York. Semmes tried to pounce on
her with the gold, but could only manage the job when
she was making for Frisco to fetch it. He got up the
Alabama in exact imitation of the U.S. cruiser Tornado,
and sported the Stars and Stripes. Thus he actually
spoke the Vanderbilt boat—shouted through his trumpet,
“ This is the Confederate war steamer Alabama ! ” and
added to his quartermaster, “Haul down that polluted
rag!” whereupon the Palmetto flag went up.
The Star had a great number of passengers,
who were waltzing on deck at the time.
The Star tried to get away. Semmes trained his swivel
on her, and brought down the fore-top with a round shot,
whereupon she hauled to. Semmes said to his mate, “ I
would dearly like to burn her, but can’t dispose of the
people.” He called his handsomest lieutenant. Chap-
man, put him in a magnificent purple uniform, with gilt
epaulettes, buttons, and facings, and said, “Go aboard,
and give ’em a g-ood frightening.” Chapman, reaching
the Star, bullied the captain and owner’s-agent to rights.
The passengers were in consternation, ladies fainting
around. At last Chapman took off the mask of terror, and
said the Star might go. Semmes writes : “ A timid fawn
crept up v itli a little pair of scissors and snipped off one of
his gilt buttons as a memento. Then the women all began
to operate on him. Buttons, epaulettes, plumes all went.
When he returned I said: “By thunder! Chapman,
you re plucked clean as a fowl.” This is how Semmes, in
writing, opera-bouffes a serious occurrence. It recalls a
little-known thing which once occurred in Hobson’s Bay,
Melb., related to me by Mr. T. Cook, now in Melb.,
who was a sailor-boy on lI.M. brig Fantome at the time.
Capt. Forbes, of the Black Ball liner Sehoinberg, after-
wards wrecked, was a martinet. As she was actually
leaving the bay four of her sailors managed to hang shirts
on the ratlines-a familiar signal that they volunteered
for the navy. The Fantome sent a boat. Forbes tried to
get his ship away,, but the Fantome fired around shot
across her bows—the only hostile one ever fired in Hob-
son's Bay. The men were transhipped, and the Schoin-
berg had to wait. In connec.tion with the Alabama, just
a mention of the Melbourne docking of the Shenandoah
and the banquet to her officers at the Melbourne Club.
England paid. Captain Waddell, of the Shenandoah,
afterwards commanded a Pacific mail-boat between Syd-
ney and San Francisco.

Frank Philpott, the Silverstream (M.L.)
murderer, who planted his dead mate in the bush
d la Butler, was peacefully hanged at Wellington,
and left this beautiful confession behind him :
Thank God because I die this morning at peace with all
men. I have acknowledged and confessed my sin in the
face of Almighty God. ... I pray God to have mercy
upon me. I realise my position and thank God that He
has pardoned me. ... I bid you all farewell. May God
for ever bless you.
If there is anything in the typical, parson-made
confession of faith, the one really sure way to
Heaven is to start early in the morning from the
end of a greased rope. One epitaph would fit
almost every such case: “Scragged in the sure
and certain hope of a blessed resurrection.”
Eye-witness : “ How’s this for grit? Jack
Graham, a massive M.L. West-coast miner, turned
up one morning at the local hospital to be operated
on for a growth upon his burly chest—due, doubt-
less, to the many ox-felling blows received in that
region. * Chloroform be d d!’ he said ; lit
his pipe, stripped, stretched himself on the
table, and told the doctors to * carve
away! ’ They gashed him accordingly for
about a foot in length, but Jack smoked
calmly through it all, and, when he had
been duly stitched up, he asked somebody to
fill his pipe for him, and departed.” Which re-
calls that in Western Victoria, years ago, one
Hourigan, smashed up by a threshing machine,
refused chloroform and smoked while both arms
were amputated, one above and the other below
the elbow.
Col. Kenneth Mackay’s Australian Horse
rolled up 400 strong to camp—which will take a
lot of beating for a first muster. They are a fine
lot of blanky bushmen, and, looked at beside the
city warriors, are an effective argument in favor
of the contention that, in war-time, the bush
troops will be the backbone of our army. Their
horses, too, are far ahead of anything else in camp.
If the libel-action Paddy M’Caughan v.
Melb. Argus should come to trial, a good deal
will depend on the judge’s summing-up of the
words “amoving mass of brawn” as applied to
plaintiff’s overwhelming appearance in fancy cos-
tume. Brawn means muscle. The muscles of the
Village Blacksmith’s brawny arm “were strong
as iron bands.” Also brawn is a dictionary term
for the flesh of swine—prepared in a certain way—
but the Argus didn’t indicate that Mr. Mac’s
brawn was comminuted and encased in sausage-
skin. Anyhow, the bard who is dealing with an
Irishman’s physique can’t be too careful in his
choice of rhymes.
William John Lyne : “If the result of the
(Federal) Referendum is a substantial number of
votes and a majority in favor of the Bill, I think
it is the duty of Parliament to recognise that it is
the will of the people.” The other way of putting
it is that, according to all the canons of Parlia-
mentary Government, if there is a majority of
even one, that simply brushes William John out
of the way like a fly that is blown off the national
ear. It means, unless Constitutional Govern-
ment is all a howling joke, that the express train
is coming and that William John is standing on
the line, sadly, slowly thinking whether he should
recognise it or not. The fact that Lyne really
thinks Parliament should recognise the voice of
the majority of its masters—the people—is very
interesting. It is the voice of the grasshopper
that the elephant has trodden on remarking that
he really thinks it his d”ty to turn flat, as provided
by the constitoosh’n.
111 a list of reasons (mostly untrue) why
N.S.W. should vote against the Federal Constitu-
tion, Sydney Telegraph includes this one : “ Be-
cause the Convention Bill perpetuates existing
bounties on exports in Victoria and prevents retali-
ation in other states.” Which is another of the
many crams with which the parochial party
endeavors to educate the community. What the
Draft Constitution says (clause 91) is that the
power of granting bounties on exports remains
with all the states until 30th June this year, and
any agreement to grant bounties made before then
holds good. After that date the states have no
power to grant bounties, and when those previ-
ously granted expire they can’t be renewed.
Therefore N.S.W. has the same power as any
other province to grant bounties on everything,
the unprincipled yell of the morning shoddy organ
to the contrary notwithstanding.
In justice to the Vic. Labor-members and
other people who may have felt hurt by
its correspondent “ Lunchtime’s ” picture of
the Federal Convention restaurant, at Mel-
bourne Parliament House, The Bulletin rises
to remark that “Lunchtime” was a luridly
inartistic perjurer, whose representation of al-
leged facts went into this journal for what it
was worth. The truth of the matter is somewhat
as follows : The Turner Government, having very
little outdoor entertainment to offer to Federal
delegates from other provinces, decided to be hos-
pitable on a liberal scale at Parliament House,,
thereby inviting the Convention to stick to the
premises instead of getting beyond earshot of
Duty’s call. All members of "the local Parlia-
ment and the other four Parliaments interested in
the Federation Bill were placed on the free-feed
list, together with a certain number of pressmen
and all officers of the House engaged in working
the show. Things were done in handsome style
on sound commercial principles, and the cost
of the whole eight weeks’ entertaining will
amount, it is said, to very little more
than the cost of the three weeks’ Conventioneer-
ing in Sydney. The Vic. legislators didn’t roll
up for chicken and champagne every time the bell
rang, and when they came they didn’t bring
“ groups of satellites ” with them, satellites not
being included in the invitation, anyhow. More-
over, the Labor-members, whom “Lunchtime”
depicted as the “ worst offenders,” were among
the least frequent feeders, and not one of them
ever brought, or attempted to bring, a deadbeat
to “ tyke it out of the Government’s champigne.”'
If ’Ancoek, on behalf of the Labor-party, would!
like to curse “ Lunchtime ” in good, round terms,
he has The Bulletin’s permission to relieve his;
feelings accordingly.
Premier Keid, being gazetted Attorney-
General, not Acting-Attorney-General, it is con-
sidered certain that he will hold the office till the
death of the present Parliament. So far, N.S.
Wales has only had one Premier who performed
the full duties of Attorney-General as part of his
Premiership. Martin, on the three occasions of
his Premiership, took over the duties of Attorney-
General. Dailey was virtually Premier part of
the time he was Attorney-General—rather in-
stead of being Premier and Acting-Attorney-
General, he was Attorney-General and Acting-
Premier.
It seemed at one time as though Sturt’s
desert-pea was going to be torn out of existence
in its home in Western N.S. Wales ; but the last
three blazing summers, followed by the rains in
February, have produced such a crop as has not
been seen for years past.
E.S.S. : “Be Dr. Rolph’s hiccough-
remedy. Have never yet met a case that I
couldn’t cure at once. I walk up to the patient,
slap him suddenly, but lightly, behind the
shoulder, and, pointing, say, ‘ What the deuce is
that?” His attention is at once diverted, and
that does the trick. Another way : drink a table-
spoonful of water out of a pannikin. Hold the
far side of the rim between the teeth so that the
mouth of the pannikin is half under the chin.
Then lower the head until you can drink the
water.”
The anti-Federation speech of Hughes,
M.P. (N.S.W.), got nearly three columns of
space and a leading article in Sydney Tels-
graph. The clever Balmain umbrella-man Of four
years ago would have been dismissed contemptu-
ously in three lines, and even now, if he favored
Federation, a paragraph might have been given—
yet they call this ’fair reporting. Also, barrister
Canaway, hitherto unheard-of outside the courts,
gets 14 col. —more than the space allotted to a
Convention delegate who happens to support the
The Federal poll is to be taken in N.S.W.,
Victoria and Tasmania on Friday, June 3. In
S.A. it is fixed for Saturday, June 4, and a public
holiday has been proclaimed to give every citizen
a show to get to the polls. Westralia follows some
time in the dim distance. In that, red, tired land
the Federal Act reads that Parliament is to meet
when it is quite rested, and decide whether any-
thing so common as the people shall be allowed to
have a say in the matter at all. Parliament is due
about the beginning of July, and it has to float
the usual loan before much can be done.
Trenwith’s best point— ad captandum, any-
how—in his defence of the Federal Bill, before the
Melb. Trades, was that, although in a certain rela-
tion four votes in Victoria only equalled one in
Westralia, Tom Jones, miner, of the Kalgoorlie
alluvial, voted with four-power force as well as
John Forrest, Premier. And from that, the
obvious policy for the Laborites and Liberals of
the more populous provinces is to rouse, if they
can, their fellows in the less populous provinces
to recognise this new four-power force of theirs.
Queensland Govt., per medium of its
bacteriologists and subsidised medical men, keeps
tinkering away with the tick question and plum-
ing itself hugely on a number of petty discoveries
never turned to practical account. Most of the
expensive dips have fallen into complete disuse ;
most of the costly anti-tick specifics have been
emptied down gutters and creeks and water-holes.
Meanwhile the cattle have become immune in the
North and West to a great extent, and the old
cattle industry is flourishing just as of yore.
Aid. Carter, talking at Melb. City Council,
supported Bulletin’s view re licensing plumbers
for Melb. sewerage works. “If we have to wait
till these few licensed men make the connections,”
he said, “ very few of us will live to see the sewer-
age work done.” Meantime, the Met. Board of
Works is giving second-class certificates to plumbers
who pass in practice exams, only, and these should
be quite good enough to tackle the bulk of the job.
While Spain threatens to sweep the sea
with privateers it is worth remembering that little
Australia, nearly 100 years ago, did a stroke of
privateering which put a considerable sum of
money in its empty pocket. Two whale-boats one
morning escorted into Port Jackson a Spanish
ship, which the skippers, brandishing rusty
muskets and long knives, declared they had duly
captured on the high seas. The Spanish crew
swore very intelligently that the capture was
illegal, but the whalers overswore them, and the
Vice-Admiralty Court of the day found in favor
of the captors. The result was that the ship was
put up to auction, the captors were rewarded, and
the balance of the cash went into the coffers of
the young nation.
A Yankee paper tells how the captain of
the U.S. warship Maine was in his cabin, writing
to his wife, when Sheol struck the ship. The
lights went out, the shattered ironclad heaved
and plunged, and the captain rushed out and
bumped against a marine who immediately
saluted (in the dark), and, “in a hard, metallic
voice,” said : “ Sir, I have the honor to inform
you that the ship is blown up and is sinking.”
Which beautiful episode will go down to posterity
along with Casabianca and the Roman sentry at
Pompeii, and all the other eloquent tales about
people who stopped to do their back-hair at the
cannon’s mouth.
“ In the Know ” :
Some Sydney cheap restaurant-keepers are in the habit
of throwing in a few coppers amongst boiling peas to
heighten the greenness. Also, a handful of washing-
soda always goes into the tea to extract the utmost from
the leaves. It gives the tea a bitter twang, and cus-
tomers from an honest house, being used to weaker stuff,
think they are getting a superior article. The soda may
not hurt, but the tea is vile to anyone with the good taste
which frequenters of 6d. restaurants are not supposed to
possess. Also, at a coffee-stall, the other night, I saw two
festive idiots having saveloys and potatoes, and when the
proprietor had his back turned and the lid was off the
coffee-tin, into the tin went their saveloy and potato
remnants. In a word, the man who asks for coffee some-
times gets Irish stew !
“When I was a young man,” said a well-
known Sydney merchant, the other day, “ I was
a clerk, but the firm failed and I worked as a
blacksmith’s striker for want of something better
to do. My shopmates played a good joke on me.
I had seen (only at a distance) the pretty daughter
of Mr. , the wool-broker, and openly re-
marked how charming she looked. Presently I
received a regular love-letter, asking me to call at
the house —parents would be out, &c. I went (so,
I afterwards heard, did my mates to- see me
* fired ’) and was admitted. The father received
me. Taken on the hop, I told him pretty well the
truth. He laughed and spoke kindly to me,
asked my name, and said he was glad to see the
son of the man who gave him his first rise in the
world —he would see if he could not do something
for me. And he did. It was the best joke ever
played on a man. It got me, in the long run,
both the daughter and the business.”

So far as the country districts of N.S. W.
are concerned, the administration of the rieiv
Health Act is chiefly a farce (writes a Bulletin
Correspondent). White Cliffs Opal-field, for in-
stance, being miles away from anywhere, and
having only 500 or so people, has no doctor;
and, though the township has been the scene of
One of the most Severe epidemics known in the
province for years, the Board of Health in Sydney
incidentally heard of only 12 cases, and, ori
the strength of that, reported that the epidemic
was riot serious. In other country towns, scores
Of cases are hot reported. Comparatively little
harm would be done if the Board, on the strength
Of the imperfect, returns, did hot advisfe the Govt,
as though it had full infOrhiation, thus preventing
the making of enquiries and the granting of assist-
ance in cases where there is urgent necessity for
both.
Of Vladivostock, Mrs. Emily Patton, a
Melb. lady, who lately spent some time there,
says:—
The first impression received is that of Dirt and
Dirt, in one form or another, you never get away from.
Everything is as unclean as it is possible to be, and my
first glimpse of the markets was enough to make me
religiously abstain from meat and fish during the whole
of my stay. The sidewalks are merely loose planks
laid upon the otherwise impassable mud ; the house-
sewage is removed daily through the streets in kero-
sene-tins, slung round the necks of Chinamen, and this
city of 100,000 inhabitants remains still unlighted, un-
drained, and dependent for its w r ater-supply upon what
is conveyed from wells, in kerosene-tins, about the
identity of which w’ith those used for the other purpose
the visitor w'as never able to free herself of a horrible
suspicion.

Tired, Pale, Sleepless, and
Miserable.
WHAT A LADY SAYS ABOUT ANAEMIA’S
RAVAGES.
“ After niy last journey,” she says, “I began
to feel very low and depressed in spirits. I could
scarcely eat anything, and I became very thin,
whilst my face was as yellow as if I had had a
severe attack of jaundice. An old friend, who
met me in the street looked me straight in the
face and passed me by without recognition, and
she was positively shocked when I ran after her
and told her who I really was. I got from bad
to worse ; the least exertion worried me, and my
ordinary housework became too much for me.
My gums grew quite white, and my lips looked
a blueish grey color. My sleep was restless and
disturbed, and I was so tired in the mornings
that I could hardly get out of bed, and when I
did I used to lay on the sofa, feeling I hardly
cared what happened to me. I saw a doctor,
who said I needed a strong tonic, and I had
several bottles of physic from him, besides other
bottles of tonics which I obtained from chemists;
but I grew no better. I was in this miserable
and hopeless condition when I heard from some-
one who had been cured by them about Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills, and lat once got some. After
taking a few, I was agreeably surprised to find a
real and distinct change for the better. I got rid
of that miserable depression of spirits, which in
itself was enough to keep me ill, and, in a few
days, I was able to sleep the ,night through with-
out interruption. The Pills, too, gave me a good
appetite, and I began slowly to get cheerful and
fat again. My color came back, and, after taking
six boxes, I was completely cured, and found my-
self as strong as ever. I shall always remember
my cure with gratitude, and shall, you may be
sure, recommend this wonderful medicine to any
of my friends who may have the bad fortune to
suffer as I did.”
Mrs. Johns is now most bright and vivacious,
whilst the pretty pink glow of perfect health is
noticeable upon her cheeks—a marvellous change
for the better when compared with her former ap-
pearance.
A * Well-known Broker on
’Change.
HOW BUSINESS WORRIES PROSTRATED
AND UNNERVED HIM.
Mr. A. Sayers, of 57 Adam-street, South Yarra,
is, perhaps, one of the best-known mining men of
Victoria. It would be a difficult thing, in fact, to
find any mining centre where this gentleman has
not, with more or less success, assisted in the de-
velopment of Victoria’s golden treasures. At Bal-
larat, Sandhurst, Maldon, etc., his name and
person are familiar. While awaiting fresh oppor-
tunities, and, at the same time, recuperating—
there is nothing so wearing to the system as a life
on ’Change- -he has been so successful in the latter
respect that a press representative, visiting
him on business, could not help remarking it.
“ I feel better now than I have for many years
East. The last two months my whole system has
een re-tuned —Wound up to concert-pitch, as it
were,” was the reply. “ You suffered severely ? ”
“Yes, but not so much physically as from an
entire absence of tone in the system. I could
transact my business fairly well, but the continual
excitement of over three decades of mining life—
prospecting, promoting, speculating, and Ex-

An Eminent Australian Actor.
POSITIVELY CURED OF NERVOUS
PROSTRATION.
There is no man with whom the theatrical and
theatre-going people of Australasia are better
acquainted than Mr. Dan Barry, the popular
actor-manager of the Alexandra Theatre, Mel-
bourne. Recently a press representative paid
a visit to him at his office, and he courteously
granted a request for a few minutes’ conversation.
“My life has been a varied one,” said Mr.
Barry, “ and there are but few cities and towns
in Australia I have not visited. A theatrical
manager has much to do mentally, and as a result
I commenced to feel the strain some two years
ago. Frequently I felt, mentally and physically,
quite exhausted. I was also affected with neu-
ralgia in my head, which often seriously inter-
fered with my duties. At night my sleep was
restless and of no recuperative benefit, and my
illness commenced to tell on me so seriously that
I consulted a medical man. He told me that my

times I was in such pain that I could hardly sleep,
and often I would wake up in the middle of the
night with a start and not get any more sleep
till the morning, and when I did get out of bed
I would feel depressed in spirits and quite over-
come by a feeling of lassitude and weariness. I
had violent headaches, and a most awkward
giddiness when I got up suddenly from a sitting
posture; I also suffered from a continual bad
breath and an unpleasant taste in the mouth. I

me now, but was much thinner, with a
pale, unhealthy complexion. During all these
long years, in addition to the attention of
