Years of Tireless Indigestion.
HOW A WOMAN AT LAST FOUND PER-
FECT RELIEF.
Whether Mrs. Boyes, now residing at lf>2
Dowling-street, Sydney, merited the 15 years’
torture she has just completed, we will leave an
unbiased public to say. Certain it is that but for
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People her
sufferings would have been even more prolonged.
“For 15 years I suffered from indigestion,”
said Mrs. Boyes to a reporter from the Leichhardt
Standard, and continued she, “a long, weary time
to be ill, and I might have been suffering still if I
had not the good fortune to drop on a paragraph
good. At length I tried Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. The effect was astonishing : after taking
them for only a short time the attack I was then
suffering from was completely got rid of. I had
a sound, healthy appetite, which I hadn’t had
for years, and my sleep was no longer disturbed.
I took altogether about 12 boxes, and the Pills
completely cured me of both my indigestion and
biliousness, and I have never had the least return
of these complaints, or, in fact, of any illness
since.”
Mrs. Boyes is at present quite a picture of
health and strength.
How a Lady became Blushing
and Beautiful.
ANAEMIA ONCE MORE COMPLETELY
VANQUISHED.
Since the days of Eve the acquisition of perfect
health and beauty ot form has been the desidera-
tum of every worn in. Ninon del’Enalos, the
celebrated French beauty, attributed her health
and beauty of form to the care which she bestowed
upon her toilette. In these latter days Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People have come to be
regarded as an indispensable adjunct to a lady’s
dressing table, and to those suffering from the
many ailments peculiar to her sex. In support of
the statement we append the case of Miss May
Dickenson, who was miraculously cured of a very
obstinate case of anaemia.
Miss May Dickenson was born at Soldier’s Hill,
Ballarat, Victoria, some 18 years ago, and lived
there up to three years since, when her family re-
moved to Tasmania, her father having purchased
a selection on the “Seven.” Shortly after her
arrival on the tight little island, Miss Dickenso 11
commenced to experience very uncertain health*
which, as time went on, showed no improvement,
and caused her parents considerable anxiety.
Medical advice was sought and various tonics
advised and taken, but without any noticeable
result. In the early part of the year, change of
air was advised and Miss Dickenson came back to
Victoria, with a hope that her native air would
succeed when “ doctor’s medicine ” had failed.
Having some relations in Essex-street, Ballarat
East, Miss Dickenson took up her residence with
them. Still she continued unwell, and again
medical advice had to be obtained. Unable to
take solid nourishment, she lost flesh to such an
extent as to make it a labor to undertake any
exertion. Nothing is more weakening than
anaemia, or poorness of
blood. Energy departs
and leaves listlessness to
fill its place, the blood
goes from one’s lips and
tongue, and a terrible
pallor, ghastly to behold,
is the result.
“Could you not seek
any nourishment?”
asked the representative,
■who met Miss Dicken-
son at her aunt’s, Newry-
street, North Fitzroy.
“ Well, a little, but
most of it refused to
stay. I had pains in
my back, and had to
sleep on my side.”
“ Did you see any
medical men in Mel-
bourne ? ”
“ Yes, one, who said I
was overgrowing my
strength, and gave me a
tonic ; but in spite of all
advice and medicine, nothing would stay on my
stomach. One day, I was in a chemist’s, in
Brunswick-street, getting a prescription made up,
and the dispenser asked me if I had suffered much.
On my replying in the affirmative, he said, ‘ Why
don’t you try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills ? ’ Some-
how, that took a hold on me, and I purchased a
box, and commenced to take them instead of my
physic.”
“ Did you receive benefit ? ”
“ Yes ; shortly after I commenced to take them
I experienced a vast improvement, and, within a
few days, I ate my first substantial meal for 18
months. In a week I gained 61b. in weight, and
I kept on improving until three weeks since, when
I felt justified in discontinuing them, having taken
seven boxes.”
“ And you consider yourself fully cured?”
“ Undoubtedly ; and I can imagine the surprise
my friends wili receive when I return to my
parents next month.”
Pale and Sallow Girls.
SERIOUS WARNING TO MOTHERS.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth.
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, sallow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeaks disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched,
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents and guardians rests a great re-
sponsibility at the time their daughters are bud-
ding into womanhood. If your daughter is pale,
complains of weakness, is “tired out” upon the
slightest exertion; if she is troubled with head-
ache, backache, pain in the side, if her temper is
fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
for the most dreaded of all diseases —Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly ; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pills upon the approach of the period of puberty,
and thus avoid all chances of disease or early de-
cay.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are
not a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly
scientific preparation, the result of years of care-
ful study on the part of an eminent Edinburgh
University physician, and they were successfully
used by him in his everyday practice for years
before being offered for general sale. They are
a perfect blood builder and a nerve restorer,
curing such diseases as rheumatism, neuralgia,
partial paralysis, locomotor ataxia, St. Vitus’
dance, nervous headache, general debility, ner-
vous prostration, the after effects of la grippe,
influenza, and severe colds, diseases depending
on humours in the blood, such as scrofula,
chronic erysipelas, etc. Pink Pills give a healthy
glow to pale and sallow complexions, and are a
specific for all troubles peculiar to the female sys-
tem, such as anaemia, poor and watery blood,
female irregularities, etc. In the case of men they
effect a radical cure in all cases arising from men-
tal worry, over-work, or excesses of any nature.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are
sold only in boxes bearing the firm’s trade-mark
and wrapper (printed in red ink). Experiencing
any difficulty in obtaining the genuine Dr. Wil-
liams’, send direct to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Company, Sydney, who will forward (post paid,
on receipt of stamps or post order), one box for
2s. !)d., or half-a-dozen for 15s. 3d.

The Bullocky’s Love-Episode.
(As told by himself while driving the team.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sez to her —“Gee, Boxer, gee”—behind the
shed last night,
“ Old girl,” I says—“ Come up, there, Star ! I’ll
make you pull all right!
“ I’ve saved a quid or two,” I says, “ and if you’ll
only say ”
“ Gee, Boxer ! Star ! Come hither, Spot! Gee,
Boxer ! Come ’ee way ! ” —-
“We might get ” —“ BRANDY ! Bless my soul,
• such bullocks drive me mad !
I’ll take the livin’ hide off you in strips, I will,
my lad ! ”
“So, whatd’yer think, old girl,” I says, “just
tell me fair and straight,
I’m tongue-tied like and ” —“Holy Frost! they’ve
caught the bloomin’ gate !
You cross-eyed, jumped-up, wobblin’ swine!
What’s set you all astray !
It’s no good, boss; they can’t go back —though
there’ll be Hell to pay !
They can’t back fifteen ton of load— the post must
come away !
Stand back in case the rails should fly—stand back
there up the bank.
I’ll rouse ’em with the bleedin’ butt along the
bleedin’ flank.

of blankers ! Blank !

she says, says she,

A. F. YORK.
A Bible in use in one of the Melb. courts
bears this inscription : —“ The Master in Equity.
Melb., Sept., 1853.” This book has been in use
throughout the whole of those 45 years, and is now
a noxious ruin, with a nest of disease germs on
every text.
Committee of local residents arranging for
“Pleasant Sunday Afternoon ” meeting at Lost
Dog Swamp, 17 miles by foot from Melbourne.
Chairman: We have settled matters all right so far,
but how p.re we going to get a good speaker from Mel-
bourne to coine out and give an address?
_ Committeeman (confidently ): That’s all- right. If we
invite him, Mr. Reay, the editor of the Herald, will come
and give us a lecture on his “ Jubilee Impressions.”
Chairman : But there’s half a mile of flood-water around
us!
Committeeman : He’ll swim.
Chairman : And then there’s another half-mile or more
of liquid }nud !
Committeeman : He’ll wade.
Chairman : But why should he take all that trouble ?
Committeeman : He has to. Doctor’s orders. If he
don’t talk Jubilee at least once a month to a public audi-
ence he’ll
Chairman {anxiously): What ?
Committeeman : Burst!
Where Ah Loh Worked.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Blaekham turned over and swore at the
“ b °y.” . , .
He’d had a late night of it, playing poker and
drinking pyt; besides, it was raining, and he hated
water.
“ Five o’clock, past, and Mandore Saleh wants
to know if he shall turn out the Chinamen, or will
you give them a half-day off ? The rain is heavy,
and they don’t want to turn out.”
Blackham’s inclination was to let them have the
half-day and to take it himself, too; but the last
part of the message angered him, and, springing
out of bed, he commenced to dress. “ Tell Saleh
-if the d d Chinamen are not in the fields in ten
minutes I’ll fix’em with a rotan.”
Ten minutes later, armed only with a ray’s tail,
Blaekham ascended the ladder leading up to the
bangsal occupied by his gang.
The muttering ceased when his figure appeared
in the narrow doorway, but the dark, forbidding
looks of the half-naked Chinamen boded ill. Some
few made a pretence of getting up, but the
majority sat on in sullen silence, a few merely
uncoiling their tails as a mark of respect to the
white blackguard who lorded it over them. Black-
ham said nothing. He laid about with his cruel
weapon and his diabolical temper broke loose. In
English, in Dutch, in Chinese, in Malay, he
cursed them and their ancestors till the bangsal
was empty, and 40 miserable coolies stood shiver-
ing and cowering under the eaves.
“ I don’t care if no one else on the river works
again this year; my gang is not going to make a
Hari Basaar for a few drops of rain. Lu pergi
kreja dau lekas juga, babi, chelaka and here
Blaekham swore himself breathless again.
Slowly the men gathered up their implements,
and started down the road towards their division.
Behind them trudged Blaekham.
The rearmost Chinaman stumbled, and, in his
effort to regain himself, splashed some yellow mud
into the angry Blackham’s face.
“ Ampun, Tuan ! ” cried Ah Loh, as he sank on
his knees in a puddle.
“ I’ll ampun you, babi! Take that —and that—
and that! ’’—each “ that ” emphasised with a cut
from the ray’s tail; but Ah Loh was guilty and did
not resent punishment.
Slowly the procession went on. Ah Loh looked
down at his mud-covered feet. A fat black leech
was on one. Left there, an ulcer might result ;
then sickness, relegation to the “ kongsicans ”
gang on day wages, and so good-bye to his chance
of a good crop. He would risk it. One cut more
or less would not matter.
Blaekham had been a famous place-kick at
Winchester. Ah Loh rose some distance in the
air and landed on a stump. Pulling himself
together, he wiped the blood from his face and
stood still. ....
“ Man minta lagi ? ” asked Blaekham, with his
cruel face all smiles.
Ah Loh stood looking at him for a moment.
Suddenly a gleam shot across his hitherto impas-
sive face. “Tah, tah, tah!” he shouted, and,
whirling his parang-benkok over his head, he
rushed at Blaekham. It was all over in a moment;
Blaekham lay face downwards in the path,
and Ah Loh and the gang went back to their
bangsal.
» * * *
The Devil looks after his own. Four
months later Blaekham was well enough to appear
against Ah Loh and others, charged with at-
tempted murder.
There was no attempt at a defence. One and
all pleaded guilty, and in ten minutes sentences
totalling a century had been imposed, and the court
was about to adjourn for a drink when Blaekham
spoke up:—
“ Your Worship, I understand you have power
to order sentence to be carried out wherever your
Worship sees fit. Should these men be detained
in prison here, the estate will suffep over <£looo
loss, and I therefore make application for an order
that these men be handed over to me, so that they
may work out their sentences on the estate. As
they are bad characters, I ask that they be leg-
ironed, which will have a good and salutary effect
on the other Chinamen at Tanjong Merah.”
Order made accordingly.
* * * *
Months went by. Things had prospered'
exceedingly with Tanjong Merah. Nine-picul
crop. Good price, 3.20 guilders ; good health and
fine prospects.
Business took me up river, and the launch
landed me at Tanjong Merah. I went home with
Blaekham, and after dinner the manager, the
doctor, and another assistant came round, and we
made a night of it. Blaekham was in great spirits;
he had won a hundred dollars at poker.
I asked about Ah Loh, and Blaekham said he
would enlighten me on the morrow.
I turned out early and saw the men commence
work.
“He doesn’t appear to be the hard-hearted
ruffian they say he is,” I thought, as I watched
Blaekham moving about amongst the hands, and
giving his orders quietly.
“You don’t have Ah Loh or any of that Kongsi
working for you ? I suppose you’re a bit funky
yet ? ” I said when he rejoined me.
“It’s not exactly that. I had ’em for a while ;
in fact, Ah Loh was working for me last week.
I have only just turned him over to a stronger
man. I never could get enough out of any of the
lot; perhaps the new boss will.”
“ Where are they working ? ”
“Oh, if you like I’ll show you something of
them ; there’s just time before breakfast.”
We wandered on. Off the road and into the
jungle, and presently we stumbled across a lot of
mounds. Some quite fresh, others a few months
older.
“ What are these ? ”
“Graves—Ah Loh’s, and the rest of the Kongsi.”
“ What! Are they all dead ? ”
“ Yes, the lot are now working for Shaitan.”
“ What was it; bad water ? ”
“ Partly ; they didn’t get much. Bad food, too
—what there was of it! ”
“ Good God ! man ; what do you mean ? ”
“ Well, let us say the beggars died of too much
salt fish!” —and Blaekham playfully tapped his
shoe with the ray’s-tail that had originally started
the mischief.
TANG ANYUT.
The bicycle is destined to play its part in
the sex problem in more ways than one. Just
lately a complaint was made to an Australian
police commissioner that one of his men had,
twelve months ago, loved a country lass, and
laughed and ridden away. “He came to our
township on a bicycle. I only saw him once, and
not since till the other day, when I recognised
him doing duty in .” Now she wants him to
maintain baby !
A Woman’s Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Flanagan"; «ne chainman, had been entertaining
with anecdote and reminiscence a small and select
party at the fire before his tent. A momentary
lull in the conversation gavo a solitary morepork
in the valley temporary monopoly *f the ear of
Night, and the camp was very quiet, when sud-
denly, with startling emphasis, a man’s voice broke
the stillness with loud announcement of intention
to “ smash the face ” of somebody.
“ And he will too,” said Flanagan sententiously.
“ He’ll break it, for a dead cert. It’s near measur-
ing-up day ! ”
Next moment broke out a shrill uproar of
mingled curses and appeals, and a woman, pant-
ing and sobbing, rushed past the little group and
out of the camp.
This episode didn’t appear at all to disturb the
group around the fire, till presently one spoke.
He was a comparatively new arrival in camp.
“ Say, Flanagan, why should old Brennan beat
the woman because it’s measuring-up day ? ”
Flanagan sucked at his pipe without replying,
till the questioner began to feel hurt. “Well,
young ’un,” he said then, “ this is why : ’Cause
he’s got sense, and’s been places, and seen things,
and knows what he’s about! It’s this way: If
you’ve got as much sense as a bandicoot, you must
know there’s barely wages in that bit of a cuttin
of his, at the price he’s ‘ sub’d ’ it at, and that,
with his way o’ livin’, he can’t earn as much in a
day, himself, as the worst of his six men.”
The other nodded affirmatively. “So I should
think ! ”
“So /know. Also, that now his woman will
spend the night in the bush, and, in the morning,
she’ll be ashamed, and afraid to go home. Ac-
cordin’ly, when Brennan gets down in the cuttin’,.
first thing, he’ll find her there, stripped to the
buff, earning her pardon. And she’ll slog like
blazes till measurin’-up day, and Brennan ’ll have
a good pay and a big drunk. See ? ”
“ I see ; but the woman—what does she get out
of the thing ? ”
“She? Oh, she gets a chance to let her face
heal! ”

Chinaman Lee’s Receipt.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Attorney Magee, of Millagadee,
He had for a client one Timothy Lee,
A Chinaman he;
But christened, you see—
A Sunday-school scholar of Millagadee,
Where charming young ladies
Preach Heaven and Hades
To simpering Chinkies of humble degree !
Attorney Magee collected a fee
For services rendered to Chinaman Lee.
Said Timothy Lee :
“ My missee Magee
What for you no give it leceiptee for me ?
No Chinaman cheatee,
My want it leceiptee,
Much better you give it,” said Chinaman Lee.
Attorney Magee objected that he
Would be hanged if he’d give a receipt for a fee
To a blanketty B
Of a heathen Chinee !
[HiSdangnage, you’ll notice, was painfully free,
* And better befitting
A Parliament sitting,
Or bibulous bullockies out for a spree !]
But Chinaman Lee propounded a plea :
“ Suppose you no give it leceiptee,” said he,
“ Me die, and, you see,
The Lord askee me;
‘You allee same Clistian in Millagadee?
You got it clean sheetee ?
Nobody you elieatee ?
You got it leceiptee flom Missee Magee ? ’ ”
Said Chinaman Lee to Attorney Magee :
“Me say you no give it leceiptee for fee !
The Lord He tell me
‘ Go lookee for he ;
No halo can get till leceiptee me see ! ’
No likee Him tellee
Look all over hellee
With devils and lawyers for Missee Magee ! ”
Orange.
W. T. GOODGE.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent:—
Lord Salisbury’s determination to disintegrate the Lon-
don County Council, and split the municipal control of
the metropolis into a dozen feeble vestries, is worthy of
his consistency, and will please every large ground-
owner. It seems but the other day that the Conservative
leader attempted—on the opening of Charing Cross Road
from Oxford-street to Trafalgar Square through his
ground—to pass a Bill that would have netted him close
on a million and robbed the ratepayers of their right,
through the municipal administrators, to the building-
frontages along the new road. But the Commons
laughed at Salisbury, and he had to yield the frontages in
accordance with the law like everyone else. To-day, in
the Council area, the ground is valued at £16,000,000, on
which not a penny of rates is levied, and most of the earth
round Leicester Square, and along the Embankment, Hotel
Cecil-wards, belongs to this ponderous political bassoonist.
He fears a rate on ground-rents, hence his hatred of the
L.C.C., which threatens to be more “progressive” than
ever after the coming elections.
On Our Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nearly every Sunday saw Andrews at our
place for dinner, and if ever we did happen to
have cabbage or anything extra, Dad, who was
full of hospitality, always put a good half on An-
drews’ plate. Then Joe and I scraped the dish,
despising Andrews. It bothered us to find out
what he catne to our place at all for, and Joe
asked Norah if she knew ; but she didn’t.
Andrews was ploughing for Murphy. Murphy
was giving him a pound a week—at least, he didn’t
exactly give him a pound a week ; he gave him that
much twice a year, and owe.d him the rest. An-
drews got tired, and asked Murphy for his wages—
.£7B. Murphy said he couldn’t well pay him just
then, but offered to raise his wages to 255. a week.
Andrews declined. Then they slang-whanged
each other until Murphy silenced Andrews by
knocking him down with a rake. After that he
kicked Andrews off his ground, and went straight
to town and got him bound over to keep the peace.
He was a tough contract, was Murphy.
Andrews came to our place to get Dad’s advice
about summoning Murphy for wages, and regard-
ing prospects generally. Dad reckoned he had
better let Murphy alone, but told him he could
stay with us for a day or two until he got time to
look about. He stayed, and Joe took down the
dictionary and scratched out “cabbage.” An-
drews made himself at home for a week—a month
—three months ! Dad began to reflect. Some-
times he and mother held short conferences, from
which the rest of us were carefully excluded ; but
nothing came of them.
Six months saw Andrews more at home at our
place than ever! He was a very kind man, and
most obliging. If a traveller called for a drink of
water, Andrews would give him a cup of milk and
ask him to wait and have dinner. If Maloney, or
old Anderson, or anybody except Murphy, wished
to borrow a horse, or a dray, or anything about the
place, Andrews would let them have it with
pleasure and tell them not to be in a hurry about
returning it.
We couldn’t make him out at all, and we kept
on wondering more and more why he didn't go
away—till we got up one morning and found that
he had gone at last—and taken Norah with him !
The two best horses for riding were gone too.
Dad fell to the occasion in an unparalleled burst
of blasphemy. But that was the last, for two
years, we heard of Andrews.
* • * *
Perhaps it was Andrews’ perfidy that
made Dad more than usually disgusted with the
selection. Anyway, after making £7 10s. out of
100 bags of corn, he decided to leave the cultiva-
tion-paddock to the weeds, and he and Dave
started out after kangaroos—scalp-hunting. They
camped in an old hut on the back ridges about
eight miles away ; and every Saturday night Dad
came home for bread—they killed their own meat
out there. Joe was left in charge of the selection.
* * * *
Singularly enough, Dad complained that
kangaroos were getting scarce where he was
camped ; while our paddocks were full of them.
Joe started a mob nearly every day, as he walked
round overseeing things; and he pondered.
Suddenly he had an original inspiration—urgin'
ality was Joe’s, strong point. He turned the barn
into a workshop, and buried himself there for two
days. For two whole,days he was iiwer ‘‘.at
home,” except when he stepped out to threw ‘ '
hammer at the dog for yelping for a drink. The
grexly brute ! it wasn’t a week since he’d had,a
billyful—Joe told him. On the morning of the
third day the barn-door swung open, and forth
came a kangaroo, with the sharpened carving-
knife in its paws. It hopped across the yard and
sat up, bold and erect, near the dog-kennel. Bluey
nearly broke his neck trying to get at it. The
kangaroo said: “ Lay down, you useless hound ! ”
and started across the cultivation, heading for the
grass-paddock in long, erratic jumps. Half-way
across the cultivation it spotted a mob of other
kangaroos, and took a firmer grip of the carver.
Bluey howled and plunged until mother came
out to see what was the matter. She was in time
to see a solitary kangaroo hop in a drunken man-
ner towards the fence, so she let the dog go and
cried, “Sool him, Bluey! Sool him!” Bluey
sooled him, and mother followed with the axe to
get the scalp. As the dog came racing up, the
kangaroo turned and hissed, “G’ home, y’ mong-
rel! ” Bluey took no notice, and only when he
had nailed the kangaroo dexterously by the thigh
and got him down did it dawn upon the marsupial
that Bluey wasn’t in the secret. Joe tore off his
head-gear, called the dog affectionately by name
and yelled for help ; but Bluey had not had any-
thing substantial to eat for over a week, and he
worried away vigorously.
Then the kangaroo slashed out with the carving-
knife, and hacked a junk (Iff Bluey’s nose. Bluey
shook his head, relaxed his thigh-grip, and grabbed
the kangaroo by the ribs. How that kangaroo did
squeal! Mother arrived. She dropped the axe,
threw up both hands, and shrieked. “Pull him
off! he’s eating me! ” gasped the kangaroo.
Mother shrieked louder, and wrung her hands ;
but it had no effect on Bluey. He was a good
dog, was Bluey !
At last, mother got him by the tail and dragged
him off, but he took a mouthful of kangaroo with
him as he went. Then the kangaroo raised
itself slowly on to its hands and knees. It was
very white and sick-looking, and mother threw
her arms round it and cried, “Oh, Joe! My
child ! my child ! ”
It was several days before Joe felt better.
When he did, Bluey and he went down the gully
together, and, after a while, Joe came back—like
Butler—alone !
STEELE RUDD.
A J.P. named Norton, out Gundagai
(N.S.W.) way, possesses what he alleges to be the
largest topaz ever found. His son happened on it
in the Murrumbidgee ranges. The stone passed
through the hands of Rev. W. B. Clarke, geolo-
gist, who, according to the old man, extolled it as
the largest found anywhere. It has been lent
several times to English and. Continental exhi-
bitions.
A Big Scrub Interlude.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
School had just been d - missed for the day. I
was sitting in my Chamber of Horrors, staring
moodily at a big pile of exercise and dictation
books, and wondering if it would ever be possible
to invent my long-dreamt-of “ Combination
Patent Reliance Defiance Exercise Corrector and
Accurate Compiler of Official Returns,” when J
perceived a strong smell of strong rum mingled
with the characteristic odor of a; fat, hot person
who did not wash regularly. Presently Old
Granny Meyles shambled into the little class- ,j
room, looking flushed and flabby. I detested the
sight and the smell of the old crone : but I said —
“ Good afternoon, Madame,” for I always tried
to be as courteous to women as the road super.,
our guide in social, if not in moral, matters.
“ Good day, Mr. Watkin. I jes’ come to see if
y’ll read th’ burial suvvjs over poor Billy Marsh.”
“When did old Bill die? This is the first I
have heard of his death.”
“He died yistidee. I never—hie —heared of it
meself till about a ’our ago. I could ’ardly ’ave
beleft it, for he looked so well when I see
’im last, poor bowjer. W’en I heared th’ sad noos,
I went hell-for-leather to e’s ’umpy. It was a
good job I went. He was laid out somethink
blanky cruel.”
I rose and opened the window wide, for a breath
of fresh air. The old hag continued—
“ Whw tied e’s jor rup ?” I sez.
“ Wlff, I did,” sez Mother Lomax.
“ Why didn’t you straighten them legs ? ” I sez.
“ Coz I diden know ’ow,” sez she. (Snort.)
“ And would ya bury a decent corp with e’s legs
all tucked up ? ” I sez.
“ What kin ya do to urn ? ” sez she.
, “ Why, set on ’em, ya fool,” sez I.
“Oh ! oh ! my Gawd, Granny Meyles, ya don’t
ketch me a settin’ on um,” says she.
“ Git me some ’ot water, an’ a board, an’ th’flat
irons, an’th’old hanvil,” I sez, “an’even then I
coulden git ’em quite straight; but if old Bill does
go ta ’ell ’e’ll go with a better pair a legs under ’im
nor if that higgerant old fool ’ad ’ad ’er way.”
Granny shuffled out. At the door she stopped.
“ Ya’ll oblige, Mr. Watkin, won’t ya ? Poor Bill
always spoke well a you, though ya diden drink at
all. Th’ funeral’s at 6 o’clock.”
“ I’ll read the prayers for the dead, Madame,” I
said.
* * * •
And all through that bush burial I could not,
try as I would, help wondering if Granny Meyles’s
knees would require straightening when she
pegged out.
